FAMILY  Phanerochaetaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ceriporiopsis pseudoplacenta  Vlasak & Ryvarden^  Mycotaxon 119: 222. 2012
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include flat growth on conifer wood, pale apricot to salmon pink pore surface, drying dark reddish brown, pores that are small, and circular to angular, the margin whitish at first, and a hyphal system monomitic without cystidia.^ RANGE Collections were examined from WA, CA, and TN, and it is "probably widely distributed in the western USA", (Vlasak)
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  growing flat on wood with pores facing outward (i.e. resupinate), up to 20cm x 10cm, "not readily separable"
FLESH  "soft when fresh, tough and fleshy when dry, and soaked with resinous substance"; context less than 0.1cm thick, "pale orange-pinkish when fresh, blackish when dry" 
PORES  3-4 per millimeter, circular to angular, "pale apricot to salmon-pink when fresh, exuding white drops, turning up dark reddish brown on drying"; "entire dissepiments that finally become thick and lacerate"; tube layer up to 0.5cm thick, pale salmon-pink; margin "narrowly sterile" but sometimes up to 1.5cm wide in actively growing stage, "whitish at first, later pale apricot, on drying dark reddish brown to black and often peeling off"
STEM  
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on rather rotten logs of various conifers, associated with a white rot (although the wood under fruitbodies is "usually quite dark as the fungus causes some red-brownish wood coloration")
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 3.5-4.5(5) x 2.2-3 microns, broadly elliptic, "distinctly narrowing to the apex in most cases", smooth, inamyloid, colorless, thin-walled^; basidia 4-spored, narrowly clavate, inconspicuous, not much wider than hyphal ends, thin-walled, with basal clamp connection; cystidia none; hyphal system monomitic, hyphae 2-4 microns wide, colorless, "thin-walled, often branched", with abundant, often closely spaced clamp connections, in Melzer's reagent "the hyphae are covered by small oil drops that often fuse into large, brownish, oily blotches", "in KOH the hyphae are stripped of the oily matter that forms irregular, brown aggregates in the tissue"
NAME ORIGIN  means "false placenta", based on superficial similarity with Postia placenta
SIMILAR  Postia placenta has similar colors and soft, fleshy consistency, but 1) it begins growing usually as a deep-red fruitbody, changing to rosy pink and, when old or dry, to whitish pink or light ocher, while C. pseudoplacenta follows the opposite coloration steps, starting "as whitish or light salmon pink, becoming increasingly more deeply red until maturing to red brownish or black in the subiculum and margin", 2) the consistency of the dried fruitbody is soft and brittle in Postia placenta, but "tough and rubber-like (reminiscent of beef jerky)" in C. pseudoplacenta, 3) it is associated with a dry brown rot, 4) microscopically it has larger spores [longer rather than wider], (Vlasak). Auriporia aurea has similar colors and soft, fleshy consistency, but 1) is associated with a dry, brown rot, 2) has thick-walled cystidia and skeletal hyphae, (Vlasak).
SOURCES  Vlasak(1)

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Cerrena unicolor  (Bull.: Fr.) Murrill^  J. Mycol. 9: 91. 1903;  Daedalea unicolor  Bull.: Fr.;  Trametes unicolor  (Bull.: Fr.) Cooke;  Daedalea cinerea  Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  mossy maze polypore, gray polypore
NOTES  {See also Trametes versicolor and similar polypores Table.} Cerrena unicolor is easy to recognize because of the hirsute (coarsely hairy) cap, the black line in the flesh, and the labyrinthine pore surface.^ This species was identified as the fungal symbiont of the wood wasp Tremex columba on Fagus grandifolia in eastern Canada. RANGE Cerrena unicolor has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NS, NWT, ON, PQ, AK, AR, CO, CT, DE, IA, IL, IN, KS, KY, LA, MA, ME, MI, MN, MO, MT, NC, ND, NE, NH, NJ, NY, OH, PA, SD, TN, TX, VA, VT, WI, WV, and WY, and it is widespread in Asia and Europe, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  growing on wood, semicircular cap with no stem, or with part of the fruitbody flat (with pore surface exposed) and part of it shelving into a cap, or rarely flat with pore surface exposed, up to 10cm wide, often in shingled clusters, annual; upper surface pale brownish to gray, often green due to algae; hirsute (with coarse elongated hairs) to almost bald, grooved^, (Gilbertson), 0.5-7.5cm across, "starts as a spreading resupinate, then forms crowded often overlapping brackets; white to gray; thin; with dense covering of stiff hairs, often zoned and with covering of algae on upper surface", (Phillips)
FLESH  up to 0.3cm thick; duplex, with corky pale brown layer separated from soft spongy darker upper layer by a thin dark zone^, (Gilbertson), thin, tough; white, (Phillips)
PORES  3-4 per mm, maze-like, variable, in parts larger; ivory to pale buff when young, becoming darker when old; walls "at first thick and tomentose, becoming thin and splitting"; tube layer up to 1cm thick, continuous with lower flesh^, (Gilbertson), 2-3 per mm, white to gray, tube layer 0.04-0.4cm thick, maze-like, becoming tooth-like or rarely pore-like, whitish, (Phillips)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  no (Phillips)
HABITAT  annual, on dead wood of many genera of hardwoods, rarely on conifers, causing white rot of dead hardwoods^, (Gilbertson), on living hardwoods as wound parasite, or dead trunks, as well as stumps and hardwood on ground, (Breitenbach), fall (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Phillips)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5-7 x 2.5-4 microns, cylindric-elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 20-25 x 5-6 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia 40-60 x 4-5 microns, hyphoid (not clearly differentiated from vegetative hyphae), thin-walled, with basal clamp; hyphal system trimitic: generative context hyphae 2-4 microns wide, thin-walled, nodose-septate, skeletal context hyphae 2.5-5 microns wide, thick-walled, aseptate, binding hyphae 2-4 microns wide, thick-walled, aseptate, much-branched, tramal hyphae similar, (Gilbertson), spores 5.5-7 x 3-3.5, elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin meaning "of one color"^, (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
SIMILAR  Daedalea quercina and Daedaleopis confragosa have fewer hairs (Lincoff); Irpex lacteus has long flattened tooth-like tubes (Lincoff); Gloeophyllum saepiarium has brown spore-bearing surface that is gill-like or with poroid or tooth-like surface or with elongated pores, and upper surface is rusty brown to dark brown or maroon brown, often with yellow or orangish zones; Lenzites betulina has spore-bearing surface that is mostly gill-like, but forked and fused together in places. See also SIMILAR section of Daedaleopsis confragosa.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)*, Breitenbach(2)*, Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Climacocystis borealis  (Fr.) Kotl. & Pouzar^  Ceska Mykol. 12: 103. 1958;  Polyporus borealis  Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  "recognized readily in the field because of the watery and sappy fruitbodies, often occurring in abundance, the whitish to pale ochraceous color and the coarsely hirsute pileus and slightly irregular pores. Microscopically, the cystidia are diagnostic." (Gilbertson(1)).^ RANGE Climacocystis borealis has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, NB, NS, ON, PE, AK, AZ, CO, CT, MA, ME, MI, MN, MT, NC, NH, NJ, NM, NY, OH, PA, TN, UT, VA, VT, WI, and WY, and it is circumglobal and boreal, (Gilbertson). It occurs in Europe, Asia, Australia, (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 15cm long, 8cm wide, and 4cm thick toward the base, fan-shaped to broadly stemless, flat and semicircular, often imbricate [shingled], upper surface flat to slightly convex, consistency "soft and watery when fresh, light and brittle when dry"; "white to light cream or straw-colored when fresh, deep straw-colored when dry"; "tomentose to hirsute when fresh, but the hairs agglutinate in drying and surface then often partly glabrous, partly tufted with short stiff hairs, pileus when dry often with radial striae"^, (Gilbertson), 0.5-20cm along wood, projecting outward 3-12cm, 1.5-3cm thick, bracket-like, spathulate, or fan-shaped, attached broadly or by a stem-like prolongation; white when young then straw-yellow; "coarsely fibrillose-scaly-hispid, radially channeled-grooved, uneven"; margin inflated when young then +/- sharp, wavy, usually yellower than cap surface, (Breitenbach) 
FLESH  up to 2cm thick, duplex with a lower dense layer; white to cream^, (Gilbertson), "fibrous, tough, succulent, with duplex structure"; white to cream, (Breitenbach)
PORES  about 1-2 per mm, thin-walled and angular, in parts more irregular and split, pore surface usually somewhat oblique; white to cream or light straw-colored; tube layer up to 0.5cm thick, colored as pore surface^, (Gilbertson), 1-3 per mm, rounded to angular, somewhat labyrinthine [maze-like] when old; white at first, layer yellowish; tube layer 0.2-0.6cm thick, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none, but may be attached by a stem-like prolongation of cap^, (Breitenbach)
ODOR  pleasant^ (Breitenbach)
TASTE  bitter (Breitenbach), mild (Phillips)
EDIBILITY  no (Phillips)
HABITAT  annual, known almost exclusively from conifers, causes "a white mottled rot in the butt and roots of living conifers, continues decay in dead trees and stumps", developing late in season^, (Gilbertson), August to November (Phillips), gregarious, sometimes adjacent fruitbodies growing together, rarely single, on dead wood of conifers, rarely on hardwood, (Breitenbach)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4.5-6.5 x 3-4.5 microns, broadly elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, thin-walled^; basidia 4-spored, 15-25 x 4-5 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia embedded in the hymenium or slightly projecting, up to 50 microns long from the clamp connections from which they arise, 5-12 microns wide, "mostly numerous, ventricose and tapering, either acute or slightly rounded, mostly smooth, but also with a few grainy crystals on the top, thin-walled in the lower part, distinctly thickened towards the top"; hyphal system monomitic: generative hyphae up to 4 microns wide in hymenium, (in context mostly wider, up to 8 microns wide), with clamp connections, thin-walled to thick-walled, moderately branched, (Gilbertson), spores 5-6 x 3.5-4 microns, oval, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, sometimes with granular contents, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'northern'
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Phillips(1)*, Breitenbach(2)*, Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Coltricia cinnamomea  (Pers.) Murrill^  Bull. Torr. Bot. Cl. 31: 343. 1904;  Polyporus cinnamomeus  Pers.;  Polyporus splendens  Peck;  Polyporus subsericeus  Peck;  Polyporus parvulus  Klotzsch
ENGLISH NAMES  fairy stool
NOTES  Features include a thin silky-shiny cinnamon to amber-brown cap that stains black with KOH, rusty brown flesh, a brown pore surface, and a reddish brown to dark brown stem.^ RANGE Coltricia cinnamomea has been found in BC, OR, ON, PQ, AL, AZ, CA, CO, CT, GA, IA, KY, LA, MA, ME, MI, MO, MS, MT, NC, NJ, NY, OH, PA, TN, VA, VT, WI, and WV, (Gilbertson). It has been reported from WA by Andrew Parker, pers. comm.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  cap surface stains black with KOH (Arora), flesh black with KOH (Lincoff)
CAP  with cap and stem, cap rarely above 3-4cm wide (in tropics up to 12cm), up to 0.5cm thick, circular, flat to funnel-shaped, margin "lobed, incised to entire, often fused with adjacent fruitbodies", sharp and mostly deflexed [downcurved] when dry; brown to deep reddish brown; finely velvety, "shiny to glossy with numerous distinct to indistinct concentric zones"^, (Gilbertson), 1-5cm, more or less circular, centrally depressed or umbilicate, margin often fringed or torn; "bright cinnamon to reddish-brown, yellow-brown, rusty-brown, or darker"; dry, "with shiny or silky striations and narrow or inconspicuous concentric zones", (Arora), annual (Phillips)
FLESH  up to 0.1cm thick, fibrous; rusty to reddish brown^, (Gilbertson), 0.1cm thick or less, pliant when fresh; rusty-brown, (Arora)
PORES  2-4 per mm, angular and thin-walled; reddish brown; tube layer up to 0.2cm thick, more or less colored as pore surface^, (Gilbertson), 2-3 per mm, yellow-brown to brown or reddish-brown; tube layer 0.05-0.3cm thick, typically not decurrent, (Arora)
STEM  up to 3-4cm long, 0.2-0.6cm wide, round in cross-section to flattened, mostly widening towards base; ochraceous rusty to deep reddish brown; finely velvety^, (Gilbertson), 1-5cm x 0.1-0.4cm, usually central and more or less equal, tough; brown to reddish-brown; hairy or velvety, (Arora)
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  too tough (Arora), unknown (Phillips)
HABITAT  annual, on the ground usually in deciduous forests, apparently not a wood-rotter^, (Gilbertson), "solitary or in small groups on ground or moss in woods, often along well-beaten paths, roadbanks, and in clearings (rarely on rotten wood)", (Arora)
SPORE DEPOSIT  yellowish-brown^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6-10 x 4.5-7 microns, oblong to broadly elliptic, smooth, weakly dextrinoid, golden yellow, thin-walled to distinctly thick-walled^, cyanophilous; basidia 2-spored to 4-spored, 18-30 x 5-7 microns, clavate; setae or cystidia or other sterile elements absent; hyphal system monomitic: generative hyphae 2-5 microns wide in hymenium, (in cap and stem up to 10 microns in diameter and there sometimes very thick-walled), with simple septa, first thin-walled and colorless, "later more thick-walled and golden to light rusty brown, septation frequent in hymenium and subhymenium, more scattered in the context where the hyphae are longer and straighter and not branched to the same degree as in the hymenium, branching at right or wide angles", (Gilbertson), 6-10 x 4.5-7 microns, elliptic, smooth, (Arora)
NAME ORIGIN  means "cinnamon-colored"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Coltricia perennis is typically in coniferous forests, the cap is generally 3-10cm across, the cap surface is dull, the flesh is 0.1-0.3cm thick, the stem is generally 1.0cm wide, the spores are 3.5-5.5 microns wide, and there are antler-like hyphal tips on the cap surface, whereas C. cinnamomea is generally in hardwood forests, the cap is rarely over 4cm across, the cap surface is shiny, the flesh is up to 0.1cm thick, the stem is up to 0.6cm wide, the spores are 4.5-7.0 microns wide, and antler-like hyphal tips on the cap surface are lacking, (Ginns(28)). C. perennis has larger fruitbodies, a more dull, yellowish brown to gray and often more hirsute cap, and narrower spores, (Gilbertson), C. perennis is up to 10cm, has strongly zoned velvety rather than silky surface, usually thicker stem and brown to grayish-brown pores that are sometimes decurrent, (Arora); Coltricia montagnei has maze-like pores or concentrically arranged plates, (Arora); Polyporus and other stemmed polypores in general do not have rusty brown flesh (Arora)
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Arora(1)*, Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(1), Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Coltricia montagnei  (Fr.) Murrill^  Mycologia 12: 13. 1920;  Polyporus montagnei  Fr.;  Cyclomyces greenei  Berk.;  Polystictus obesus  Ellis & Everh.;  Coltricia memmingeri  Murrill;  Polystictus cuticularis  Lloyd
ENGLISH NAMES  Montagne's polypore
NOTES  The cinnamon to rusty brown circular plates that develop from pores are characteristic. Other features include a large thick cap and stem, the cap circular to fan-shaped and usually depressed, a cinnamon to reddish rusty brown upper surface that is velvety to scaly, a similarly colored stem, and growth on the ground in hardwood forest.^ In the past it was divided into two varieties: montagnei with a pore-like spore-bearing surface and greenei with concentric plates. The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where indicated. RANGE Coltricia montagnei has been found in OR, ON, CT, GA, KY, MI, NC, OH, MA, MD, ME, NH, NJ, NY, PA, TN, VA, and WV, (Gilbertson(1)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  flesh black with KOH (Lincoff)
CAP  with cap and stem, cap up to 12cm wide and 1-2cm thick, circular to more irregular and partly fan-shaped, mostly depressed in center or toward lateral attachment of stem, soft when fresh, more brittle when dry, margin wavy and sometimes incised; upper surface cinnamon to deep reddish rusty brown, margin paler when actively growing; usually not zoned, more rarely slightly banded in somewhat grooved zones, at first velvety to tomentose, later more irregularly hispid (with stiff erect hairs) to warted or fibrous scaly, especially toward center
FLESH  up to 2cm thick at center, "upper part soft to corky, lower part distinctly denser, but no distinct duplex character", colored as pore surface
PORES  with pores or with concentric gill-like plates, the pores being 1-3mm wide, angular, thin-walled, often slightly expanded and radially elongated toward stem, but when old some pores split up and take on a partially or completely concentric plate appearance, 1-3mm between the circles of plates; cinnamon to rusty brown; tube layer up to 0.1-0.4cm thick, rarely up to 0.8cm long near stem
STEM  1-4cm x 0.5-1cm, slender to thick, mostly narrowing downward; cinnamon to deep rusty brown; tomentose to warted and in parts bald when old
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  no (too tough), (Phillips)
HABITAT  annual, "on ground in hardwood forests, rarely in coniferous ones, often on exposed soil, footpaths, clay banks, etc.", apparently not a wood rotting fungus
SPORE DEPOSIT  pale brown^ (Phillips)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 9-14 x 5.5-7.5 microns, elliptic, smooth, slightly dextrinoid, "pale golden yellow, becoming slightly thick-walled with age"^; basidia 2-spored to 4-spored, 20-35 x 6-8 microns, clavate, simple-septate at base; setae none; hyphal system monomitic: "hyphae with simple septa, thin to thick-walled, hyaline to golden or slightly rusty brown, 5-8 microns wide in the trama, wider and up to 14 microns in the context, richly branched, often at almost right angles"
NAME ORIGIN  named for J.P.F.C. Montagne^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Small specimens can look like Coltricia cinnamomea which has smaller spores, (Gilbertson). Small specimens may look like Onnia tomentosa but latter has distinct setae, (Gilbertson). Phaeolus schweinitzii also has dark brown large pored fruitbodies but has smaller spores and thin-walled tubular cystidia, (Gilbertson). See also SIMILAR section of Coltricia perennis.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(2)* (both varieties), Ginns(28)*
56
FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Coltricia perennis  (Fr.) Murrill^  J. mycol. 9: 91. 1903;  Polyporus perennis  Fr.;  Polyporus proliferus  Lloyd;  Polyporus decurrens  Lloyd
ENGLISH NAMES  tiger's eye
NOTES  Features include 1) a more or less circular cap that is pale cinnamon to deep brown, tough, thin, velvety tomentose, and zoned, 2) rusty brown flesh that stains black with KOH, 3) brown pores that are often slightly decurrent on the stem, 4) central dark brown velvety stem, and 5) growth on the ground in coniferous forests.^ It is common in the Pacific Northwest. According to Siegel(2) more than one Californian species go by the name Coltricia perennis. RANGE It is found in BC, WA, OR, ID, NS, ON, PQ, AL, AZ, CA, CO, CT, FL, GA, IA, MA, MD, ME, MI, MN, MO, MT, NC, NH, NJ, NM, NY, OH, PA, TN, VA, VT, WI, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  flesh blackens with KOH (Lincoff)
CAP  with cap and stem, up to 10cm wide, 0.2-0.5cm thick at center, cap more or less circular, often confluent with nearby specimens when growing in groups, tough and leathery when fresh, brittle and hard when dry, "margin thin and wavy, bent down in dry specimens"; pale cinnamon to deep brown becoming grayish when old; velvety tomentose, usually densely zoned, often with slightly different tomentum from zone to zone^, (Gilbertson), 2-8cm, flattened or funnel-shaped, thin; "finely velvety, then zoned and bald, rust-colored and brown", (Lincoff), funnel-shaped, cap edge may be fused with other caps; "concentric zones of grayish brown, golden to cinnamon-brown, or darker brown, and usually a pale irregular edge"; velvety, (Trudell)
FLESH  0.1-0.2cm thick, "rusty brown and dense, paler toward cap"^, (Gilbertson), thin, leathery; cinnamon-colored, (Lincoff)
PORES  2-4 per mm, angular, thin-walled, often slightly decurrent on stem, pores when old sometimes slightly incised or toothed; golden brown, cinnamon to dark brown when old; tube layer up to 0.3cm thick, cinnamon to rusty brown^, (Gilbertson), quite broad, roundish or polygonal, slightly decurrent; "initially whitish then cinnamon-brown, velvety"; tubes cinnamon colored, (Lincoff), yellowish white to brownish, bruising brown when handled, (Trudell)
STEM  not described by Gilbertson(1), but Phillips, who follows their description, gives it as 1.5-3.5cm x 0.2-1cm, central, dark brown^, (Phillips), 1-3cm x 0.5cm, cylindric; reddish-brown; velvety, (Lincoff)
ODOR  mushroomy^ (Lincoff), without special odor (Breitenbach)
TASTE  astringent (Lincoff), mild, (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  no, due to consistency, (Lincoff)
HABITAT  annual, on ground "in coniferous forests, often on exposed soil like paths, roadsides, clearings, in vicinity of fireplaces, etc."^, (Gilbertson), single to gregarious, "in hardwood and coniferous forests, on soil, among needle litter and mosses, commonly on embankments of sandy soil", (Breitenbach), fruiting in summer and fall (Miller), on the ground or on woody debris, "most characteristic of disturbed areas such as trail edges and roadsides in coniferous forests", (Trudell), fall to early winter (Bacon), all year (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  pale yellowish brown^ (Phillips)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6-9(10) x 3.5-5(5.5) microns, elliptic to cylindric-elliptic, smooth, slightly dextrinoid, pale yellowish brown^; basidia 4-spored, 15-25 x 5-7 microns, clavate, simple-septate at base; setae none; hyphal system "monomitic with two types of generative hyphae, both with simple septa, a) predominantly straight and sparingly branched, rusty brown, 4-8 microns wide, in most sections with numerous septa, b) twisted, pale yellowish and narrow wide and with few septa, these hyphae are especially common in the context and the central part of the stem, hyphae on pileus surface thick-walled, erect, with distinctive dichotomous branching, pale yellowish brown, 3-8 microns in diam.", (Gilbertson), spores 5-10 x 3.5-6 microns, elliptic, smooth, yellow to ochreous, (Lincoff)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin, "long-lasting"^, (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  Coltricia cinnamomea is generally in hardwood forests, the cap is rarely over 4cm across, the cap surface is shiny, the flesh is up to 0.1cm thick, the stem is up to 0.6cm wide, the spores are 4.5-7.0 microns wide, and antler-like hyphal tips on the cap surface are lacking, whereas C. perennis is typically in coniferous forests, the cap is generally 3-10cm across, the cap surface is dull, the flesh is 0.1-0.3cm thick, the stem is generally 1.0cm wide, the spores are 3.5-5.5 microns wide, and antler-like hyphal tips on the cap surface are present, (Ginns(28)). C. cinnamomea has smaller fruitbodies; has a velvety, shiny to glossy brown to deep reddish brown cap with distinct to indistinct concentric zones; favors hardwood forests, and has wider spores, (Gilbertson). C. cinnamomea is 1-5cm across; has a silky rather than strongly zoned velvety surface; usually has a thinner stem; and has yellow-brown to brown or reddish brown pores that are typically not decurrent, (Arora). Coltricia montagnei has maze-like pores or concentrically arranged plates, (Arora). Polyporus and other stemmed polypores in general do not have rusty brown flesh (Arora). Onnia tomentosa has nonzoned cap, usually more robust fruitbody, and microscopically has setae in the hymenium, (Breitenbach). Phellodon tomentosus is usually smaller but can look similar from the top - it has teeth rather than pores on the underside.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(1)*, Courtecuisse(1)*, Arora(1)*, Trudell(4)*, Miller(14)*, Breitenbach(2)*, Sept(1)*, Bacon(1)*, Buczacki(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Ginns(28)*, Siegel(2)* (as Coltricia perennis group)

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Coriolopsis gallica  (Fr.) Ryvarden^  Norw. J. Bot. 19: 230. 1973;  Polyporus gallicus  Fr.;  Trametes stuppeus  Berk. & M.A. Curtis;  Polyporus lindheimerii  Berk. & M.A. Curtis;  Trametes peckii  Kalchbr.
ENGLISH NAMES  brownflesh bracket
NOTES  "When typically developed this is an easy species to recognize because of its quite thick basidiocarps with a hispid to villose, often grayish pileus, large pores, and a brown pore-surface and context", (Gilbertson).^ "The combination of large-diameter pores, coarsely hairy (bristly) pileus surface, and large basidiospores separate these species [Coriolopsis gallica and C. trogii] from nearly all other polypores in British Columbia", (Ginns(28)). RANGE Coriolopsis gallica has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NS, SK, AR, AZ, CA, CO, IA, IL, IN, KS, LA, MI, MN, MO, MT, ND, NE, NH, NM, NY, OH, OK, TX, UT, WI, and WY, (Gilbertson). It also occurs in Europe, Asia, Africa, (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  flesh at first black in KOH, then fading back to almost the original color
CAP  with cap and no stem, cap "up to 10cm wide, 7cm broad, and 1cm thick, semicircular or elongated", often several imbricate [shingled] caps bent back from a common part flat on the wood (with pore surface exposed), corky to tough; at first brownish, soon dirty gray; zoned or not zoned, densely hirsute (with coarse elongated hairs) to hispid (with stiff erect hairs), more hispid at base than at margin, "the hirsute tomentum is clearly differentiated towards the brown context"^, (Gilbertson), bracket-like, shelf-like or entirely flat on wood, semicircular to linear, individual caps 10-15cm along wood, projecting 2-6(10)cm, 1-3cm thick, dark brown, rust-brown to gray-ocher-brown; "coarsely hispid-tomentose, +/- zoned, sometimes rather radially furrowed, hairs stiff and some agglutinated into tufts", cap margin sharp, somewhat lighter in color than cap surface when young, appressed-tomentose and slightly undulating, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  mostly thin, more rarely up to 1cm thick; rusty to umber brown^, (Gilbertson), 0.5-1cm thick, corky, tough; brown, (Breitenbach)
PORES  1-3 mm in diameter, angular, thin-walled, "in larger and older specimens often radially elongated and deeply split" [but in the key the pores are given as 1-3 per mm]; brown to gray; tube layer up to 1.5cm thick, whitish to gray on inner walls, trama between tube walls brown^, (Gilbertson), 1-2(3) per mm, rounded angular, sometimes also oblong; ocher-brown when young, later gray brown to dark brown; tube layer up to 1cm thick, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on dead hardwood, most common on Salix (willow) and Populus spp., very rarely on conifers^, (Gilbertson), single or in groups or rows on dead or living wood of hardwoods, (Breitenbach), fall (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^, (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 10-16 x 3-5 microns, cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, thin-walled, spores sometimes varying considerably even in the same fruitbody^; basidia 20-40 x 5.5-8 microns, clavate; cystidia absent; hyphal system trimitic: generative hyphae 2-4.5 microns wide, thin-walled, colorless and clamped, binding hyphae 2.5-4.5 microns wide, "tortuous, thick-walled to almost solid, light golden brown", skeletal hyphae 2.5-6 microns wide, "thick-walled to solid, golden brown in trama and context, hyaline in the tomentum", (Gilbertson), spores 10-15 x 4.5-5.5 microns, elliptic-cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  means "of France"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Coriolopsis trogii has white to pale cream flesh, the ochraceous buff pore surface has pores 1-2 per millimeter, and spores measure 8-12 x 2.5-4.0 microns, whereas C. gallica has yellowish brown to dark brown flesh, the grayish brown to gray pore surface has pores 0.3-1 per millimeter, and spores measure 10-16 x 3-5 microns, (Ginns(28)). C. trogii is generally paler and has a white to wood-colored pore surface and white tubes and context (Gilbertson), Coriolopsis trogii has light colored flesh without a KOH reaction (Breitenbach)
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Breitenbach(2)*, Buczacki(1)*, Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Coriolopsis trogii  (Berk.) Domanski^  Mala Flora Grzybow, I Basidiomycetes (Podstawczaki), Aphyllophorales (Bezblaszkowce). (5) Corticiaceae (Krakow) 1: 230. 1974;  Trametes trogii  Berk.;  Cerrena trogii (Berk.) Zmitr.;  Trametella trogii  (Berk.) Domanski;  Funalia trogii  (Berk. in Trog) Bondartsev & Singer
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include a bracket-like to shelf-like fruitbody with bristly whitish to ochraceous buff or brownish upper surface that is not zoned or faintly zoned, double layered flesh, angular to maze-like whitish pores with thin torn walls, and growth on dead hardwood.^ "The combination of large-diameter pores, coarsely hairy (bristly) pileus surface, and large basidiospores separate these species [Coriolopsis trogii and C. gallica] from nearly all other polypores in British Columbia", (Ginns(28)). The online Species Fungorum, accessed October 25, 2015, gave the current name as Trametes trogii Berk. RANGE Coriolopsis trogii is known in BC from one collection (J. Ginns, pers. comm.). It is also found in ID, AB, MB, NB, NS, ON, AK, AZ, CA, CO, CT, MA, MD, ME, MI, MN, NH, NM, NY, PA, VT, and WI, (Gilbertson), and Europe and Asia, (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  flesh with no KOH reaction (Breitenbach)
CAP  bracket-like, or bent outward from flat pore surface forming shelf-like cap, or rarely entirely flat on wood with pore surface exposed, caps up to 4cm wide, cream-buff to ochraceous buff or wood-colored, not zoned to faintly zoned; coarsely hispid [bristly]^, (Gilbertson), individual caps up to 10cm along wood, projecting nearly 6cm outward, 1-3cm thick, semicircular to extended laterally; gray-ocher to brownish; "coarsely hispid-pilose, zoned with indistinct undulations, hairs stiff and agglutinated into tufts"; cap margin sharp, appressed-tomentose, and slightly wavy, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  up to 0.2cm thick, duplex, "with a firm lower layer and soft spongy upper layer"; cream-buff (whitish)^, (Gilbertson), 0.5-2cm thick, corky, tough; whitish cream, (Breitenbach)
PORES  up to 11 mm [sic] in diameter, angular to labyrinthiform [maze-like], walls thin and torn; ochraceous buff; tube layer up to 0.9cm thick, colored as lower layer of flesh and continuous with it^, (Gilbertson), 1-2 per mm, rounded-angular, toothed; cream to ocherish, "often with a hint of pink"; tube layer up to 0.8cm thick, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on dead hardwoods, most common on Populus and Salix (willow)^, associated with a white rot of dead hardwoods, (Gilbertson), occasionally single but usually irregularly imbricate [shingled] on dead wood of Populus, rarely on other hardwoods, (Breitenbach)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 10-12 x 2.5-4 microns, cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 25-33 x 6-9 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia absent; hyphae trimitic, generative hyphae of context 2-4 microns wide, "with clamps, thin-walled, with rare branching", skeletal hyphae of context 3-4 microns wide, "thick-walled, with occasional branching, nonseptate", binding hyphae of context 2-3.5 microns wide, "thick-walled, nonseptate, much branched"; hyphae of trama similar, (Gilbertson), spores 6.5-11 x 3-3.5 microns, cylindric-elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  named for J.G. Trog^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Coriolopsis gallica has yellowish brown to dark brown flesh, the grayish brown to gray pore surface has pores 0.3-1 per millimeter, and spores measure 10-16 x 3-5 microns, whereas C. trogii has white to pale cream flesh, the ochraceous buff pore surface has pores 1-2 per millimeter, and spores measure 8-12 x 2.5-4.0 microns, (Ginns(28)). C. gallica is generally less pale in color, and has a brown to gray pore surface, brown flesh, and tubes whitish to gray on the inner walls with brown tissue between the tubes, (Gilbertson). C. gallica has brown flesh that turns black with KOH, (Breitenbach). Trametes hirsuta has "softer non-agglutinated hairs, smaller pores (2-4 per mm), and tube mouths with entire margins", (Breitenbach).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Trametes trogii), Breitenbach(2)* (as Trametes trogii), Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Cryptoporus volvatus  (Peck) Shear^  Bull. Torrey Bot. Club 29: 450. 1902;  Polyporus volvatus  Peck
ENGLISH NAMES  veiled polypore, cryptic globe fungus
NOTES  Features include a hoof-shaped to spherical, tan-colored fruitbody on conifers, which when sliced open is seen to have a hollow cavity lined in its upper part by pores, a tube layer and flesh, and in its lower part by a relatively thin layer that may be perforated. Spores are apparently spread by bark beetles as well as by wind.^ RANGE It is found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NB, ON, AZ, CA, CO, DE, FL, MA, MD, ME, MI, MN, MT, NE, NH, NJ, NM, NV, NY, PA, SD, UT, VA, VT, WV, WY, and east Asia, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  1.5-8.5cm, more or less spherical to ovoid or slightly compressed or hoof-like, with hollow interior, upper surface whitish to warm tan or yellowish drying ocher-brown to reddish brown, with thin, smooth, glazed or resinous crust, 'margin extending down and under to form "veil" which completely covers pore surface; in age the underside perforated by one (rarely two) holes'^, (Arora), up to 4cm x 5cm x 4cm, hoof-shaped, margin continuous with a volva-like structure that encloses pore surface except for a small hole at the base; upper surface cream to yellowish or tan; not zoned, bald but often coated with a clear lacquer-like layer, smooth or wrinkled, (Gilbertson)
FLESH  tough; whitish^, (Arora), up to 2cm thick, soft-corky; ivory-white, not zoned, (Gilbertson)
PORES  3-4 per mm, hidden by "veil", "white becoming pinkish or brownish in age"^, (Arora), 4-5 per mm, circular, with thick, entire walls; pale to dark chocolate brown; tube layer up to 0.6cm thick, pinkish buff, (Gilbertson)
STEM  none
ODOR  "often fragrant (like Sparassis)"^, (Arora), slightly resinous (Miller)
TASTE  slightly bitter^ (Gilbertson)
EDIBILITY  too tough (Arora)
HABITAT  annual, "solitary or more often in groups on dead or occasionally old living conifers"^, (Arora), single or in large numbers, on recently killed conifers, "commonly fruiting a year after trees are killed by fire, bark beetles, or other factors and then displaced by other saprophytic wood-rotting fungi", especially on Pinus (pine), Abies (fir), and Pseudotsuga (Douglas-fir), causes soft grayish-white rot of sapwood, (Gilbertson), fruiting in spring, summer, and fall, (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  pinkish or flesh-colored (Arora), white to cream-color (Miller)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-12 x 3-5 microns, cylindric to elliptic, smooth^, (Arora), spores 12-16.5 x 4.5 microns, cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless; cystidioles 20-28 x 5-7 microns, not projecting, but fusoid, thin-walled, with basal clamp; hyphal system trimitic, contextual generative hyphae mostly 3-7 microns wide (but with inflated parts at branches up to 15 microns wide), thin-walled, with clamp connections at all septa, with occasional branching, contextual skeletal hyphae 2.5-8 microns, thick-walled, colorless, nonseptate, with occasional branching, binding hyphae 1.5-2.5 microns wide, thick-walled, nonseptate, much branched, tramal hyphae similar, (Gilbertson)
NAME ORIGIN  means "provided with a volva"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Arora(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Miller(14)*, Desjardin(6)*, Ginns(28)*, Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Daedalea quercina  Fr.^  Syst. Mycol. 1: 333. 1821
ENGLISH NAMES  thick-maze oak polypore, oak maze-gill, oak polypore
NOTES  Features include tough shelf-like cork-colored fruitbodies on hardwood, an undersurface with thick walls forming elongated pores or maze-like to gill-like structures, and a resulting a brown rot.^ It is less common in western North America than Daedaleopsis confragosa, Gloeophyllum saepiarium, Lenzites betulina, and Cerrena unicolor, all of which may have a maze-like spore-bearing surface. RANGE Daedalea quercina has been found in OR, NB, NS, ON, CA, CT, DE, GA, IA, MA, ME, MD, NC, NH, NJ, NY, OH, PA, WV, VA, and VT, rare west of the Mississippi, and also circumglobal through Asia and Europe, (Gilbertson). Confirmation would be desirable for the OR record as species like Lenzites betulina and Daedaleopsis are sometimes misidentified as Daedalea quercina. Michael Beug's attached photos were taken Coyote Wall, Klickitat County, WA on a Quercus garryana stump (Columbia River Gorge).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  4-20cm wide, 1.5-8cm thick, shelf-like, more or less fan-shaped, convex or flat, rigid, tough or corky, margin often thick; whitish to tan, ochraceous, grayish, or brown, often blackening and cracking when old; uneven or roughened, usually concentrically furrowed or zoned when older^, (Arora), up to 20cm wide, 15cm broad, and 8cm thick, broadly shelf-like to semicircular, single or sometimes fused laterally, strongly attached to wood, corky to woody, upper surface flat to convex, often with slightly raised base, margin sharp; margin ochraceous, inner parts of upper surface "deeper brownish to grayish in old specimens, sometimes with pads or smaller areas with fresh outgrowth of light ochraceous mycelium"; upper surface smooth to finely velvety, "in some specimens with tufts of raised hyphae or scattered nodulae or warts, the base more rough or scrupose than the margin which is usually smooth", (Gilbertson)
FLESH  "very tough and corky; white to buff, ochraceous, or pale brown (never dark)"^, (Arora), up to 1cm thick, ochraceous to tobacco brown with indistinct annual zones, (Gilbertson)
PORES  usually greatly elongated and pocket-like or maze-like, sometimes forming gill-like structures, tube walls thick (1mm or more), spaces between walls at least 1mm; whitish to buff, tan, or dull ocher; tube layer 0.5-3cm thick, without distinct stratified layers^, (Arora), ochraceous, surface flat to oblique, especially close to the wood, near margin elongated pore-like, in the inner parts with sinuous pores or maze-like to almost gill-like, on oblique parts the pores deeply split in front, pores mostly 1-4mm wide measured tangentially with walls 1-3mm thick; tube layer up to 4cm thick, light ochraceous on inner walls but trama between them distinctly darker, (Gilbertson)
STEM  none or rudimentary
ODOR  faintly acrid or mushroomy^ (Phillips), pleasantly fungoid, (Breitenbach), none (Miller)
TASTE  sharpish^ (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  too tough (Arora)
HABITAT  perennial, "solitary or sometimes in shelving groups on dead or living hardwoods, especially oak, chestnut, and chinquapin"^, (Arora), on different hardwoods, especially Quercus (oak) but also Castanea (chestnut), Fagus (beech), Fraxinus (ash), Juglans (walnut), Populus, Prunus, Ulmus (elm), causes a brown heart rot that eventually hollows the wood, (Gilbertson), also on railway ties and wood used in construction, (Breitenbach), fruiting in summer and fall (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  whitish^ (Arora), white (Miller)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5-7.5 x 2-3.5 microns, cylindric to elliptic, smooth^, (Arora), spores 5.5-6 x 2.5-3.5 microns, cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, thin-walled, difficult to find (period of spore-formation seems to be short and basidia collapse quickly under drying); basidia 4-spored, 20-27 x 6-7.5 microns, very difficult to find; cystidia none, but "skeletal hyphae bend into hymenium as a dense catahymenium with cystidia-like, rounded and thick-walled organs, often with a fine granular exudate, some of the skeletal hyphae are slightly swollen and pointed in the end and their similarity to true hymenial cystidia is striking unless they are followed into the vertical trama where they originate"; hyphal system trimitic, generative hyphae 1.5-4 microns wide, thin-walled, colorless, and with clamp connections at the septa, binding hyphae "tortuous with short branches, thick-walled to solid, light golden yellowish-brown", skeletal hyphae dominating, 3-6 microns wide, thick-walled to solid, light-brown, (Gilbertson), spores 5.5-6 x 2.5-3.5 microns, cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, thin-walled, "difficult to find in most specimens as the periods of sporulation seem to be short and the basidia collapse rapidly on drying"; basidia 4-spored, 20-27 x 6-7.5 microns, clavate, "very difficult to find even if fresh specimens are examined, occurring singly between the projecting skeletal hyphae", with a basal clamp, (Lindner)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'pertaining to oak'
SIMILAR  Daedaleopsis confragosa is commoner in western North America, with thinner often shallower tube walls that are more variable in form, and usually annual growth, (Arora). Lenzites betulina has a spore-bearing surface that is plate-like with a gilled appearance, often branching toward the margin or forming elongated pockets (rather than thick-walled elongated to maze-like structures). Gloeophyllum sepiarium has a brown spore-bearing surface that is gill-like or with a poroid or tooth-like surface or with elongated pores, and the upper surface is rusty brown to dark brown or maroon brown, often with yellow or orangish zones. Cerrena unicolor has a hirsute cap, a finer pore structure with 2-4 pores per mm, and a black line in the flesh. Trichaptum laricinum has thin but rigid hairy caps that are tan to gray, and a purplish gill surface.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Arora(1)*, Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Lincoff(1)*, Miller(14)*, Courtecuisse(1)*, McKnight(1)*, Breitenbach(2)*, Bessette(2)*, Lindner(1), Bacon(1)*, Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Daedaleopsis confragosa  (Bolton: Fr.) J. Schroet.^  Krypt. Fl. Schles. 3: 492. 1888;  Daedalea confragosa  Bolton: Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  thin-maze flat polypore, thin-walled maze polypore, blushing bracket
NOTES  Features include a relatively thin, corky, fan-shaped, brown to grayish cap with a bald, often zoned surface, and an elongated to maze-like (but sometimes poroid or gill-like or tooth-like) spore-bearing surface that is white to brown and may bruise pinkish.^ It is more common in the east than the west in North America. RANGE Daedaleopsis confragosa has been found in BC, WA, AB, MB, NS, ON, PQ, AL, AR, AZ, CT, FL, GA, IA, IL, IN, KS, KY, LA, MA, ME, MI, MN, MO, MS, MT, NC, ND, NE, NH, NJ, NM, NY, OH, PA, SC, SD, TN, TX, VA, VT, WI, and WV, (Gilbertson). It has been found in YT (Ginns(28)). It also occurs in Europe, Asia, (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  (3)5-15(22)cm wide, shelf-like or sometimes bracket-like, fan-shaped to semicircular, broadly convex to flat, leathery or corky, drying rigid, margin thin, acute (sharp); reddish-brown to brown to grayish, sometimes blackish when old; "dry, smooth or slightly hairy, usually zoned or ridged concentrically, often radially wrinkled or bumpy in age"^, (Arora), up to 12cm wide, shelf-like semicircular without stem or bending into a cap from a part of the fruitbody that is flat on wood with pore surface exposed, tough-corky, annual; upper surface buff to light brown; usually zoned and shallowly grooved, surface matted-strigose to bald, (Gilbertson)
FLESH  tough; white to pinkish or brownish^, (Arora), up to 2cm thick, firm-corky; pale buff to brown, not zoned, (Gilbertson)
PORES  0.5-1.5mm wide, "usually elongated and mazelike with relatively thin walls, but sometimes circular and at other times forming gills or becoming toothlike in age; white to tan or brown, sometimes bruising pinkish or reddish"; tube layer up to 1.5cm thick^, (Arora), pores variable: circular or radially elongated and up to 1mm wide, maze-like, or with walls splitting to form a radially gill-like structure; tube layer up to 1cm thick, colored as flesh and continuous with it, (Gilbertson)
STEM  none
ODOR  mild^ (Breitenbach)
TASTE  mild to somewhat bitter^ (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  no (Arora)
HABITAT  usually annual, single or in groups on dead hardwoods or from wounds in living trees, especially willow, but also birch and other hardwoods and very rarely on conifers^, (Arora), annual, on dead wood of hardwoods, rarely conifers, causes white rot, (Gilbertson), spores released in fall (Bacon)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-11 x 2-3 microns, cylindric, smooth^, (Arora), spores 9-11 x 2-2.5 microns, cylindric, slightly curved, smooth, inamyloid, colorless; basidia 4-spored, 30-45 x 4-5 microns, narrowly clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia none, branching dendrohyphidia 2-3 microns wide, thin-walled, not incrusted; hyphal system trimitic: context generative hyphae 2-6 microns wide, thin-walled, nodose-septate, with occasional branching, context skeletal hyphae 4-7 microns wide, thick-walled, nonseptate, with rare branching, context binding hyphae 2-4.5 microns wide, thick-walled, nonseptate, much-branched, trama hyphae similar, (Gilbertson), spores 6.5-8 x (1.5)2-2.5 microns, cylindric, slightly curved, smooth, inamyloid, some with drops, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  means "rough, rugged"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Daedalea quercina is rare in western North America, with thicker often deeper tube walls that are less variable in form, and usually perennial growth, (Arora). Lenzites betulina has a spore-bearing surface that is plate-like with a gilled appearance, often branching toward the margin or forming elongated pockets. Gloeophyllum sepiarium has a brown spore-bearing surface that is gill-like or with a poroid or tooth-like surface or with elongated pores, and the upper surface is rusty brown to dark brown or maroon brown, often with yellow or orangish zones. Cerrena unicolor has a hirsute cap, a finer pore structure with 2-4 pores per mm, and a black line in the flesh. Trichaptum laricinum has thin but rigid hairy caps that are tan to gray, and a purplish gill surface.

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Datronia mollis  (Sommerf.: Fr.) Donk^  Persoonia 4: 338. 1966;  Daedalea mollis  Sommerf.: Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  common mazegill
NOTES  Distinctive field characters are the large irregular pores and the black layer separating the upper tomentum from the lower flesh. These together distinguish it from other Datronia spp. in the Pacific Northwest. Other features include a usually shelf-like form, a dark brown concentrically grooved upper surface, and a pore surface that is buff to umber brown with angular to maze-like pores.^ RANGE Datronia mollis has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, NS, ON, PQ, AK, CO, IA, IL, IN, KY, ME, MI, MN, MT, NH, NY, OH, PA, TN, VA, VT, WI, WV, and WY, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  usually bent out to form cap from part of the fruitbody that is flat on wood with pore-surface exposed, occasionally shelf-like or entirely flat on the wood, cap up to 2cm broad, annual; upper surface dark brown to black; strigose to bald, concentrically zoned and grooved^, (Gilbertson), up to 7cm wide, 2.5cm broad, 0.6cm thick, shelf-like brackets often in tiers, wavy, thin but tough and leathery; upper surface umber brown becoming darker brown to black; velvety then smooth, (Phillips), bound +/- loosely to wood, when shelf-like projecting up to 1.5cm from wood, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  lower layer pale buff, up to 0.1cm thick, "separated from dark brown upper layer of tomentum by a thin black layer"^, (Gilbertson), up to 0.1cm thick, lower layer pale buff, upper layer dark brown, separated by thin black layer, (Phillips), leathery and tough when fresh, hard and brittle when dry, use hand lens to see black line between flesh and tube layer, (Breitenbach)
PORES  1-2 per mm, some over 1mm wide, pore shape angular to maze-like, walls becoming thin and splitting; buff to umber brown; tube layer up to 0.3cm thick, colored as lower flesh^, (Gilbertson), 1-2 per mm, angular, irregularly elongate or slot-like; buff to umber brown; tube layer up to 0.3cm thick, pale buff, (Phillips), gray-brownish to ocher-brownish, discoloring somewhat brown when bruised, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  no (Phillips)
HABITAT  annual, dead hardwood, causing white rot with conspicuous black zone lines in decayed wood^, (Gilbertson), on hardwoods, especially Alnus (alder), (Ginns), all year (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 10-12 x 3-4.5 microns, cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 30-45 x 5-6.5 microns, clavate, with a narrow base, with basal clamp; cystidioles imbedded or slightly projecting, 25-45 x 4-5 microns, fusoid, thin-walled, with basal clamp, dendrohyphidia present on dissepiment edges, 1.5-3 microns wide, branched and contorted; hyphal system dimitic: context generative hyphae 2.5-4 microns wide, thin-walled, nodose-septate, context skeletal hyphae "thick-walled, aseptate, those in lower context pale greenish-brown in KOH, those in upper dark layer darker brown in KOH, some with frequent branching, 2.5-4 microns in diam", trama hyphae similar to those in lower context, (Gilbertson), spores 8-10.5 x 3.5-4 microns, cylindric-elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, some with drops, (Breitenbach) 
NAME ORIGIN  means "soft"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  See also SIMILAR section of Datronia stereoides.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Phillips(1)*, Breitenbach(2)*, Buczacki(1)*, Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Datronia scutellata  (Schwein.) Gilb. & Ryvarden^  Mycotaxon 22: 364. 1985
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Datronia scutellata is recognized by the small black cap with a pale pore surface. Other features include a tough consistency, small pores, and large cylindric spores.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1). RANGE Datronia scutellata has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, MB, NB, NS, ON, PE, PQ, AZ, CT, FL, GA, IA, IN, ME, MI, MN, MT, NC, NH, NM, NY, OH, PA, TN, VT, and WV, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 3cm x 1.5cm x 0.3-1cm, shelf-like or bent out from flat pore surface to form cap, semicircular to almost pendent (hanging by slender attachment) or broadly attached, tough, drying hard, annual; upper surface whitish at first, soon dark brown to black, margin often remaining paler than basal parts; at first velvety but soon bald and often slightly grooved in zones
FLESH  0.1-0.3cm thick, dense; wood-colored to pale brown with a distinct black crust on top
PORES  4-5 per mm, round to slightly angular, walls often finely farinose; white to buff or pale brown when old; tube layer up to 0.7cm thick, cork-colored to wood-colored
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on several genera, with a slight preference for Alnus (alder), causing a white rot in dead hardwoods
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-12 x 3-3.5 microns, cylindric, inamyloid, colorless, thin-walled^; basidia 20-25 x 7-10 microns, broadly clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia none but cystidioles 20-26 x 7-8 microns, fusoid, with basal clamp; hyphal system trimitic: generative hyphae "with clamps, hyaline in context and trama, 2.5-4 microns wide, pigmented hyphae with clamps present on pileus surface"; skeletal hyphae 2-4.5 microns wide, solid, pale yellowish, binding hyphae of same width as skeletal hyphae but much more branched
NAME ORIGIN  means "covered with small scales or plates"^ (Gilbertson)

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Datronia stereoides  (Fr.) Ryvarden^  Flora over Kjuker p.42. 1968;  Coriolus planellus  Murrill
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Datronia stereoides is distinguished in the field by the granulose (under a hand lens) walls of the small regular pale pinkish-buff pores, and the brown tomentum separated from the pale lower flesh by a black layer. Other features include a shelf-like form, a brown, non-zoned, upper surface that is tomentose to strigose, thin flesh, and microscopic characters.^ RANGE Datronia stereoides has been found in BC, ID, AB, MB, AK, AZ, CA, CO, IA, IL MA, ME, MN, MO, MT, ND, NH, NY, VT, and WI, (Gilbertson). It is also known from SK (Ginns). It occurs in Europe, Asia, and Australia, (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  bent out to form cap up to 1cm wide from pore surface lying flat on wood, or sometimes entirely flat, cap elongate or semicircular, annual; upper surface brown; usually azonate, tomentose to radially strigose (with coarse stiff appressed hairs)^, (Gilbertson), forming patches flat on wood with pore surface exposed, several centimeters to decimeters across, or forming shelf-like caps projecting 0.5-2cm, dark brown to blackish, narrowly zoned, and slightly tomentose; margin of pore surface distinctly bounded, whitish, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  up to 0.2cm thick, lower part pale brown, separated from dark upper tomentum by a thin black layer^, (Gilbertson), up to 0.1cm thick, ocher-brownish, (Breitenbach)
PORES  4-5 per mm, circular to hexagonal, regular; pale pinkish buff; walls thick, entire, appearing granulose under 30x lens; tube layer up to 0.1cm thick, colored as flesh and continuous with it^, (Gilbertson), 4-5(6) per mm, rounded; "gray-ocher to gray-brown, sometimes with slight pink tint, when fresh turning slightly brown when handled"; tube layer 0.1-0.2cm thick, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on dead hardwood, most commonly on Populus and Betula (birch), very rarely on conifers, causes a white rot^, (Gilbertson), single or in rows, on underside of dead trunks and branches of Fagus (beech) lying on the ground, also on other hardwoods, (Breitenbach for Europe)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 10-13 x 4.5-6 microns, cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 25-40 x 7-8.5 microns, clavate with a narrow base, with basal clamp; cystidioles not conspicuous or abundant, not projecting, 30-40 x 5-6 microns, fusoid; dendrohyphidia abundant on dissepiment edges, 1.5-2 microns wide, much branched and contorted; hyphal system dimitic: context generative hyphae 2-2.5 microns wide, thin-walled, nodose-septate, context skeletal hyphae 2-4 microns wide, thick-walled, aseptate, with frequent branching, those in lower context pale greenish brown in KOH, those in dark upper layer dark brown in KOH, trama hyphae similar to those in lower context, (Gilbertson), spores 9.5-12 x 3.5-4.5 microns, cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, with droplets, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  means "like Stereum"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Datronia mollis has large labyrinthine pores and a thicker fruitbody, (Breitenbach).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Breitenbach(2)*, Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Dichomitus campestris  (Quel.) Domanski & Orlicz^  Acta Soc. Bot. Pol. 35: 627. 1966;  Trametes campestris  Quel.;  Coriolellus campestris  (Quel.) Bondartsev
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include cushion-shaped fruiting body lying flat on wood with the ochraceous to straw-colored pore surface exposed, often with a steep, typically blackened margin, relatively large pores (1-3 per mm) and microscopic characters including spores 9-19 microns long.^ It is rare in North America (Gilbertson). RANGE Dichomitus campestris has been found in ID, AZ, and NM, and found in Europe as well, (Gilbertson)

CAP  growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, up to 10cm long, up to 1.5cm thick in center, typically cushion-shaped with a distinctly thickened central part, oblong to oval, "now and then nodulose with a blackened upper part as if with a sloping pileus"; margin narrow, dirty ochraceous to blackish^, (Gilbertson), cushion-shaped fruitbody lying flat on wood with pore surface exposed, 3-5(20)cm across, 0.5-1(1.5)cm thick; margin soon turning black, (Breitenbach), margin steep, typically black, (Ginns)
FLESH  0.1-0.3cm thick; white to wood-colored^, (Gilbertson), corky, +/- soft; light wood-colored to ocherish, (Breitenbach)
PORES  1-2(3) per mm, thin-walled and angular, on sloping wood surfaces often elongated and more irregular; ochraceous to straw-colored; tube layer up to 1.5cm thick, colored as pore surface^, (Gilbertson), 1-2 per mm, "irregularly polygonal and large but irregular in size"; cream to pale wood-colored; tube layer 0.1-0.3cm ("tube length 1-3 mm"), layered in perennial fruitbodies, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual to perennial, on dead hardwoods including Alnus (alder) and Quercus (oak) in North America, in Europe on many other hosts as well, causes a white rot^, (Gilbertson), single to several on one trunk, on "dead, usually standing or attached trunks and branches of Corylus (hazel), but also on Quercus (oak) and Juglans (walnut tree)", (Breitenbach for Europe), mainly fall (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 13-19 x 5-6.5 microns, cylindric, inamyloid, colorless, thin-walled^; basidia 4-spored, 25-35 x 7-10 microns, clavate; cystidia absent; hyphal system dimitic: generative hyphae 2-3.5 microns wide, thin-walled, colorless, with clamp connections, binding hyphae sparingly and dichotomously branched, up to 8 microns wide in main stems, tapering down the long branches, colorless, thick-walled, straight to slightly sinuous, (Gilbertson), spores 9-12.5 x 3.5-4.5 microns, elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, with droplets, (Breitenbach), spores 9-10 microns long (Ginns)
NAME ORIGIN  means "growing in a field"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Breitenbach(2)*, Buczacki(1)*, Ginns(28)

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Dichomitus squalens  (P. Karst.) D.A. Reid^  Rev. Biol. 5: 149. 1965;  Trametes squalens  P. Karst.;  Polyporus anceps  Peck;  Tyromyces ellisianus  Murrill
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include fruitbodies the grow flat, or shelf-like to shingled on conifers, tough caps that are whitish discoloring darker, small round to angular pores that are white to wood-colored discoloring darker, and microscopic characters including arboriform binding hyphae.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1). RANGE Dichomitus squalens has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NB, NF, ON, PQ, SK, YT, AK, AZ, CA, CO, CT, LA, MD, ME, MI, MN, MS, MT, NE, NH, NJ, NM, NWT, NY, PA, SD, VA, VT, and WI, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  shelf-like, single to imbricate [shingled] or with cap(s) bent out from fruitbody growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, or entirely flat (may spread out up to 100cm or more), caps usually triquetrous, "up to 3cm wide, 1-7cm long", 0.3-1.5cm thick at base, tough and corky drying hard, annual to reviving a second year; "upper surface white to cream, with age discolored and ultimately bay to almost blackish from the base and then with a very thin cuticle, at first finely tomentose, later glabrous, azonate or slightly concentrically zonate, in dry condition often slightly wrinkled radially, margin white, narrow to glabrous, discolored by age"
FLESH  0.1-0.4cm thick, dense; white
PORES  4-5 per mm, round to angular, thin-walled; white to wood-colored, when old "more yellowish or discolored in light brown and gray shades, often unevenly"; tube layer up to 1cm thick, colored as pore surface or paler
STEM  none
ODOR  none
TASTE  slightly bitter
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual to reviving a second year, on conifers, causes "a white pocket rot in heartwood of living conifers and also of dead conifer wood"
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-10 x 2.5-3.5 microns, cylindric to oblong-elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, thin-walled^; basidia 15-22 x 6-8 microns, clavate; cystidia absent; hyphal system dimitic: generative hyphae 1.5-4 microns wide, thin-walled and colorless, with clamp connections, "arboriform and usually dichotomously branched binding hyphae predominant in the fruitbody, hyaline, thick-walled to solid, up to 7 microns wide in the main stems, tapering down to thin whip-like ends"
NAME ORIGIN  means "dirty" or "filthy"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Tyromyces and Trametes species can look similar macroscopically, but do not have the arboriform binding hyphae microscopically (Gilbertson).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Diplomitoporus lindbladii  (Berk.) Gilb. & Ryvarden^  Mycotaxon 22: 364. 1985.;  Cinereomyces lindbladii  (Berk.) Julich  Biblthca Mycol. 85: 400 (1982) [1981];  Poria subavellanea  Murrill
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include growth flat on wood with the typically pale grayish pore surface exposed, separability, small pores, and microscopic characters including allantoid to cylindric spores and large skeletal hyphae which become gelatinized in KOH.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where indicated. RANGE Diplomitoporus lindbladii has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, NF, AZ, CA, CO, LA, MA, MN, MS, MT, NM, NY, SC, TX, and WI, and it is circumpolar in the coniferous zone, [so presumably in Europe and Asia], (Gilbertson).

CAP  growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, up to 0.6cm thick, separable, soft to tough, margin narrow to wide, white
FLESH  up to 0.3cm thick, cottony; white

ODOR  faint, unpleasant^ (Buczacki)
TASTE  indistinct^, (Buczacki)

SIMILAR  Diplomitoporus crustulinus has a pore surface that is cream to very pale straw yellow, and skeletal hyphae are neither dextrinoid nor amyloid (Ginns).

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota

ENGLISH NAMES

FAMILY  Echinodontiaceae, Order Russulales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Echinodontium tinctorium  (Ellis & Everh.) Ellis & Everh.^  Torrey Bot. Club Bull. 27: 49. 1900;  Fomes tinctorius  Ellis & Everh.
ENGLISH NAMES  Indian paint fungus, toothed conk
NOTES  Also listed in Toothed category. Echinodontium tinctorium forms hard hoof-shaped conks on hemlock and true fir, with a cracked hairy blackish upper surface, grayish blunt spines, and rusty-orange flesh. The common name India paint fungus refers to its use by First Nations people in preparing war paint: in powdered form it was also used by shamans as medicine. It can also be used as a red dye for yarn.^ RANGE It is found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, AK, AZ, CA, CO, MT, NM, NV, UT, WY, Mexico, and not elsewhere in the world, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  4-25cm broad, up to 15cm thick, hoof-shaped, very tough or woody; upper surface dry, finely hairy to rough, often covered with moss^, (Arora), up to 40cm wide, 30cm deep and 20cm thick, without a stem, hoof-shaped to flattened, usually developing under branch stubs, perennial; dark dull brown quickly becoming blackened, margin brown; hispid (with bristles) to matted-hirsute (matted, with long stiff hairs) at first, quickly becoming crustose and rimose, cracking radially and concentrically into rectangular blocks, sulcate (grooved), margin hispid to coarsely hirsute, (Gilbertson)
FLESH  very tough or woody; "bright orange to rusty-red or cinnamon"^, (Arora), up to 5cm thick, upper blackish crustose layer up to 0.2cm thick, the rest brick-red, with radially arranged fibers, and hard and woody in consistency, in older fruitbodies with a thick layer of fused older teeth below the fibrous flesh, (Gilbertson)
PORES  1-3cm spines rather than pores, brittle, blunt, thick, flattened, grayish to pale olive-buff, sometimes with darker tips^, (Arora), irregularly pore-like to maze-like at margin, the walls quickly splitting to form flattened to cylindric teeth, which at first are thin and brittle but become thick, rigid and crowded by layering of the surface when old, the teeth up to 1.5cm long and 0.25cm thick, pale buff to pinkish buff when bearing spores, inner tissue becoming brick red as layering of spore-bearing surface progresses, (Gilbertson), teeth variable in color, eventually grayish then black, (Lincoff(1))
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  no (Arora)
HABITAT  single or several on living or occasionally downed conifers such as fir and hemlock^, (Arora), perennial, primarily on Abies (true fir) and Tsuga (hemlock), rarely on other conifers, causes a yellowish laminated to stringy heartrot of living conifers, the main cause of heartrot and volume loss in true firs in western coniferous forests, (Gilbertson)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white when obtainable^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5.5-8 x 3.5-6 microns elliptic, minutely spiny, amyloid^, (Arora), spores 6-8 x 4.5-6 microns, elliptic, smooth to minutely echinulate, weakly to moderately amyloid, colorless, thick-walled; basidia 4-spored, 35-45 x 6.5-8 microns, narrowly clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia abundant, 25-65 x 8-17 microns, "becoming thick-walled, ventricose to subulate, dark reddish brown in KOH and Melzer's reagent, some paler at the tip, fusoid to mammillate, apically incrusted but incrustation dissolving readily in KOH and Melzer's reagent"; hyphal system dimitic, context generative hyphae 2.5-6 microns wide, thin-walled, colorless in KOH, with clamp connections and simple septa, occasionally branched, context skeletal hyphae 3-6.5 microns wide, thick-walled, bright reddish brown in KOH and Melzer's reagent, with rare branching, nonseptate, trama generative hyphae 2-5 microns wide, with abundant clamp connections, trama skeletal hyphae similar to those in context, (Gilbertson)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin meaning "used for dyeing"^ (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Phillips(1)*, Arora(1), Lincoff(2)*, Lincoff(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fibroporia vaillantii  (DC.) Parmasto^  Consp. System. Corticiac. (Tartu): 177 (1968);  Antrodia vaillantii  (Fr.) Ryvarden  Norw. J. Bot. 20: 8. 1973;  Polyporus vaillantii  Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Fibroporia vaillantii is "normally easy to recognize because of its relatively large spores and the rhizomorphic margin" (Gilbertson). Other features include flat growth on conifer wood, particularly structural timbers in humid locations like mines and basements, a white to cream pore surface, and mild taste.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where noted. RANGE Fibroporia vaillantii has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AZ, CA, CO, CT, FL, IL, MD, NV, NY, MO, MS, NC, NJ, PA, TN, VA, and WI, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, often widely spread out, up to 0.4cm thick, separable, soft-fibrous, margin often wide with strands and rhizomorphs; pore surface "white to cream, becoming pale sordid brown in parts when dry", margin also white to cream
FLESH  0.1-0.2cm thick, soft and cottony; white
PORES  2-4 per mm, circular to angular, when old also larger; "white to cream, becoming pale sordid brown in parts when dry"; tube layer colored as pore surface, soft and cottony, drying somewhat brittle when old
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  mild
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, known only from conifers and "mostly found in mines, basements, etc. on structural timbers in humid locations", causes a brown cubical rot^, (Gilbertson), probably all year (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5-8 x 3-4 microns, broadly elliptic, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 14-18 x 5-6 microns, clavate; cystidia absent; hyphal system dimitic, generative hyphae 2-6 microns wide, thin-walled, with clamp connections, skeletal hyphae predominant in context and rhizomorphs, 2-5 microns wide, solid to thick-walled, colorless, inamyloid, straight and unbranched, rarely dichotomously branched
NAME ORIGIN  "named for S. Vaillant"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Postia angulopora is rather similar macroscopically and microscopically, and also causes a brown rot in Douglas-fir, but "differs mainly in the distinctive contorted thick-walled hyphae in the trama and in its lack of rhizomorphs", (Gilbertson) . See also SIMILAR section of Antrodia gossypium and Antrodia radiculosa.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Antrodia vaillantii), Ginns(28)* (as Antrodia vaillantii), Buczacki(1)* (as Antrodia vaillantii)
73
FAMILY  Fistulinaceae, Order Agaricales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fistulina hepatica  (Schaeff.) With.^  Bot. Arr. Brit. Pl., Edn 2 2: 405. 1792
ENGLISH NAMES  beefsteak fungus, ox-tongue fungus, poor-man's beefsteak, ox-tongue, beefsteak
NOTES  Fruitbodies are semicircular to kidney-shaped or fan-shaped, often lobed, at first fleshy and exuding reddish sap when bruised or squeezed. The upper surface is orange-buff to pinkish, reddish brown, dark red, or liver-colored, and suede-like to gelatinous or sticky. The pore surface underneath is white to yellowish but turns brown when bruised or old. The tubes are closely packed but are seen to be discrete under a hand lens. The stem is absent or short and lateral. Taste is sour or acidic.^ The rich brown hue it gives to wood is prized by cabinet-makers (Arora). The English name 'beefsteak' is apparently used also for Lactarius corrugis and Gyromitra caroliniana. RANGE Fistulina hepatica has been found in southern OR (Michael Beug, pers. comm.), and CA, AL, AR, FL, GA, KY, LA, MS, NC, NY, PA, SC, TN, TX, and VA - it is transglobal in temperate hardwood forest, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  7-30cm wide and 2-6cm thick when mature, "tongue-shaped or fan-shaped", "firm or very soft and spongy, sometimes knobby", margin often lobed; orange-buff to reddish orange to pinkish, reddish brown, dark red, or liver-colored; minutely roughened and suede-like or velvety, but often gelatinous when wet^, (Arora), up to 20cm in diameter and 6cm thick, annual, dimidiate to kidney-shaped or nearly circular, "at first soft and fleshy and readily exuding a reddish blood-like sap when squeezed or bruised, eventually more fibrous and tough in older specimens"; "pinkish brown to more reddish or purplish brown"; finely hispid [bristly] to scurfy, "wearing away to expose a relatively smooth, slimy, reddish to pale purplish brown cuticle with minute darker scales or radially striate"; margin rounded to rather acute, colored as cap surface, (Gilbertson), upper surface streaked, and moist to sticky when young and fresh, (McKnight)
FLESH  "thick, soft and watery when fresh and oozing with dark reddish juice; whitish to grayish, olive-brown, or reddish, streaked or marbled horizontally with paler veins (usually reddening after standing)"^, (Arora), "reddish, fleshy and juicy when fresh, with a blood-like exudate where cut or broken, mottled or irregularly zonate with alternating pale and darker areas, in older specimens or on drying becoming soft-fibrous, pale wood-brown, up to 5cm thick", (Gilbertson)
PORES  1-3 per millimeter, "circular, whitish to yellowish, buff, or pale flesh-color", becoming dark reddish brown when bruised or when old; tubes 1-1.5cm deep, "closely packed but discrete" (their walls not joined, so that they are arranged like the bristles of a brush)^, (Arora), white at first, bruising darker on handling and becoming dull brown when old or dry; individual tubes crowded, about 4-6 per millimeter; tube layer up to 1cm thick, consisting of individual, crowded but easily separable tubes, white to pale buff, bruising dark reddish brown, drying pale brown, (Gilbertson)
STEM  "absent or if present then lateral and rather short", 2-6(10)cm long, 1-4cm thick; "tougher than the cap but continuous with it and colored similarly, firm"^, (Arora), sessile or with lateral stem, single or several from a branched base or stem; stem up to 5cm long and 3cm wide (or on some subsessile specimens simply a broad, tapering base up to 8cm wide), reddish at first, darkening to blackish brown on the basal part; "scurfy with papillate tufts, these merging with tubes on the decurrent tube layer", (Gilbertson)
ODOR  pleasant (Phillips), mild (Miller)
TASTE  rather sour or strongly acidic^ (Arora), sour (Miller)
EDIBILITY  yes, with strong sour taste (which can be reduced by parboiling), (Arora)
HABITAT  single or sometimes several together "at the bases of hardwoods and on stumps, especially of chestnut and oak"; in coastal California on chinquapin in the fall and winter^, (Arora), generally on Quercus (oak) or Castanea (chestnut) but occurring on other hardwoods; causes brown rot of living and dead hardwoods, (Gilbertson), July to October (Lincoff(2))
SPORE DEPOSIT  pinkish salmon to pale ocher or pale rusty brown^, (Arora), pale rusty brown to pinkish brown (Miller)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4-6 x 3-4 microns, elliptic, smooth^, (Arora), spores 3.5-4.5 x 2.5-3 microns, oval to tear-shaped, smooth, IKI-, colorless; basidia 4-spored, 15-20 x 5-6 microns, clavate; cystidia absent from hymenium but "cylindric, thin-walled cystidial elements present in dissepiment edges", 6-7 microns wide and up to 60 microns long; hyphal system monomitic; trama hyphae 2-5 microns wide, "agglutinated and difficult to separate in sections from dried specimens", with rare branching, colorless, thin-walled, simple-septate; context hyphae thin-walled, with simple septa and clamped septa, rarely branched, mostly 4-10 microns wide, but with inflated parts up to 20 microns wide; gloeoplerous hyphae also present in the context, (Gilbertson) 
NAME ORIGIN  means "liver-colored, dark reddish brown", (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Arora(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(1)*, Miller(14)*, Bessette(2)*, Courtecuisse(1)*, McKnight(1)*, AroraPocket*, Bacon(1)*, Buczacki(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)*
