FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fomes fomentarius  (L.: Fr.) J.J. Kickx^  Flore Crypt. Flandres 2: 237. 1867;  Polyporus fomentarius  L.: Fr.;  Ungulina fomentaria  (L.: Fr.) Pat.
ENGLISH NAMES  tinder polypore, tinderconk, tinder bracket, horse's hoof fungus, hoof fungus, tinder fungus, amadou
NOTES  Features include 1) hoof-like growth on hardwood, 2) a woody, gray to gray-brown or gray-black cap with faint horizontal semicircular markings and a light brown margin, 3) light brown flesh, 4) a white-marbled mycelial core at the place of attachment, 5) a brown pore surface with fine circular pores, and 6) cylindric spores.^ In Europe before matches were invented, this fungus was ground to a powder and used in tinderboxes. Pieces of Fomes fomentarius context at one time were soaked in saltpeter solution until saturated, then dried carefully to form tinder, which a spark would ignite (Ginns(28)). It has also been used for cautery of wounds. RANGE Fomes fomentarius has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NB, NF, NS, NWT, ON, PE, PQ, SK, AK, CT, IA, MA, ME, MI, MN, MT, NC, ND, NH, NY, OH, PA, SD, TN, VA, VT, WI, and WV, (Gilbertson), TX (Lincoff(2)), and also Europe, Asia, Africa, (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  KOH on a small piece of the fruit body from the upper surface gives dark blood red reaction (Schwarze)
CAP  up to 15cm across, hoof-shaped, tough, woody, gray to gray-brown or gray-black with dark zones, light brown near margin; concentrically grooved, minutely velvety with hard smooth crust^, (Phillips), up to 15cm wide, hoof-shaped, tough, woody, "upper surface of pileus quickly developing a hard glabrous crust, older part gray, zonate and shallowly sulcate, marginal part light brown, also zonate, minutely tomentose", (Gilbertson), margin cream-colored, hazel, or light brown (Lincoff(1)), hoof-shaped, 10-25(30)cm across and equally high, projecting 5-20cm, ocher to red-brown when young, later light to dark gray; with concentric waves and grooves, smooth, with hard bald dark brown crust 0.1-0.2cm thick in cross-section, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  up to 1cm thick, tough, fibrous; yellowish brown^, (Phillips), up to 1cm thick, tough-fibrous, azonate, "granular core of varying size developing at upper part of the context next to the substrate, mottled with a mixture of pale and darker areas", (Gilbertson), corky, tough, fibrous, concentrically zoned; light brown; with soft white-marbled mycelial core at place of attachment, (Breitenbach)
PORES  4-5 per mm, circular; pale brown, darkening when handled; tube layer 0.5-6cm thick, very thick, not distinctly layered; light brown becoming stuffed with white mycelium^, (Phillips), 4-5 per mm, with thick tomentose walls, surface concave; pale brown; tube layers indistinctly stratified, making up most of the interior tissue of the fruitbody, "light brown and becoming stuffed with white mycelium", (Gilbertson), 3-4 per mm, rounded, cream then light ocher to brownish, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  during its growth it develops a marked banana-like odor (Lincoff(1)), pleasantly fungoid (Breitenbach), none (Miller)
TASTE  rather bitter^ (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  no (Phillips)
HABITAT  on dead or living hardwood trees, including birch, beech, maple and poplar^, (Phillips), perennial, on living and dead hardwoods, especially Betula (birch) and Alnus (alder), causing white mottled heartrot of living hardwoods, continuing to fruit on dead and fallen trees, (Gilbertson), on dead hardwoods or wounds in living trees, including maple, birch, beech, hickory, poplar, and cherry, (Lincoff(2)), fruiting all season (Miller), spores produced in fall (Bacon)
SPORE DEPOSIT  lemon-yellow (Phillips), white (Lincoff(2))^, light yellow (Miller)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 12-18(20) x 4-7 microns, cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 23-25 x 7-9 microns, with swollen base, with basal clamp; cystidioles 24-37 x 3.5-7.5 microns, thin-walled, fusoid, with basal clamp, also cystidia-like elements near dissepiment edges, up to 120 microns long, 3-5 microns wide, projecting up to 55 microns, some lightly incrusted; hyphal system trimitic, context generative hyphae inconspicuous, 2-4 microns wide, thin-walled, nodose-septate, context skeletal hyphae 3-8 microns wide, thick-walled, pale yellowish brown in KOH, aseptate, context binding hyphae 1.5-3 microns wide, thick-walled, much-branched, aseptate, "granular core a mixture of binding hyphae, narrow skeletal hyphae, and irregularly shaped, thick-walled sclerids that are brown in KOH, tramal hyphae similar, except for sclerids", (Gilbertson), spores 18.5-19 x 5.5-6 microns, elliptic-cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin, "producing tinder for fire", from its use, (Lincoff(1)), "fomentarius" points to its use in medicine: Hippocrates wrote about its use in cauterizing wounds to stop bleeding (with a smoldering conk), (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
SIMILAR  Phellinus igniarius similar in color but may be shelf-like, old caps often black and cracked, has often stratified tube layers that are often stuffed when old with white mycelial threads showing as streaks or flecks when cut open, and spores are 5-7 x 4-6 microns long, (Arora), Phellinus igniarius when very young and (rarely) older may have a smooth gray cap surface resembling F. fomentarius, but the context is dark brown with white streaks, spores are 5.0-6.5 x 4.5-6 microns, and setae are present, (Ginns(28)), Phellinus igniarius has hard dark brown flesh, (Breitenbach), Phellinus igniarius has different reaction to KOH; Phellinus species are more like cork than wood and usually have setae (Lincoff(2)); Fomitopsis species have whitish flesh (Lincoff(2)), Fomitopsis mounceae will melt if a lit match is held to the cap surface, whereas Fomes fomentarius (and Fomitopsis ochracea) will char.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Ginns(28)*, Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Lincoff(1)*, Miller(14)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)*, Courtecuisse(1)*, Breitenbach(2)*, Sept(1)*, Arora(1), Bacon(1)*, Buczacki(1)*, Schwarze(1)

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fomitiporia punctata  (Fr.) Murrill^  Lloydia 10: 254. 1947;  Phellinus punctatus  (Fr.) Pilat  Atl. Champ. Europe 3: 530. 1942;  Polyporus punctatus  Fr.;  Poria punctata  (Fr.) P. Karst.
ENGLISH NAMES  elbowpatch crust
NOTES  Features include flat growth on hardwood, a perennial yellowish brown to grayish brown pore surface that is woody and not readily separable, small circular pores, and microscopic characters including dextrinoid spores.^ RANGE Gilbertson listed Fomitiporia punctata from 44 states and provinces in North America, including British Columbia, Washington, Oregon and Idaho, but described it as fruiting on hardwoods and conifers. Ginns(28) says that reports on conifers have been has been segregated under Fomitiporia tsugina, so that the range is uncertain but says that Fomitiporia punctata has occurred on the hardwood ornamental Juglans sieboldiana in BC. 
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, becoming widely spread out, woody, adnate; margin up to 2cm wide and yellowish brown, tomentose, "receding, becoming black and rimose"^, (Ginns), growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, becoming widely spread out; woody, not readily separable; margin at first up to 2cm wide, yellowish brown, tomentose, receding, becoming black and cracked when older, (Gilbertson), up to 20cm long and 0.5-2.5cm thick, crustaceous, (Lincoff), forming patches several centimeters to decimeters across, growing flat on wood, up to 2cm thick, attached tightly, woody, hard; margin sterile, thin and distinctly bounded, whitish, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  up to 0.2cm thick, "golden brown, becoming dark reddish brown"^, (Ginns), up to 0.2cm thick, "golden brown to dark reddish brown in older specimens", (Gilbertson)
PORES  6-8 per millimeter, round, "edges thick, entire, minutely tomentose", pore surface yellowish to grayish brown, dull, smooth; tubes golden brown, each layer up to 0.6cm thick, (Ginns), 6-8 per mm, circular, walls thick and minutely tomentose, yellowish brown to grayish brown; dull, smooth; tube layers concolorous, single layers up to 0.6cm thick, (Gilbertson), cinnamon-colored or tobacco-colored with a grayish hazel pruinescence, tubes up to 0.7cm long, (Lincoff), 5-7 per mm, rounded to elongated, dull tobacco brown to chestnut brown; tube layer 0.1-0.3cm thick (tubes "1-3 mm long"), "distinctly layered in fruiting bodies several years old", (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  none in particular^ (Lincoff)
TASTE  none in particular^ (Lincoff)
EDIBILITY  no (Lincoff)
HABITAT  perennial on hardwoods, causing a uniform white rot^, (Ginns), perennial, on dead hardwoods and conifers, also on living trees, associated with uniform white rot of dead wood of hardwoods and conifers, (Gilbertson), "on all types of broadleaf wood or bushy shrubs, sometimes parasitic on vines", (Lincoff), all year (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6.5-8.5 x 5.5-7.0 microns, nearly round to broadly elliptic, some dextrinoid, walls colorless, slightly thickened^; cystidioles about 26 x 6 microns, "clavate with an attenuated finger-like apex"; generative hyphae 2.5-5.0 microns wide, walls colorless, thin to moderately thick, skeletal hyphae 2.5-5.0 microns wide, "walls dark brown, thin to thick", (Ginns), spores 6.5-8.5 x 5.5-7 microns, broadly oval to nearly round, smooth, dextrinoid, colorless; basidia 4-spored, 11-12.5 x 7-8.5 microns, broadly clavate, simple-septate at base; setae absent; thin-walled ventricose cystidioid elements present; hyphae of context of 2 types, mostly the first: 1) 2.5-5 microns wide, dark brown in KOH, thin-walled to thick-walled, rarely branched, occasionally septate, 2) almost colorless, thinner-walled; hyphae of trama similar, (Gilbertson), spores 7-8(9) x 6-7.5 microns, elliptic to nearly round, smooth, dextrinoid, some thick-walled, with droplets or granular contents; setae absent, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin meaning "spotted" or "dotted"^, (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  See also SIMILAR section of Fuscoporia ferruginosa.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Phellinus punctatus), Ginns(28), Lincoff(1)* (as Phellinus punctatus), Breitenbach(2)* (as Phellinus punctatus), Buczacki(1)* (as Phellinus punctatus)

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota

ENGLISH NAMES

Gilb. but also gave Fomitiporia repanda (Overh.) Ginns as an independent entry with the same basionym. fairly common in the Olympic Peninsula of Washington, (Gilbertson). FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fomitiporia robusta  (P. Karst.) Fiasson & Niemela^  Karstenia 24(1): 25. 1984;  Phellinus robustus  (P. Karst.) Bourdot & Galzin  Hymenomycetes de France p.616. 1928
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) a perennial fruitbody that is hoof-shaped to flat-projecting or effused-reflexed, 2) cap surface that is brown to blackish, with the rounded margin sometimes yellowish brown, 3) small circular pores that are yellowish brown or grayish brown, 4) growth on oak or other hardwoods, and 5) microscopic characters including ventricose cystidia with elongated slender apices (found also in other members of a Phellinus robustus complex).^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where noted. RANGE In western North America it is found in BC, and it is also known from CA and NM. Reports on conifers in Alaska and BC may have been based on misidentification (Ginns(28)). It occurs in CA, LA, NM, and TX, (Gilbertson(1)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 12cm x 20cm x 11cm, ungulate [hoof-shaped] to applanate [flat]; sessile or effused-reflexed [lying flat on wood with pores outward but bending out to form a cap]; brown to blackish; becoming incrusted and rimose [cracked], usually sulcate [grooved], bald; "margin concolorous or yellowish brown at first, then faintly tomentose, rounded, fertile below"
FLESH  up to 3cm thick, hard, woody; shining yellowish brown, zonate, core with white radial streaks near point of attachment
PORES  7-9 per millimeter, circular, with thick, entire dissepiments; yellowish brown or grayish brown; tube layers "distinct, light brownish, becoming whitish within, woody", each layer up to 0.3cm thick
STEM  sessile (without stem)
ODOR  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
TASTE  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, most common on Quercus (oak) in southern U.S. (but also on other hardwood genera), near the base of the living trees^, (Gilbertson), all year (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6-8.5 x 5.5-7 microns, nearly round, smooth, dextrinoid, colorless, thick-walled when mature^; basidia 4-spored, 11-12 x 8-9.5 microns, broadly ellipsoid, no clamp connection; cystidia usually abundant in hymenium, ventricose with narrow tapered apex (narrow apical part 1.5-2 microns wide), colorless, thin-walled; setae lacking or occasional in some specimens, 18-37 x 5-8 microns, subulate [awl-shaped] to ventricose, thin-walled to thick-walled; hyphae of context of 2 types: 1) 2.5-4 microns wide, brown in KOH, "thick-walled, rarely branched, rarely simple-septate", 2) inconspicuous, 2.5-5 microns wide, colorless, "thin-walled, simple-septate"; hyphae of trama similar
NAME ORIGIN  means 'hard and strong like oak' or perhaps referring to habitat on oak
SIMILAR  See also Fomitiporia tsugina. See also SIMILAR section of Phellinus prunicola.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Phellinus robustus), Ginns(28)*, Buczacki(1)* (as Phellinus robustus)

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fomitiporia tsugina  Murrill^  N. Amer. Fl. (New York) 9(1): 9. 1907;  Fomitiporia hartigii  (Allesch. & Schnabl) Fiasson & Niemela, sensu N. Am. authors  Karstenia 24(1): 25. 1984
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include fruitbodies that grow flat on wood with the pore surface exposed, or bent back to form pale a yellowish brown shelf-like cap, small grayish brown to purplish brown pores, growth on conifers, and microscopic characters including nearly round dextrinoid spores, ventricose cystidioid elements and absent setae.^ It has been a member of Phellinus robustus complex according to Gilbertson(1). RANGE. Fomitiporia tsugina has been found (as Phellinus hartigii) in BC, WA, OR, ID, AK, AZ, CA, CO, MN, MT, NM, NY, Europe, Asia, but see also SIMILAR, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  growing flat on wood or bent outward from flat pore surface to form shelf-like cap, when shelf-like with upper and lower surfaces at about 45 degrees; pale yellowish brown; tomentose near margin, becoming bald and cracked^, (Gilbertson)
FLESH  up to 2cm thick, woody; yellowish brown to reddish brown, with darker zones^, (Gilbertson)
PORES  5-7 per mm, circular, thick-walled, grayish brown to purplish brown; tube layers distinctly stratified, layers up to 0.5cm thick, "often with sterile tissue between the layers, tubes white within"^, (Gilbertson)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, on living and dead conifers, usually fruiting on the underside of branches and in branch crotches, most common on Tsuga (hemlock), associated with a uniform white rot of heartwood of living conifers, continuing in dead trees and logs^, (Gilbertson)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6-7.5 x 5-6.5 microns, round to nearly round, smooth, strongly dextrinoid, colorless, slightly thick-walled^; basidia 4-spored, 12-14 x 8-9 microns, broadly ellipsoid, simple-septate at base; setae lacking; ventricose thin-walled cystidioid elements present; hyphae of context of two types, mostly the first: 1) 2-5 microns wide, "pale yellowish brown in Melzer's reagent, thick-walled, aseptate or with rare septa", 2) 1-1.5 microns wide, nearly colorless, aseptate, thick-walled, much-branched; hyphae of trama similar, (Gilbertson)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'pertaining to Tsuga [hemlock]'
SIMILAR  Fomitiporia robusta grows on hardwoods but has been regarded in the past as the same species as Fomitiporia tsugina (as Phellinus hartigii), leading to uncertainty in distribution, (Gilbertson).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Phellinus hartigii). Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fomitopsis cajanderi  (P. Karst.) Kotl. et Pouzar^  Ceska Mykol. 11: 157. 1957;  Fomes cajanderi  P. Karst.;  Trametes subrosea  Weir;  Fomes subroseus  (Weir) Overh.;  Rhodofomes cajanderi (P. Karst.) B.K. Cui, M.L. Han & Y.C. Dai, in Han, Chen, Shen, Song, Vlasak, Dai & Cui, Fungal Diversity 80: 364. 2016 (proposed new name)
ENGLISH NAMES  rosy polypore
NOTES  Fomitopsis cajanderi is shelf-like or bracket-like with the upper surface pinkish becoming brownish to blackish, fine pores that are pinkish to pinkish brown, and curved cylindric spores.^ RANGE It is found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NB, NS, ON, PQ, AL, AR, AZ, CA, CO, CT, FL, GA, IA, IN, KY, MA, MD, ME, MI, MN, MS, MT, NC, ND, NE, NH, NJ, NM, NV, NY, OH, PA, SD, TN, UT, VA, VT, WI, WV, and WY, and it extends into subtropical regions in Central America and the Caribbean area, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  2.5-10(13)cm broad, 0.3-2cm thick, "shelflike or bracketlike to somewhat hoof-shaped, tough in age", margin usually thin and acute, often perennial; "pinkish-red to pinkish-brown becoming brown to grayish-brown and in old age blackish except for the margin"; surface covered with hairs becoming nearly bald when old but often roughened, not incrusted, often zoned or grooved concentrically^, (Arora), up to 20cm x 7cm x 10cm, without a stem, to bent back from a flat pore surface, or occasionally flat on wood substrate, single or imbricate [shingled]; upper surface "light vinaceous" to "vinaceous fawn" (Ridgway colors) when young, darkening to pinkish brown or gray to blackish when old; tomentose to fibrillose or bald, (Gilbertson)
FLESH  rather soft when young but corky when old; "rosy-pink to reddish-brown or pinkish-brown"^, (Arora), up to 1cm thick, corky, not zoned; light pinkish brown, (Gilbertson)
PORES  3-5 per mm, rosy to pinkish-red or pinkish-brown when young, often duller and darker (reddish brown) when old; each tube layer 0.1-0.3cm thick, but not distinctly stratified^, (Arora), 4-5 per mm, circular to angular, with thick entire walls; rose-colored; tube layers up to 2cm thick, stratified, paler than the flesh, (Gilbertson)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, on dead conifers, but also on madrone and various fruit trees^, (Arora), on dead wood of numerous genera of conifers, particularly common on Douglas fir, rarely on hardwoods including Populus tremuloides, Betula, Prunus, causes a brown cubical rot of dead conifers, rarely of hardwoods, plays a major role in carbon and nutrient recycling, (Gilbertson)
SPORE DEPOSIT  whitish^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4-8 x 1.5-2.5 microns, cylindric but slightly curved, smooth^, (Arora), spores 5-7 x 1.5-2 microns, cylindric, distinctly curved, smooth, inamyloid, colorless; basidia 4-spored, 14-23 x 4-6 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidioles not projecting, 15-18 x 3.5-5 microns, fusoid, thin-walled, with basal clamp; hyphal system dimitic, context generative hyphae 2-4 microns wide, thin-walled, colorless, with clamp connections, context skeletal hyphae 2.5-6 microns wide, thick-walled. pale brown in KOH, rarely branched, non-septate, (Gilbertson)
NAME ORIGIN  named for A.K. Cajander^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Fomitopsis rosea is generally hoof-shaped and bracket-like as opposed to shelf-like to applanate for F. cajanderi, and F. rosea has straight spores 2-2.5 microns wide, (Gilbertson). F. rosea differs in its typically hoof-like and sessile fruitbodies, a preference for high elevation spruce forests, and straight, slightly wider spores (2.0-3.5 microns wide), (Ginns). "F. rosea is practically identical, but has paler (silvery-pink to pale rose) flesh, slightly broader, cylindric (not curved) spores, and a cap surface that is sometimes incrusted slightly in older specimens", (Arora).

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fomitopsis mounceae  J.-E. Haight & Nakasone  Mycologia in press;  Fomitopsis pinicola (misapplied name)
ENGLISH NAMES  red-belted polypore, redbelt, red-belt conk, red-belted bracket
NOTES  Fomitopsis mounceae has shelf-like  to hoof-shaped, hard fruiting bodies with a reddish or partially reddish upper surface (often shiny, resinous, or tacky, sometimes looking varnished). The margin is more lightly colored and rounded. The pallid pores do not bruise brown. The upper surface may be entirely blackish in old fruitbodies. This is the commoner of the two species in the Fomitopsis pinicola complex in the Pacific Northwest, the other being Fomitopsis ochracea.^ |The Fomitopsis pinicola complex is the most common conk in the Pacific Northwest. Haight(1) and Haight(2) found 4 species in the Fomitopsis pinicola complex: one European (Fomitopsis pinicola), one southwest North American (Fomitopsis schrenkii), and the two previously mentioned species both widespread in North America. |In the Pacific Northwest, collections can be reported as Fomitopsis pinicola complex, Fomitopsis mounceae, or Fomitopsis ochracea, depending on whether differentiating features between the latter two species are found. If fruitbodies have a red belt they are Fomitopsis mounceae, but if it is lacking they may be either species. The application of a lit match to the colored tissue near the edge of a fruitbody is said to cause melting in F. mounceae and charring in F. ochracea. A receding pore surface suggests F. ochracea but is not always present. The spores of F. mounceae average longer but this is s difficult criterion to use in practice. |The first description given here is derived from the original description of the species (Haight(1)). The Arora(1) and Gilbertson(1) descriptions are retained for further detail (such as a larger size range) but both describe the Fomitopsis pinicola complex as a whole. |Haight(1) note that the microscopic illustration of F. pinicola in Gilbertson(1) fig. 133 is that of F. mounceae (except that the large cystidia there were not observed in the collections they studied (appearing instead "to be young, developing skeletal hyphae observed in dissepiments"), and the spore measurement given is more similar to F. pinicola. |RANGE In the range map (or the collections examined) of Haight(2), Fomitopsis mounciae is found in BC, WA, OR, ID, and also AB, NL, YT, AK, CA, MT, ME, MI, MN, NH, NY, TN, UT, VT, WI, and WY. But species in the Fomitopsis pinicola complex are more widespread in North America (see Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  flesh stains reddish to dark reddish-brown in KOH^ (Arora)
CAP  woody, sometimes umbonate, occasionally imbricate, typically applanate, 2.5-16(17)cm across, 2.2-8(15)cm deep and 2.5-9cm thick, rarely triquetrous, (4.2)8-16cm x (2.2)5-9cm x (2.0)3.5-9.5cm, rarely ungulate [hoof-shaped]; colors varied but usually with a distinct, shiny, red or reddish brown, laccate [varnished] band near the margin, when young at base black then brownish orange, reddish brown, "kaiser brown", or "Hay's brown", margin pale orange or grayish orange, when older the base and proximal surface black to brownish orange, yellowish brown, grayish brown, brown or greenish gray, at mid-radius grayish brown, brownish orange, reddish brown, "wood brown", "buffy brown", or "russet", sometimes with a narrow reddish brown band next to the margin, the margin itself narrow to wide, yellowish white, orange white, pale orange, or grayish orange; the cap surface bald, "often with a sticky resinous coating, smooth to uneven, sulcate", in older specimens the upper surface "uneven often with rounded warts or bumps"; the margin rounded and smooth^, (Haight), |5-40(75)cm broad, 3-22cm thick, knob-like at first, becoming hoof-shaped, shelf-like, or bracket-like, in outline fan-shaped to semicircular, hard and woody when old, growing margin rounded; starting as whitish, pale yellow or lilac-tinged knob, when mature "usually at least partially reddish to dark red, but sometimes brown and often rusty or blackish-brown toward the base and brightly colored (white, yellow, ochraceous, or reddish) at the margin"; developing a thin hard resinous crust that is sometimes slightly varnished, when old concentrically furrowed and/or zoned, (Arora for Fomitopsis pinicola complex), |up to 38cm along wood, projecting 20cm, 15cm thick, usually without stem, rarely bent back from flat pore surface to form a cap, or entirely flat, applanate (flattened horizontally) to hoof-shaped; upper surface at first with a sticky reddish brown resinous layer that often persists over younger marginal areas, becoming grayish to brown or blackish; becoming bald, sometimes appearing varnished, smooth to grooved, (Gilbertson for Fomitopsis pinicola complex)
FLESH  "woody, dense, azonate", brown^, (Haight), "corky or woody, very tough, white to pinkish-buff or yellow when young, pale brownish or straw-colored in old age; usually bruising pinkish when actively growing ", (Arora for Fomitopsis pinicola complex), up to 12cm thick, corky to woody; cream to buff, not zoned or zoned, (Gilbertson for Fomitopsis pinicola complex)
PORES  3-5(6) per mm, circular, pore surface not receding or contracting, yellowish white, pale yellow, grayish yellow, grayish orange, pale orange, orange white, or "light ochraceous buff"; tube layers at first distinctly stratified and colored as the pore surface, older layers indistinct, becoming light brown^, (Haight), 3-5 per mm, white or pale yellow becoming brownish when old, "not turning brown when scratched but sometimes bruising yellow or pinkish-lilac"; tube layers distinctly stratified, each layer 0.2-0.8cm thick, (Arora for Fomitopsis pinicola complex), 5-6 per mm, circular, cream, with thick entire walls; tube layers up to 6cm thick, indistinctly stratified, sometimes separated by thin flesh layer, colored as flesh, (Gilbertson for Fomitopsis pinicola complex)
STEM  without a stem^ (Haight)
ODOR  rather strong and fungal when fresh^ (Arora for Fomitopsis pinicola complex), citrine-like when fresh and growing (Miller for Fomitopsis pinicola complex)
TASTE  none (Miller for Fomitopsis pinicola complex), bitter (Breitenbach for Fomitopsis pinicola in Europe)
EDIBILITY  no (Arora for Fomitopsis pinicola complex)
HABITAT  perennial, mainly on conifers and aspen, producing a brown cubical rot of dead hardwoods, especially aspen, and conifers^, (Haight), perennial, "solitary or in groups on dead trees, logs, and stumps or rarely on living trees", (Arora for Fomitopsis pinicola complex), on "dead conifers and occasionally causing heartrot of living conifers, a major heartrot fungus in black cherry", "also occasionally on aspen and birch", causes brown cubical rot of living and dead conifers and hardwoods, conspicuous "white mycelial felts develop in shrinkage cracks of the decayed wood", (Gilbertson for Fomitopsis pinicola complex), buttons forming in spring, but conks present all year round, (Miller for Fomitopsis pinicola complex), spores produced in fall (Bacon for  for Fomitopsis pinicola in Europe)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white or pale yellowish^ (Arora for Fomitopsis pinicola complex), light yellow (Miller for Fomitopsis pinicola complex)
MICROSCOPIC  spores (5.5)6-7(8) x (3)3.5-4(4.5) microns, elliptic to cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, walls colorless, thin to slightly thickened^; |basidia 4-spored, 14-25 x 6-8 microns, basidia clamped at base, walls colorless; |cystidia scarce to numerous, 16-35(40) x 3-6.5 microns, obclavate to subfusiform with subacute or rounded apices, enclosed or protruding up to 25 microns, walls colorless, thin, and smooth; |hyphal system dimitic with 1) clamped generative hyphae 1.5-3.5(4.5) microns wide, sparingly to moderately branched, walls colorless, thin to slightly thickened, smooth, then becoming sclerified, up to 7 microns wide, with walls colorless becoming dark yellow, thick with lumen lacking, "dominant in pore trama and mycelial stuffed pores, scattered to numerous in context", and 2) skeletal hyphae (4.5)5-9(10) microns wide, "aseptate, sometimes with adventitious septa, rarely branched, even, straight", walls colorless, thin at first, then brownish yellow, up to 4.5 microns thick, smooth, "dominant in context, dissepiments, and pore trama", (Haight), |spores 5-8 x 3.5-5 microns, cylindric to elliptic, smooth, (Arora for Fomitopsis pinicola complex), |spores 6-9 x 3.5-4.5 microns, cylindric-elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless; |basidia 4-spored, 17-24 x 7-8.5 microns, short-clavate, with basal clamp; |cystidia up to 150 microns long, 3-10 microns wide, projecting up to 90 microns, "hyphoid, often thick-walled at the base, thin-walled toward the apex", narrowed at tip or not; |hyphal system "trimitic, contextual generative hyphae thin-walled, with clamps, 2-5 microns in diam, contextual skeletal hyphae thick-walled, hyaline, [line appears to have been left out here ending description of skeletal hyphae and starting description of binding hyphae] thick-walled, nonseptate, much branched, 1.5-4 microns in diam, tramal hyphae similar", (Gilbertson for Fomitopsis pinicola complex, and note that Haight said these spore measurements are more like Fomitopsis pinicola of Europe)
NAME ORIGIN  named for Irene Mounce (1894-1987), "a pioneering Canadian mycologist, for her contributions to mycology and  Fomitopsis (fide Ginns 1988)" (Haight(1) with Latin name italicized)
SIMILAR  Fomitopsis ochracea has an ocher band or no band (instead of red, reddish brown, orange, or yellow), the band chars instead of melting in a flame, the underside is sometimes receding, and the spores are shorter on average. |F. ochracea, according to Voitk(2) is 'Marginally smaller than F. pinicola, it can also grow quite big, making size an unhelpful differentiating character. The top is similarly black to gray and only moderately zonate. There is no red band. If there is some coloured tissue near the edge, it does not melt, but chars when exposed to a flame. The edge may be ochre in colour, giving it the name, but it may also be quite drab and gray. The undersurface is light ochre to yellow, often staining darker yellow on handling. The pores are small and round, about 4-6/mm. The cut surface has the characteristic Fomitopsis whorls in the upper body and successive pore layers in the lower. It has a distinctive "sour" smell. During active growth the pore surface, and at times the entire conk, may exhibit dramatic guttation. Its commonest hosts are balsam fir and spruce, but proportionately it is more common on birch than F. pinicola. The original find was on poplar in Alberta. It, also, is quite variable in appearance and can present a definite puzzle for the unwary identifier. Absence of a red laccate band should usually make the determination. If still puzzled, looking at the spores with a microscope should help. The spores of F. ochracea are subglobose (almost round - about like a blown up balloon), while those of F. pinicola are more elliptical' (5.0-6.5 x 3.5-6.0 microns for F. ochracea, 5-8 x 3-4.5 microns for F. pinicola). Voitk(2). But Haight(2) give average spore sizes of 5.1-5.9 x 3.6-4(4.4) microns for  F. ochracea and 5.8-6.6(7.1) x (3.1)3.4-4.1 microns for F. mounceae. |Species of Fomitopsis not in the F. pinicola complex lack the glossy resinous crust or red marginal belt, (Lincoff(2)). |Fomes fomentarius [which also lacks the red marginal belt] will char if a match is held to the cap surface, whereas Fomitopsis mounceae will melt. |Phellinus species have brown flesh (Breitenbach). |Ganoderma species are less dense, often more conspicuously varnished, and regrow annually. |Heterobasidion 'annosum' when shelf-like 1) "is usually thinner and rougher (knobby, pitted, grooved, etc.)" than F. pinicola, 2) the upper surface is usually brown to grayish-brown with a pallid margin, but is sometimes reddish-brown, 3) its flesh does not redden in KOH, and 4) it usually grows at the base of the trunk or from its roots, (Arora for Fomitopsis pinicola complex). Spores of H. 'annosum' are nearly round to oval, and finely spiny (although this not always evident under light microscope). |See also SIMILAR section of Ganoderma applanatum, Ganoderma lucidum, and Ganoderma oregonense.
SOURCES  Haight(2)* (colors individually in double quotation marks from Ridgway(1)), Gilbertson(1) (as Fomitopsis pinicola), Arora(1)* (as Fomitopsis pinicola), Phillips(1)* (as Fomitopsis pinicola), Lincoff(2)* (as Fomitopsis pinicola), Miller(14)* (as Fomitopsis pinicola), McKnight(1)* (as Fomitopsis pinicola), Trudell(4)* (as Fomitopsis pinicola), Sept(1)* (as Fomitopsis pinicola), AroraPocket* (as Fomitopsis pinicola), Voitk(2) (as Fomitopsis pinicola), Desjardin(6)* (as Fomitopsis pinicola), Ginns(28)* (as Fomitopsis pinicola), Haight(2) (concerning the Fomitopsis pinicola complex), Siegel(2)* (as Fomitopsis pinicola group), Breitenbach(2), Bacon(1)

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fomitopsis ochracea  Ryvarden & Stokland^  Syn. Fung. (Oslo) 25: 46. 2008;  Fomitopsis pinicola  (misapplied name)
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) large applanate to hoof-like or rarely reflexed brackets growing perennially on logs and dead standing trunks, 2) a cap surface that ranges from grayish or brownish to ochraceous, orangish or whitish, often with a pale ochraceous band near the margin, the surface smooth but concentrically grooved, 3) a charring reaction when colored parts of the cap surface near the margin are heated with a flaming match, rather than boiling reaction that occurs in Fomitopsis mounceae, 4) small, round pores in a cream to pale tan surface that does not stain when bruised, and is sometimes retracted, and 5) microscopic characters including shorter spores than Fomitopsis mounceae.^ RANGE Collections have been mislabeled Fomitopsis pinicola (Ginns). The range map, distribution, and specimens examined in Haight(2) indicate a range that includes BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, ON, NS, NL, YT, AK, GA, ME, MI, MN, NC, NH, NY, TN, UT, VT, WI, and WY.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  woody, "usually sessile, occasionally imbricate", usually applanate, 4.0-23cm x 2.5-13(30)cm x 2.5-13cm, sometimes ungulate [hoof-shaped], 6.5-10cm x 4.0-6.5cm x 4.5-11cm, rarely triquetrous, 3.5-6.0cm x 2.5-3.0cm x 3.0-4.5cm; when young with more or less evenly pigmented bands, at base orange white or grayish orange, then further out darkening to pale orange, grayish orange or brown, then lightening before the margin to orange white, and finally pale orange at the margin, when older in even or mottled shades of black, brown and beige - base and nearby cap black, grayish orange, light brown, brown, grayish brown, or brownish gray, then at mid-radius often orange, grayish orange, grayish brown, brownish gray, light brown, brown, reddish brown, or nearly black, somes with scattered black spots or irregular brown lines, finally at the margin dull yellowish white, pale yellow, orange white, pale orange, light orange, grayish orange, or brownish orange, rarely brown; bald, "smooth to uneven, coarsely undulate or somewhat radially plicate, sulcate, sometimes rimose", margin rounded and smooth^, (Haight), up to 30cm along the substrate, projecting up to 20cm, and up to 7cm thick, "applanate to hoof-like, sessile or rarely reflexed", margin rounded; cap surface "varying from near white or ochreous to dark brownish grey and black, not boiling when heated with a flaming match"; bald, "smooth, sulcate", (Ginns)
FLESH  woody, azonate^, (Haight), up to 4cm thick, woody, azonate, cream to buff, (Ginns)
PORES  (3)4-5(6) per mm, circular, pore surface sometimes receding, yellowish white, pale yellow, orange white, pale orange, grayish orange or "light ochraceous buff"; tube layers distinctly stratified, colored as pore surface, older layers becoming indistinct, darker^, (Haight), pores 5-6 per mm, round, mouths even, pore surface "cream to pale tan, not staining when bruised"; tubes 0.05-0.3cm deep, (Ginns)
STEM  usually sessile^ (Haight)
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, producing a brown cubical rot of dead hardwoods and conifers, occasionally on living Picea sitchensis (Sitka Spruce)^, (Haight), perennial, on "logs and dead, standing trunks of hardwoods and conifers, causing a brown cubical rot", (Ginns)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores (4)4.5-6.5(7) x (3)3.5-4.5(5) microns, broadly elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, walls colorless, walls thin to slightly thickened^; basidia 4-spored, (10)15-25 x (5.5)7-9 microns, clavate, subclavate, or short-cylindric, clamped at base, basidial walls thin, smooth, and colorless; cystidia scarce to numerous, (14)20-4[sic](60) x (3)4-6.5 microns, "subfusiform with subacute, acuminate, or rounded apex, rarely obclavate to cylindrical with obtuse or slightly capitate apex", clamped at base, protruding up to 40 microns, walls thin, smooth, and colorless; hyphal system dimitic with clamped generative and skeletal hyphae - 1) generative hyphae 2-4.5(5.5) microns wide, "clamped, sparingly to moderately branched", walls colorless, "thin to slightly thickened, smooth", "observed in context, pore trama, and hymenium", often becoming sclerified with walls colorless, up to 2 microns thick, irregular or strangulated, "dominant in mycelial stuffed pores, numerous in context and pore trama, and 2) skeletal hyphae (4.5)5-9(10) microns wide, "aseptate, rarely branched, even, straight, lumen may contain resinous-like aggregations", walls colorless and "slightly thickened at first then dark yellow to brownish yellow", up to 3.5 microns thick, "wall thickness sometimes variable along hyphal length", smooth, "dominant in context, dissepiments, and pore trama",(Haight), spores 4-5 x 3-4 microns, broadly elliptic; hyphal system trimitic, generative hyphae 2-5 microns wide with clamp connections, skeletal hyphae 3-7 microns wide, walls colorless, binding hyphae rare, 1.5-4.0 microns wide, thick-walled or solid, (Ginns)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'ochraceous'
SIMILAR  Fomitopsis mounceae differs in having 1) reddish brown, red, orange, yellow, or ochraceous colors included in bands on the cap except when old, whereas F. ochracea has grey to brown or orangish colors in bands on the cap, often with an ochraceous band near the margin, 2) a cap crust near the margin "that boils when heated with a flaming match" as opposed to charring, and 3) a spore surface that is not retracted (sometimes retracted in F. ochracea), and 4) spores that average longer.
SOURCES  Ginns(28)*, Haight(2)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fomitopsis officinalis  (Vill.: Fr.) Bondartsev et Singer^  Ann. Mycol. 39: 55. 1941;  Polyporus officinalis  Vill.: Fr.;  Laricifomes officinalis  (Vill.) Kotl. & Pouzar;  Fomes laricis  Jacq. ex Murrill
ENGLISH NAMES  larch polypore, larch fungus, quinine conk, quinine fungus, enfant-du-pin, Agarikon
NOTES  Features include chalky white hoof-shaped to columnar fruitbodies on conifers, chalky white flesh, crumbly consistency, and extremely bitter taste.^ This is a source of agaricin (agaric acid) or 2-hydroxynonadecane-1,2,3-tricarboxylic acid, which has been used in the past to inhibit sweating, for instance the night sweats of the tuberculosis sufferer. The quinine-like bitter taste also caused people to falsely attribute anti-malarial properties to it. With Phaeolus schweinitzii and Porodaedalea pini it is said to be one of the three major destroyers of standing coniferous timber in the West of North America. RANGE Fomitopsis officinalis has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, ON, AK, AZ, CA, CO, MI, MT, NM, NV, SD, and WI, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  4-30cm wide and 5-40cm or more high, starting as a knob, becoming convex then hoof-shaped to cylindric, hard and tough when old; white to yellowish but aging grayish and sometimes with a greenish covering of algae; with a thin crust, often cracked or furrowed when old^, (Arora), up to 15cm x 23cm x 45cm, hoof-shaped to columnar, without stem, consistency becoming chalky, margin rounded; upper surface chalky white or discoloring to tan, not zoned, margin the same color; upper surface bald, often grooved, becoming cracked, (Gilbertson)
FLESH  thick, cheesy when young but chalky or crumbly when mature or old; white^, (Arora), up to 10cm thick, cheesy when fresh, chalky and crumbly when dry; chalky white, not zoned, (Gilbertson) 
PORES  3-4 per mm, white or whitish, discoloring when old or dry; tube layers each 0.3-2cm thick, often stratified^, (Arora), typically 4-5 per mm, but up to 1mm in some specimens, circular to angular, with thick entire walls that become torn when old; white to tan; usually rough; tube layers each up to 1cm thick, indistinctly stratified, colored as flesh or pale brownish, (Gilbertson)
STEM  none
ODOR  farinaceous^ (Arora)
TASTE  extremely bitter^ (Gilbertson)
EDIBILITY  mildly poisonous (Ginns(28))
HABITAT  perennial, single or several on living or dead conifers^, (Arora), single on living conifers, continuing decay and fruiting on dead trees and stumps, especially on old growth western larch and Douglas-fir, but also on Pinus (pine), Picea (spruce), Abies (fir), and Tsuga (hemlock), causing brown cubical heartrot of living conifers, commonly called brown trunk rot, thick white mycelial felts develop in shrinkage cracks, (Gilbertson)
SPORE DEPOSIT  whitish^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4-5.5 x 3-4 microns, broadly elliptic, smooth^, (Arora), spores 6-9 x 3-4 microns, cylindric-elliptic to short-cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless; basidia 4-spored, 20-25 x 6-8 microns, clavate, with basal clamp connection; cystidia none; hyphal system dimitic: context generative hyphae 2.5-7 microns wide, thin-walled, colorless, rarely branched, with clamp connections, lactiferous or gloeoplerous hyphae also abundant, up to 13 microns wide, thin-walled, "some with simple septa, often sinuous or branching, staining strongly in cotton blue", "sclerids also present in context, these contorted and irregularly shaped, often lobed, thick-walled, hyaline, walls up to 9 microns thick", trama generative and skeletal hyphae similar to those in context [context skeletal hyphae not described, but illustrated as thick-walled, nonseptate, and in one case branching], (Gilbertson) 
NAME ORIGIN  means "used in medicine"^ (Gilbertson), (as an official drug plant in pharmacopoeia)
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Arora(1), Lincoff(2)*, Breitenbach(2)* (as Laricifomes officinalis), Courtecuisse(1)* (as Laricifomes officinalis), Ginns(28)*, Siegel(2)* (as Laricifomes officinalis)

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fomitopsis rosea  (Alb. & Schwein.: Fr.) P. Karst.^  Krit. Finl. Basidsv. P. 306. 1889;  Polyporus roseus  Alb. & Schwein.: Fr.;  Rhodofomes roseus  (Alb. & Schwein.) Vlasak  Ceska Mykol. 44(4): 235. 1990 (proposed new name)
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Fomitopsis rosea is bracket-like to hoof-shaped with the upper surface pale rose-pink, darkening to brownish black, small pale rose pink to pinkish brown pores, a tough-woody consistency, growth on conifers or aspen, and cylindric spores.^ RANGE It has been recorded from BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, NB, NS, NV, ON, PE, AZ, CA, CO, IA, ME, MN, MT, NH, NM, NY, TN, UT, VT, WV, and WY, (Gilbertson), and also occurs in Europe and Asia, (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 12cm wide, without a stem, hoof-shaped, tough-woody; upper surface at first pale rose-pink, darkening to brownish black; at first tomentose, becoming bald and crustose when old, becoming cracked^, (Gilbertson), bracket-like, 2-10cm along wood, projecting 1-4cm, up to 3cm thick; pink-brown, gray-brown, to dark brown; weakly zoned, undulating, appressed-tomentose, with tubercular swellings; margin sharp when young, then blunt and wavy, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  up to 3cm thick, tough-woody or fibrous; pinkish brown, not zoned to faintly zoned^, (Gilbertson), corky, tough, woody toward outside, hard, indistinctly zoned; pink, (Breitenbach)
PORES  3-5 per mm, circular to angular, pale rose pink to pinkish brown; tube layers up to 2cm thick, stratified, sometimes separated by a thin layer of context tissue^, (Gilbertson), 3-4(5) per mm, rounded to elongated; pink; tube layer 0.5-0.7cm thick, tubes of perennial fruitbodies indistinctly layered; rudimentary caps often formed in tube layer, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  weakly fungoid^ (Breitenbach)
TASTE  somewhat bitter^ (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, mainly on conifers, especially Douglas-fir and spruce, also on quaking aspen logs at high elevations, causing brown cubical rot of dead conifers or rarely, hardwoods, reported to cause a decay called yellow-brown top rot in Douglas-fir^, (Gilbertson), single to gregarious on dead wood of Picea (spruce) with and without bark, commonly on construction wood, (Breitenbach)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5.5-7.5 x 2-2.5 microns, cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 15-18 x 5-6 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia none; hyphal system dimitic, context generative hyphae 2-3.5 microns wide, thin-walled, with clamp connections, context skeletal hyphae 4-6 microns wide, thick-walled, with rare branching, aseptate, trama hyphae similar, (Gilbertson), spores 6-7 x 2.5-3 microns, cylindric-elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, some with drops, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  means "red like a rose, ruddy"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Fomitopsis cajanderi is generally shelf-like to applanate rather than hoof-shaped and bracket-like for F. rosea, and F. cajanderi has curved spores 1.5-2 microns wide.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Breitenbach(2)*, Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fomitopsis spraguei  (Berk. & M.A. Curtis) Gilb. & Ryvarden^  Mycotaxon 22: 364. 1985;  Polyporus spraguei  Berk. & M.A. Curtis;  Niveoporofomes spraguei (Berk. & M.A. Curtis) B.K. Cui, M.L. Han & Y.C. Dai, Fungal Diversity 80: 360. 2016 (proposed new name)
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Fomitopsis spraguei is characterized by a tough, annual but persistent fruitbody with a white to ochraceous non-zoned upper surface, flesh of similar color, fine cream to pale brown pores, growth particularly on oak or chestnut, ovoid to broadly elliptic spores, and a trimitic hyphal system.^ It is rare in the Pacific Northwest. The description is derived from Gilbertson(1). RANGE Fomitopsis spraguei has been found in WA, OR, ON, AR, CT, GA, IA, IL, IN, LA, MA, MI, MO, NC, NE, NH, NJ, OH, NY, PA, TN, VA, VT, and WV, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 7.5cm x 9cm x 4cm, without a stem, or bent out from flat growth with pore surface exposed, single or shingled, semicircular, applanate (flattened horizontally), margin rounded to acute, "sterile or fertile below"; upper surface ivory white to ochraceous, not zoned, margin colored as surface; appressed strigose to bald, smooth or rugose (wrinkled) 
FLESH  up to 3.5cm thick, tough-corky; white to ochraceous, not zoned
PORES  3-6 per mm, circular to angular, "with thick, entire dissepiments that may become thin and slightly lacerate"; cream to buff or pale brown; smooth; tube layer up to 0.5cm thick, colored as flesh and continuous with it
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  slightly acidic
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on living trees and dead wood of various hardwoods, "particularly common on Quercus and Castanea", causes a brown cubical rot "of dead hardwoods and also a butt rot in living trees"
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5.5-7 x 4-5 microns, ovoid to broadly elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 22-28 x 7-8 microns, clavate, with a basal clamp; cystidioles not projecting, 18-22 x 4-7 microns, fusoid, thin-walled, with basal clamp; hyphal system trimitic, context generative hyphae 2.5-5 microns wide, thin-walled, rarely branched, with clamp connections, context skeletal hyphae 3.5-5 microns wide, colorless, rarely branched, nonseptate, context binding hyphae 2.2-3.5 microns wide, thick-walled, colorless, much branched, nonseptate, trama hyphae similar
NAME ORIGIN  after C.J Sprague^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota

FLESH  up to 2.5cm thick, woody; not zoned, light reddish brown
PORES  7-8 per mm, circular, thick-walled; yellowish to reddish brown; tube layers distinctly stratified (each layer up to 0.3cm thick), woody, colored as flesh and continuous with it
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fuscocerrena portoricensis  (Fr.) Ryvarden^  Trans. Br. Mycol. Soc. 79: 280. 1982;  Polyporus portoricensis  Fr.;  Irpex farinaceus  Fr.;  Irpex coriaceus  Berk. & Ravenel;  Cerrenella subcoriacea  Murrill
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Fuscocerrena portoricensis is recognized in the field by a dark brown cap and the often greenish white irregular spore-bearing surface that becomes dark brown and split when old.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1). RANGE Fuscocerrena portoricensis has been found in WA, AL, FL, IA, IL, LA, MD, MO, OH, SC, TN, WV, TX, and MN, and "Central and South America to Southern Brazil" is mentioned in the Distribution. It is presumably also found in Puerto Rico.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 1cm broad, 1-5cm long (often laterally fused), 0.1-0.2cm thick, growing flat with pore surface exposed or bent outward to form a cap, tough and leathery, tightly attached, margin sharply delimited toward substrate; upper surface umber to dark vinaceous brown, margin dark brown; tomentose, grooved and zoned, margin finely velvety
FLESH  up to 0.1cm thick, dark brown, trama between tubes also dark brown
PORES  "irregular, at first poroid, pores angular 1-2 per mm, later the pores split and the hymenophore becomes daedaleoid, sinuous to hydnoid with flattened teeth, often slightly incised"; surface dark brown to greenish white, "actively growing specimens with greenish-white tube walls with a brown tint while the dissepiments remain brown due to projecting skeletal hyphae, tube walls in old specimens dark brown as the whitish, fine grainy layer disintegrates and the brown color emerges"
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual to perennial, on hardwoods, causing a white rot of dead hardwoods
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5-7 x 2-2.5 microns, cylindric, often slightly bent, inamyloid, colorless, thin-walled^; basidia 4-spored, 8-14 x 4-6 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia or other sterile elements absent, dendrohyphidia "present in the hymenium, not seen along the dissepiments, up to 22 microns long, with numerous protuberances in the upper part, often covered with fine crystals and their outline may be difficult to observe, more irregularly bottle-shaped in the basal part and with a basal clamp"; hyphal system dimitic, generative hyphae 2-4 microns wide, colorless to faintly yellowish, thin-walled, with clamp connections, skeletal hyphae predominant, 2-6 microns wide, "dark brown to yellowish brown, thick-walled to apparently solid, usually unbranched, rarely dichotomously branched and in rare cases almost arboriform", in one specimen seen with interhyphal and apical swellings (up to 15 microns wide)
NAME ORIGIN  means "from Puerto Rico"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Hydnochaete species are "in general more rusty brown and microscopically distinguished by simple septate generative hyphae and above all, by long, acute and dark brown setae", (Gilbertson).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1)

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fuscoporia ferrea  (Pers.) G. Cunn.^  Bull. N.Z. Dept. Sci. Industr. Res., Pl. Dis. Div. 73: 7. 1948;  Phellinus ferreus  (Pers.) Bourdot & Galzin  Soc. Mycol. France Bul. 41: 247. 1925;  Polyporus ferreus  Pers.;  Poria ferrea  (Pers.) Overh.
ENGLISH NAMES  cinnamon porecrust
NOTES  Features include a yellow-brown pore surface growing flat on wood, small pores, and microscopic characters including setae in the spore-bearing surface.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where noted. RANGE Fuscoporia ferrea has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, NB, NF, NS, PE, SK, AK, CA, FL, MD, MT, NT, NY, PA, TN, VA, and WV, (Gilbertson)
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 23cm across, growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, woody, not readily separable; margin fertile or narrowly sterile and then up to 0.2cm wide, yellowish brown, tomentose
FLESH  up to 0.1cm, corky; not zoned, yellowish brown
PORES  6-7 per mm, circular, with thick walls, yellowish brown; often cracking extensively; tube layers indistinctly stratified, each layer up to 1cm thick, colored as flesh and continuous with it
STEM  none
ODOR  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
TASTE  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, on dead wood of conifers and hardwoods, associated with uniform white rot^, (Gilbertson), all year (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5-7.5 x 2-2.5 microns, cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 12-14 x 5-6 microns, clavate, simple-septate at base; setae abundant, 22-29 x 6-7 microns, subulate [awl-shaped] to slightly ventricose, brown in KOH, thick-walled; hyphae in context 2-4.5 microns wide, dark brown to almost colorless in KOH; thick-walled or thin-walled, with rare branching, rarely simple-septate; hyphae of trama similar
NAME ORIGIN  means "of a rusty iron color"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Fuscoporia ferruginosa "has shorter spores and usually has setal hyphae in the marginal and context tissue", (Gilbertson). F. ferruginosa has a reddish brown pore surface, broader spores (3.0-3.5 microns wide), longer setae (up to 65 microns long), and typically, "setal hyphae in the margin and contest", (Ginns).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Phellinus ferreus), Ginns(28)*, Buczacki(1)* (as Phellinus ferreus)

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fuscoporia ferruginosa  (Schrad.) Murrill^  N. Amer. Fl. (New York) 9(1): 5. 1907 [as 'ferruginosus'];  Phellinus ferruginosus  (Schrad.: Fr.) Bourdot & Galzin  Hym. France p. 625. 1928;  Polyporus ferruginosus  Schrad.: Fr.;  Poria ferruginosa  (Schrad.: Fr.) P. Karst.
ENGLISH NAMES  rusty porecrust
NOTES  Features include a rusty reddish brown pore surface growing flat on wood, and microscopic characters including setae in the hymenium and setal hyphae in the context.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where specified. RANGE Fuscoporia ferruginosa has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NT, NB, NF, NS, ON, SK, YT, AK, AL, AZ, CA, CO, KY, LA, ME, MN, MO, MT, NC, NM, NY, OH, PA, TN, VA, and WV, (Gilbertson), and it occurs in Europe and Asia, (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  becoming widely spread out, growing flat on wood, "tough to soft-spongy, not easily separable"; margin up to 2cm wide, soft-spongy, tawny, often appearing under a hand lens to have setae
FLESH  up to 0.15cm thick, soft-fibrous; azonate, yellowish brown
PORES  "usually 7-9 per mm, but in some unusual specimens 2-3 per mm", circular, with thick tomentose walls; ferruginous; tube layer up to 2cm thick, slightly darker than the flesh or the same color, continuous with the flesh
STEM  none
ODOR  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
TASTE  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on dead wood of hardwoods and conifers, causing a white laminated rot^, (Gilbertson), all year (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5-7 x 3-3.5 microns, cylindric or short-oblong, smooth, inamyloid^; basidia 4-spored, 11-14 x 4.5-6.5 microns, clavate, simple-septate at base; setae abundant, 25-65 x 6-8 microns, mostly subulate [awl-shaped], brown in KOH, thick-walled; hyphae of context of 2 types: 1) 2-4 microns wide, light brown in KOH, rather thin-walled, with rare branching, 2) setal hyphae 5-8 microns wide, darker reddish brown, very thick-walled, tapering to a seta-like point, unbranched, hyphae of trama similar to thin-walled type, (Gilbertson), spores 4.5-5.5 x 3-3.5 microns, elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, sometimes with droplets; setae in the hymenium 20-40 x 6-7 microns, dark brown, setae in trama 3-6 microns wide and up to 100 microns long, light brown, setae in mycelium up to 500 microns long and 10 microns wide, dark brown, thick-walled, almost without a lumen, (Breitenbach for Phellinus ferruginosus (Schrad.: Fr.) Pat.)
NAME ORIGIN  means "of a rusty iron color"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Fuscoporia ferrea has narrow spores and lacks setal hyphae, (Gilbertson). Fuscoporia ferrea has a yellowish brown pore surface, narrower spores (2.0-2.5 microns wide), and shorter setae (25-45 microns long), and it lacks setal hyphae, (Ginns). Phellinidium weirii and Phellinidium ferrugineofuscum have setal hyphae but they occur in the tramal tissue and project into the tubes, (Gilbertson). Fomitiporia punctata has beaked cystidioles but no setae, (Breitenbach).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Phellinus ferruginosus), Ginns(28)*, Phillips(1)* (who describes Phellinus ferruginosus (Fr.) Pat. with more than one tube layer and growing on hardwoods), Breitenbach(2)* (describing Phellinus ferruginosus (Schrad.: Fr.) Pat.), Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fuscoporia gilva  (Schw.: Fr.) T. Wagner & M. Fischer^  Mycologia 94(6): 1013. 2002;  Phellinus gilvus  (Schwein.) Pat.;  Polyporus gilvus  (Schwein.) Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  mustard-yellow polypore, oak conk
NOTES  Features include bracket-like yellowish brown fruitbodies on living and dead hardwoods, small dark purplish red-brown pores, and microscopic characters including setae.^ Wagner(1) transferred this species to Fuscoporia gilva (Schw.: Fr.) T. Wagner & M. Fischer on the basis of molecular and other work. RANGE It has been found in BC (Ginns(28)), and WA, OR, ID, MB, ON, AL, AR, AZ, CA, CT, DE, FL, GA, IA, IL, IN, KS, KY, LA, MD, MI, MN, MO, MS, MT, NC, ND, NE, NJ, NM, NY, OH, OK, PA, SC, TN, TX, VA, VT, WI, and WV, (Gilbertson).

FLESH  tough, corky; bright ochraceous to dark yellow brown or colored like cap^, (Arora), up to 2cm thick, fibrous, zoned; bright yellowish brown, (Gilbertson)
PORES  4-8 per mm, grayish brown becoming reddish brown or dark brown; 1-5 tube layers, each 0.1-0.5cm thick^, (Arora), 6-8 per mm, circular, thick-walled; dark purplish brown; tube layer up to 1cm thick, white-stuffed, but tramal tissue colored as flesh and continuous with it, (Gilbertson)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  unknown (Arora)
HABITAT  often perennial, single or more often in groups on dead or occasionally living hardwoods, producing a general delignifying decay of sapwood, making it whitish and brittle; rarely on conifers^, (Arora), perennial or annual, on living and dead hardwoods, especially oak, rarely on conifers, associated with a uniform white rot of dead wood of hardwoods and a heart rot of living trees, (Gilbertson)
SPORE DEPOSIT  whitish^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4-5 x 3-3.5 microns, elliptic to oval, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 5-11 x 5-7 microns, broadly clavate, simple-septate at base; setae abundant, 20-30 x 5-6 microns, subulate [awl-shaped], sharp, dark brown in KOH, thick-walled; hyphae of context of 2 types: 1) 3-7 microns wide, "dark reddish brown in KOH, thick-walled, rarely branched, simple-septate", 2) 3-5 microns wide, "pale yellowish brown, thin-walled, with occasional branching, simple-septate"; hyphae of trama similar, (Gilbertson), spores 4-5 x 2.5-3.5 microns, oblong-elliptic, smooth, rarely found, (Arora)
NAME ORIGIN  means "pale yellow"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Phellinus gilvus), Lincoff(2)* (as Phellinus gilvus), Arora(1) (as Phellinus gilvus), Wagner(1), Ginns(28)*, Desjardin(6)* (as Phellinus gilvus), Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Fuscoporia viticola  (Schwein.) Murrill^  N. Amer. Fl. (New York) 9(1): 4. 1907;  Phellinus viticola  (Schwein.: Fr.) Donk  Persoonia 4: 342. 1966;  Polyporus viticola  Schwein.: Fr.;  Fomes viticola  (Schwein.: Fr.) J. Lowe;  Fomes tenuis  P. Karst.;  Trametes setosus  Weir
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) a reddish brown to blackish bracket-like or shelf-like cap with a yellowish brown pore surface, or the pore surface flat on wood, 2) small pores, 3) tubes that are usually whitish within, 4) perennial growth on hardwoods and conifers, and 5) microscopic characters including narrow cylindric spores and long narrow setae.^ Gilbertson(1) says that [at least in North America] it occurs most commonly on conifers but also frequently collected on hardwoods in North America, but Ginns(28) says that [at least in western North America] it grows on live and dead hardwoods, rarely on conifers. This has been called Trametes isabellina Fr., and the flat form has been called Poria isabellina Overh. RANGE Fuscoporia viticola has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NB, NF, NS, ON, PE, YT, AK, AZ, CA, CO, CT, FL, IL, KY, MA, MD, ME, MI, MN, MO, MT, NC, ND, NH, NM, NY, OH, PA, SD, TN, UT, VA, WV, and WY, and occurs circumglobally, (Gilbertson). It also occurs in Europe (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  usually bent outwards from pore surface flat on wood, to form shelf-like cap, often bracket-like or entirely flat on wood, flat pore surfaces extending up to 30cm, caps generally dimidiate [roughly semicircular] or narrow shelf-like, up to 1.5cm x 6cm x 1cm, reddish brown to blackish; hirsute [hairy] to almost bald, grooved; "margin usually lighter reddish brown, tomentose to hirsute, acute or rounded"^, (Gilbertson), 1.5-8cm along wood, projecting 1-3cm outward, 0.5-1.5cm thick at attachment, "semicircular to elongated and bracketlike", broadly attached, (sometimes entirely flat on wood with pore surface exposed), "corky and tough when fresh, woody and hard when dry"; upper surface dark rust brown to gray-brown; "undulating and with narrow concentric zones, rough, appressed-tomentose"; margin thin, sharp, slightly wavy, "somewhat lighter to yellow brown", (Breitenbach)
FLESH  up to 0.3cm thick, corky-fibrous; faintly zoned, yellowish brown^, (Gilbertson)
PORES  4-7 per mm, circular to angular, thick-walled, yellowish brown; tube layers up to 0.5cm thick, colored as flesh and continuous with it, "the tubes usually whitish within"^, (Gilbertson), 3-4(5) per mm, rounded-angular; gray-brown, cinnamon-brown; tube layer 0.1-0.7cm thick ("tube length 1-7 mm"), layered, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, most commonly on conifers, but also on hardwoods^, associated with uniform white rot of dead wood, (Gilbertson), [at least in western North America] grows on live and dead hardwoods, rarely on conifers, (Ginns)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5.5-8 x 1.5-2 microns, cylindric, straight or curved, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, frequently with 2 droplets^; basidia 4-spored, 9-10 x 5-6 microns, clavate, simple-septate at base; setae abundant, 25-75 x 5-8 microns, narrowly subulate [awl-shaped], thick-walled; hyphae of context of 2 types: 1) 2-3 microns wide, "brown in KOH, thick-walled, rarely simple-septate", 2) 2-3 microns wide, pale yellowish brown to colorless in KOH, "thin-walled, with occasional branching, simple-septate"; hyphae of trama similar, (Gilbertson), spores 6-8 x 1.5-2 microns, cylindric, allantoid, smooth, inamyloid, colorless; setae 40-60 x 5-8 microns, pointed, brown, thick-walled, exserted far beyond hymenium, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  means "inhabiting grape vines (Vitis)"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Porodaedalea chrysoloma has yellow-brown pores and oval spores (Breitenbach).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Phellinus viticola), Ginns(28)*, Breitenbach(2)* (as Phellinus viticola)

FAMILY  Ganodermataceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ganoderma applanatum  (Pers.) Pat.^  Soc. Mycol. France Bull. 5: 67. 1889
ENGLISH NAMES  artist's conk, artist's bracket, artist's fungus
NOTES  Ganoderma applanatum forms a flat semicircular to hoof-shaped or shelf-like fruitbody that has a hard brown to grayish black upper surface, fine whitish pores that turn instantly brown when scratched, punky or corky flesh, and brown or reddish brown spores that often dust the upper surface. It is commonly known as the artist's conk because a sharp instrument can produce brown drawings on the white pore surface that will be permanent after drying. It has been calculated that this fungus can produce 4.5 trillion spores annually^, (Sept(1)). It is common in the Pacific Northwest. RANGE G. applanatum is found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NWT, NB, NF, NS, ON, PE, PQ, SK, YT, AK, AL, AR, AZ, CA, CO, CT, FL, GA, IA, IL, IN, KS, KY, LA, MA, MD, ME, MI, MN, MO, MS, MT, NC, ND, NE, NH, NJ, NM, NV, NY, OH, OK, PA, SC, SD, TN, TX, UT, VA, VT, WI, WV, and WY, (Gilbertson)
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  bracket-like, horizontally flattened, rarely hoof-shaped, woody to corky, margin thin; upper surface grayish to black or brown, with white margin; crustose, irregular to tuberculate, usually covered with dusty layer of chocolate brown spores^, (Gilbertson), (5)10-40(70)cm along wood, projecting (4)25-40cm, 2-6cm thick, bracket-like or on horizontal wood plate-shaped, light brown when young, then cinnamon-brown to gray-brown, black when old; with concentric undulations or tuberculate, smooth, dull, often covered with spore deposit, crust indentable when old; margin inflated, sharp, whitish when fresh, (Breitenbach), 5-50cm wide, convex to steeply hoof-shaped; gray to brown or grayish black; often warty and zoned with hard, horny crust that becomes furrowed or cracked, (Lincoff(2)), 5-75cm or more broad (Arora for Ganoderma applanatum group), appears varnished (Lincoff(1))
FLESH  corky; purplish brown^, (Gilbertson), corky-cottony; red-brown to dark brown with whitish streaks, (Breitenbach), 0.5-5cm thick, brown, (Lincoff(2)), felt-like; brown, fairly dark, with whitish zones, (Lincoff(1)), soft and corky to punky (soft and fibrous), (McKnight)
PORES  4-6 per mm, circular, white, quickly bruising brown when handled, becoming dull buff when old; tube layers each up to 1.35cm thick, separated by layer of flesh, and colored the same as the flesh^, (Gilbertson), (4)5-6 per mm, white to cream, when fresh turning brownish when touched; tube layer 0.5-1(2)cm, layered in fruitbodies several years old, (Breitenbach), 4-6 per mm, circular; white, readily bruising darker, often aging yellowish to pale brownish, tubes 0.4-1.2cm thick, distinctly layered, (Lincoff(2)), tubes rust-colored (Lincoff(1))
STEM  usually absent
ODOR  none^ (Miller)
TASTE  mild^ (Miller)
EDIBILITY  too tough and woody (Arora)
HABITAT  perennial, dead standing trees, stumps, and living trees of numerous genera of hardwoods, also common on conifers in Pacific Northwest and rarely on conifers elsewhere, causes a white mottle rot and butt rot of living aspen, also found on dead standing or fallen hardwoods^, (Gilbertson), young buttons start in summer but continue fruiting all year (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown to cocoa brown or reddish brown^ (Arora), rust-brown (Lincoff(1)), appear chocolate brown on cap surface (Gilbertson)
MICROSCOPIC  spores given as (8)9-12 x 6.5-8 microns at one place in text [an error] but 6-9 x 4.5-6 microns in the key, oval, truncate at distal end, with two walls connected by interwall pillars, inamyloid, brown^; basidia 4-spored, 20-45 x 8-10 microns, clavate, some tapering to narrow base, "arising from intercalary positions on subhymenial hyphae", with basal clamp connection; cystidia none; hyphal system trimitic, context generative hyphae inconspicuous, 2-5 microns wide, thin-walled, with clamp connections, skeletal hyphae 3-6.5 microns wide, brown, thick-walled, aseptate, "occasionally branched, the extremities tapering to acute apices", binding hyphae few, (Gilbertson), spores 6.5-9.5 x 5-7 microns, broadly elliptic and blunt at one end, with thick double wall (outer perforated, inner chambered), (Lincoff(2)), 9-13 x 6-8 microns (Lincoff(1), presumably an error), 6-9 x 4.5-6 microns (McKnight), spores 7-8(9) x 4.5-6 microns, broadly elliptic, truncate, indistinctly verrucose-uneven, truncate end with colorless germ pore, inamyloid, (Breitenbach), spores 6-9 x 4-6 microns (Ginns(25), Ginns(28))
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin, meaning "flattened"^, (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  Ganoderma brownii has larger spores and a yellowish cast to the pore surface (Gilbertson). G. brownii has flesh that is darker in color and thicker (3-8cm), (Arora). G. brownii has less flattened fruitbodies, is restricted to hardwoods, and has spores that are much larger, (Siegel), Ganoderma annularis (Fr.) Gilb. of California is also similar, with flesh represented by a thin layer, most of the tissue being composed of old tubes, and has spores 11-14 x 7-8 microns, (Gilbertson). Fomitopsis mounceae has a reddish resinous band near the margin when fresh, cream to buff flesh, and a pore surface that does not mark brown easily. See also SIMILAR section of Ganoderma oregonense.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Breitenbach(2)*, Arora(1)* (as Ganoderma applanatum group), Lincoff(2)*, Lincoff(1)*, Miller(14)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)*, McKnight(1)*, Sept(1)*, Ginns(25), Ginns(28)*, Siegel(2)*, Bacon(1)*, AroraPocket*, Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Ganodermataceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ganoderma brownii  (Murrill) Gilb.^  Mycologia 53: 505. 1962;  Elfvingia brownii  Murrill
ENGLISH NAMES  western artist's conk
NOTES  Ganoderma brownii is somewhat similar to the common Ganoderma applanatum but with a slight yellowish case to the pore surface, thicker, darker flesh, and larger spores.^ RANGE found in CA (Gilbertson) and BC (Ginns(28), three collections)
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  flesh black in 3% KOH
CAP  up to 20cm x 10cm x 5cm, stemless, semicircular, flattened horizontally to hoof-shaped, cap surface "finely tomentose and yellowish at the growing margin, quickly becoming crustose" with a hard, horny crust up to 0.1cm thick; pale vinaceous to grayish brown; shallowly to deeply sulcate (grooved) and faintly zoned^, (Gilbertson), 6.0-60.0cm broad, "fan-shaped to shelf-like, seldom hoof-shaped"; "brown to grayish brown"; "surface crustose, dull (not varnished), irregularly lumpy, often with concentric ridges and furrows", "typically dusted with brown spores", (Desjardin)
FLESH  up to 4cm thick, firm-fibrous; dark purplish brown; concentrically zonate^, (Gilbertson), 1.0-6.0cm thick, "tough, woody to corky"; brown, (Desjardin)
PORES  4-5 per mm, rounded, with very thick entire walls; "with a bright yellowish cast, bruising dark brown, the yellowish cast remaining on dried specimens"; tube layer "purplish brown, sharply differentiated from the context, tubes whitish within, up to 1.5cm thick"^ (Gilbertson), 4-6 per millimeter, "white, quickly bruising brown, becoming yellowish buff to buff-brown when dried"; tubes 0.4-1.3cm long, "stratified, in multiple layers separated by a thin layer of brown context tissue in older fruitbodies", brown, (Desjardin)
STEM  none^ (Gilbertson, Desjardin)
ODOR  indistinct^ (Desjardin)
TASTE  indistinct^ (Desjardin)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, on hardwoods, causing white rot of living and dead hardwoods^, (Gilbertson), single or in small groups "on living or standing dead hardwood trees, stumps, or logs"; in California common, fruiting throughout the year, primarily on bay laurel, (Desjardin), collections in BC on live Aesculus hippocastaneum (Horsechestnut), live Prunus cerasifera var. pissardii (a cultivar of Cherry Plum), and an unidentified log, (Ginns)
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Gilbertson)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 11-12 x 7-8 microns, broadly elliptic, inamyloid, brown in KOH, with a truncate apex and an apical germ pore, wall two-layered with interwall pillars^; basidia 4-spored, about 23-11 microns, "collapsed and difficult to discern in type specimen, apparently broadly clavate with a sharply narrowed base"; cystidia absent; hyphal system trimitic: context generative hyphae 2.5-3.5 microns wide, obscure, colorless, thin-walled, with clamp connections, context skeletal hyphae 2-10 microns wide, pale brown in KOH, thick-walled, nonseptate, with occasional branching, context binding hyphae "rare to apparently absent in crushed mounts of context tissue", trama generative and skeletal hyphae similar, trama binding hyphae 2-5.5 microns wide, colorless to pale brown, "thick-walled, nonseptate, much branched", "often dendritically branched with tapering, pointed tips", (Gilbertson), 10-12 x 7-9 microns, broadly elliptic, inamyloid, "truncate at one end, thick-walled with internal wall ornamentation", cystidia absent; "hyphal system trimitic", generative hyphae with clamp connections, (Desjardin)
NAME ORIGIN  named for V.S. Brown^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Ganoderma applanatum has smaller spores and lacks the yellow cast to the pore surface (Gilbertson), G. applanatum has flesh that is lighter in color and thinner, (Arora). G. applanatum generally has flatter, fan-shaped fruitbodies, has smaller spores (7-8(9) x 4.5-6 microns), and is not restricted to hardwoods, (Siegel(2)). See also SIMILAR section of Ganoderma oregonense.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Arora(1), Desjardin(6)*, Ginns(28)*, Siegel(2)*
