FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phellinopsis conchata  (Pers.) Y.C. Dai^  Fungal Diversity 45: 309. 2010;  Phellinus conchatus  (Pers.: Fr.) Quel.;  Polyporus conchatus  Pers.: Fr.;  Fomes conchatus  (Pers.: Fr.) Gillet
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include light brown to black, bald, incrusted, grooved, rough fruitbodies that are bracket-like, shelf-like, or growing flat on wood with the pores exposed, a yellowish brown pore surface, growth on hardwoods, and microscopic characters including setae that arise from the tramal hyphae, not from the hymenium, and the colorless spore walls that become pale yellow.^ It is common in the northeast of North America but not in the northwest (Gilbertson). RANGE Phellinopsis conchata has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, MB, NB, NS, ON, PQ, AR, CO, FL, IA, IL, IN, MA, MD, ME, MI, MN, MO, MT, NC, ND, NH, NJ, NY, OH, PA, TN, TX, VT, WI, WV, and WY, (Gilbertson). In BC it is known from one report on Acer (maple), (Ginns)
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 11cm x 15cm x 4cm, bracket-like, bent back from flat pore surface to form shelf-like cap, or entirely flat on wood, dimidiate [roughly semicircular] or irregular in shape; light brown to black when old; bald, incrusted, grooved, rough, not zoned; margin rounded to sharp^, (Gilbertson), "semiresupinate, forming patches several centimeters to decimeters in size, attached tightly to the substrate", when on vertical surfaces with imbricate [shingled] bracket-like caps projecting 1-3(5)cm, tough and hard; dark brown to black-brown; concentrically zoned, "sometimes rather tomentose and overgrown with mosses"; margin sharp, margin of pore surface about 0.1cm wide, smooth and sterile, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  up to 0.4cm thick, woody; azonate, with one or more thin black layers^, (Gilbertson)
PORES  pore surface yellowish brown; tube layers colored as flesh, indistinctly stratified, each layer up to 0.2cm thick^, (Gilbertson), 4-7 per mm (Ginns), 3-6 per mm, "usually somewhat slit toward margin, labyrinthine-irpicoid" (maze-like - tooth-like); rust-brown to light brown; "undulating-nodose to stepped", often gray-pruinose, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
TASTE  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, single or imbricate [shingled] on dead wood of hardwoods, occasionally on living trees, associated with uniform white rot^, (Gilbertson), reported on live and dead hardwoods, principally on Salix (willow), (Ginns), on living or dead, standing or more rarely fallen trunks of hardwoods, (Breitenbach), all year (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  yellow-white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5-6.5 x 4-4.5 microns, oval to nearly round, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 10-12 x 6-7 microns, clavate, simple-septate at base; setae abundant, 20-50 x 7-9 microns, ventricose to subulate [awl-shaped], thick-walled, dark brown in KOH; hyphae of context of 2 types, mostly the first: 1) 2.5-4 microns wide, brown in KOH, thick-walled, rarely branched, rarely simple-septate, 2) 2-3 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled, simple-septate; hyphae of trama similar, (Gilbertson), spores 4-5.5 x 4-5 microns, nearly round, smooth, inamyloid, pale yellowish, thick-walled; setae in the hymenium 15-50 x 5-8 microns, subulate, "often without tips (as if broken off) and sometimes with hyaline appendage", brown, thick-walled, (Breitenbach), setae abundant, 20-50 x 7-9 microns, ventricose to subulate, (Ginns), the distinguishing features of the genus Phellinopsis are setae that arise from the tramal hyphae, not from the hymenium, and the colorless spores walls that become pale yellow, (Ginns(28))
NAME ORIGIN  means "like the half shell of a mollusc"^, (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Phellinopsis overholtsii is restricted to live Crataegus - it has pores 2-4 per mm, and has scarce setae, (Ginns).

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phellinopsis overholtsii  (Ginns) L.W. Zhou & Ginns^  Mycosystema 33(5): 1127. 2014;  Phellinus overholtsii  Ginns  Mycotaxon 21: 327. 1984;  Phellinopsis occidentalis  Overh. ex Y.C. Dai  Fungal Diversity 45: 309. 2010;  Phellinus occidentalis  (Overh.) Gilb.  in Lombard et al., Mycopath. et Mycol. Applic. 46: 352. 1972;  Fomes occidentalis  Overh.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features are shelf-like or bracket-like blackish to grayish caps, a light brown pore surface, growth restricted to hawthorn and microscopic characters including colorless spores that become pale yellow to brown and scattered setae arising from the tramal hyphae.^ Phellinopsis overholtsii is rather common in western Montana and northern Idaho (Gilbertson). The description is derived from Gilbertson(1). RANGE Phellinopsis overholtsii has been found in WA, OR, ID, MT, and UT, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  bent out from pore surface growing flat on wood to form shelf-like cap, or bracket-like, sometimes pore surface entirely flat on wood at first, caps up to 2cm x 6cm x 4cm, dimidiate [roughly semicircular] or laterally fused and elongate; blackish to grayish; faintly grooved and zoned, incrusted, bald; margin of pore surface up to 0.1cm wide, rounded, light yellowish brown, faintly tomentose
FLESH  up to 0.2cm thick, corky; not zoned, light reddish brown
PORES  2-4 per mm, angular to circular, thick-walled; light brown; tube layer up to 1.5cm thick, colored as flesh and continuous with it, the tubes lighter within
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, restricted to Crataegus species (hawthorns), causing uniform white rot of heartwood of living hawthorn
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5-6 x 4-5 microns, oval to nearly round, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, in old specimens distinctly brown^; basidia 4-spored, 11-16 x 5-5.5 microns, clavate, simple-septate at base; setae scattered, 40-50 x 8-14 microns, ventricose, thick-walled; hyphae of context of 2 types: 1) 2-5 microns wide, dark brown in KOH, thin-walled to thick-walled, rarely simple-septate, with rare branching, 2) 2-3 microns wide, colorless or pale yellowish, thin-walled, simple-septate, with frequent branching; hyphae of trama similar, (Gilbertson), setae are scarce, and spore walls become brown when old (Ginns(28)), the distinguishing features of the genus Phellinopsis are setae that arise from the tramal hyphae, not from the hymenium, and the colorless spores walls that become pale yellow, (Ginns(28)).
NAME ORIGIN  means 'western'
SIMILAR  Phellinopsis conchata is similar but has pores 4-7 per mm, abundant setae, and is not limited to live Crataegus (principally found on Salix (willow) as well as other hardwoods), (Ginns(28)). Porodaedalea pini is quite similar but grows on conifers
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Phellinus occidentalis), Ginns(26) (as Phellinus overholtsii), Ginns(28)

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phellinus arctostaphyli  (Long) Niemela^  Ann. Bot. Fenn. 12: 120. 1975;  Fomes arctostaphyli  Long
ENGLISH NAMES  manzanita conk
NOTES  Features include 1) hoof-shaped to flattened or semicircular fruitbodies on manzanita, 2) an upper surface that is grayish to blackened, bald, with a hard, grooved, surface layer, 3) firm reddish brown flesh that splits easily, with a core of tissue near the attachment that has pockets of white mycelium, 4) a grayish brown to rusty brown pore surface, and 5) microscopic characters including slightly thick-walled cyanophilous spores and scattered hymenial setae.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1). RANGE Phellinus arctostaphyli has been found in OR, CA, AZ, and NM, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  1-9cm wide, 1-3.5cm thick, shelf-like, flattened to hoof-shaped, semicircular; upper surface grayish becoming blackened; quickly bald and crustose [with a hard surface layer], grooved, becoming cracked when old; margin rounded, colored as upper surface to light brown
FLESH  up to 2cm thick, fissile [splitting readily], firm, woody, not zoned; reddish brown, "with a core of tissue near the attachment that has pockets of white mycelium and dark granular masses in a mass of tough, felty mycelium, this core often poorly developed or absent in small specimens"
PORES  5-6 per mm, circular; pale grayish brown to rusty brown; smooth; "dissepiments thick, tomentose, entire", "tube layers indistinctly stratified, older layers becoming stuffed with pale mycelium, up to 1cm thick"
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, primarily on Arctostaphylos [manzanita], but also on Adenostoma and rarely on Cercocarpus [mountain-mahogany], causing a uniform white rot of heartwood
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5-6 x 3.5-4.5 microns, oval, flattened on one side, smooth, inamyloid, cyanophilous, colorless, with slightly thickened walls^; basidia 4-spored, 10-12 x 7-8.5 microns, broadly clavate to elliptic, simple-septate at base; hymenial setae infrequent, 30-50 x 6-8.5 microns, subulate, dark brown in KOH, thick-walled; hyphal system dimitic: hyphae of fibrous context parallel, skeletal hyphae 2.5-5 microns wide, thick-walled, nonseptate or with occasional simple septa, with rare branching, 'core with aggregates of much-branched, interlocked hyphae, some contorted, swollen, often lobed ("core setae"), thick-walled, dark brown in KOH, up to 10 microns in diam', core also with fibrous tissue made up of interwoven colorless to golden hyphae, 1.5-2.5 microns wide, thin-walled to thick-walled, nonseptate, with frequent branching, hyphae of trama parallel, skeletal hyphae 2.5-5 microns wide, dark brown in KOH, thick-walled, nonseptate or rarely simple-septate, with rare branching, generative hyphae 2-4 microns wide, colorless to pale yellow, thin-walled, with simple septa and occasional branching
NAME ORIGIN  means "on Arctostaphylos"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Phellinus igniarius group has shorter setae. 
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Siegel(2)*

LATIN NAMES  Phellinus betulinus  (Murrill) Parmasto^  Folia cryptog. Estonica 43: 41. 2007
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  This is a member of the Phellinus igniarius complex according to Zhou, L.-W.(1). Features include 1) flat growth on wood of Betula occidentalis with the dull reddish brown pore surface exposed, 2) a narrow margin that is yellowish brown and tomentose, 3) woody consistency, 4) tight adherence to the wood, 5) round pores, 6-9 per mm with thick edges, 6) indistinctly stratified tubes, and 7) microscopic characters including spores 3.9-4.5 x 2.8-3.6 microns, a dimitic hyphal system, and setae.^ The description is derived from Ginns(28). RANGE In western North America, Phellinus betulinus is known from BC, WA, ID, and MT (Ginns(28) who notes that the range is uncertain because collections have been mislabeled as Phellinus laevigatus).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, widely spread out, "woody, adnate"; margin "yellowish brown, tomentose", up to 0.2cm wide^, (Ginns)
FLESH  up to 0.2cm thick; yellowish brown^, (Ginns)
PORES  6-9 per mm, round, "edges thick, entire", dull reddish brown; tubes "indistinctly stratified", each layer up to 0.1cm deep, paler than flesh
STEM  
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  on Betula occidentalis (Water Birch)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 3.9-4.5 x 2.8-3.6 microns, oval, thin-walled^; hyphal system dimitic: generative hyphae 2.0-3.5 microns wide, thin-walled, walls "pale yellow to hyaline", skeletal hyphae 2.5-5.0 microns wide; setae 13.2-18.6 x 5.3-6.8 microns, (Ginns)
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  Phellinus prunicola has larger spores (according to Parmasto concept) and grows on Prunus spp. (Ginns(28)). Phellinus laevigatus prefers Betula but also grows on other hardwoods: it has smaller pores (8-10 per mm), smaller spores (3-4 x 2.5-3 microns), and larger setae (17-30 x 6-9 microns in diameter), (Ginns(28) who notes that some collections are difficult to distinguish with confidence).
SOURCES  Ginns(28)*, Zhou, L.-W.(1)

LATIN NAMES  Phellinus everhartii  (Ellis & Galloway) A. Ames.^  Ann. Mycol. 11: 246. 1913;  Mucronoporus everhartii  Ellis & Galloway;  Fomes everhartii  (Ellis & Galloway) H. Schrenk & Spauld.
ENGLISH NAMES

FLESH  up to 5cm thick, woody; faintly zonate, reddish brown; "with masses of hard granular tissue that appear under 30 X lens as dark, solid or resinous areas in a matrix of paler brown interwoven mycelium"
PORES  5-6 per mm, circular to angular, with thick walls, glancing [showing a change in appearance from dull to lustrous when the orientation to light is changed] with a golden luster, dark yellowish brown to reddish brown; tube layers up to 0.6cm thick each, colored as flesh, rather distinctly stratified

ODOR

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phellinus igniarius  (L.: Fr.) Quel.^  Ench. Fung. p. 172. 1886  group;  Polyporus igniarius  L.: Fr.;  Ochroporus igniarius  (L.: Fr.) J. Schroet.
ENGLISH NAMES  flecked-flesh polypore, false tinder polypore, false tinder conk, willow bracket
NOTES  Features include a brown to grayish black bracket-like or hoof-shaped fruitbody with a velvety brown growing margin, small brown pores, brown to rusty brown flesh, and white-streaked tubes.^ There is a Phellinus igniarius complex of 15 species worldwide corresponding to a well supported clade in DNA study (Zhou, L.-W.(1)). Six of these occur in North America: Phellinus alni, Phellinus betulinus, Phellinus lundellii, Phellinus nigricans, Phellinus pomaceoides, and Phellinus tremulae. Notably absent are A) Phellinus igniarius itself about which they say "The specimens studied by Gilbertson and Ryvarden (1987) might actually represent other members of the P. igniarius complex.", B) Phellinus laevigatus - they imply that North American collections may fall under Phellinus betulinus, and C) Phellinus pomaceus - they rename the North American collections in the study Phellinus pomaceoides. (Zhou, L.-W.(1)). Tom Volk and Diane Pleninger wrote about Phellinus igniarius (in a wide sense), "In northern North America, the ash of Phellinus igniarius conks was widely used with indigenous plants before tobacco arrived. The Denaina, an Athabascan Indian people of the Alaskan interior, chewed a mixture of P. igniarius ash and balsam poplar bark in pre-contact times. Collections in Canadian and US museums show that P. igniarius was used with tobacco by the Micmac of Nova Scotia, Inuit of Labrador, Blackfoot of the North American Plains and Kwakiutl of the Pacific Northwest. This specific basidiocarp was selected and preferred above all other sources of ash. The reason for this is its high pH." (Volk). RANGE Phellinus igniarius group has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NT, NB, NF, NS, ON, PE, PQ, SK, YT, AK, AR, CA, CO, CT, IA, IN, KS, MA, MD, ME, MI, MN, MO, MT, ND, NE, NH, NY, OH, PA, SD, TN, UT, VA, VT, WI, WV, and WY, (Gilbertson), and Europe, Asia, and Africa, (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  cap tissue blackens in KOH (Arora)
CAP  5-20cm or more broad, 2-12(20)cm thick, shelf-like or bracket-like to hoof-shaped, hard and woody; brown, soon becoming grayish and finally black; finely hairy or velvety when young, usually with a crust, at least when older, when old "often cracked, furrowed and/or knobby"; "margin brown and velvety when actively growing"^, (Arora), up to 11cm x 20cm x 8cm, bracket-like or rarely bending out from a flat pore surface to form a shelf-like cap, hoof-shaped, or sometimes applanate [thin and horizontally flattened]; gray or blackish; bald, grooved, becoming deeply cracked, incrusted; margin bald and either colored as cap or colored yellowish brown, (Gilbertson), rusty brown, later gray, and finally black; concentrically ridged, becoming smooth then cracking, (Phillips), 5-25cm across, projecting 5-20cm out from wood, 3-15cm thick where attached, sometimes green from algae, margin inflated and obtuse, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  hard, woody; rusty-brown to brown^, (Arora), up to 2cm thick, woody; zoned, dark reddish brown; core absent or present next to wood, with white tissue intermixed, (Gilbertson)
PORES  4-5 per mm, grayish brown to brown; tube layers each 0.2-0.5cm thick but not always stratified, tubes of older layers often stuffed with white mycelium that shows as streaks or flecks when cut^, (Arora), 5-6 per mm, circular, thick-walled; pale cinnamon brown to dark purplish brown; tubes in distinct layers, each up to 0.4cm thick, the tubes white-stuffed, (Gilbertson), tubes rusty brown (Phillips), tube layers up to 10cm thick total in old specimens (Lincoff), red-brown when growing, gray to brownish gray at other times, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  mushroomy^ (Phillips)
TASTE  sour or bitter^ (Arora)
EDIBILITY  no (Arora)
HABITAT  perennial, single or in small groups on hardwood trunks (usually living), especially common on birch and aspen, occurring year round^, (Arora), perennial, on living hardwoods, continuing decay in dead trees; associated with uniform white rot of heartwood of living hardwoods, (Gilbertson), year round, but new pore layer in fall (Bacon)
SPORE DEPOSIT  whitish^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5-6.5 x 4.5-6 microns, broadly oval to nearly round, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 9-10 x 6-7 microns, broadly clavate, simple-septate at base; setae abundant to rare, 14-17 x 4-6 microns, ventricose to subulate [awl-shaped], core setae present in some specimens, up to 15 microns wide, irregularly lobed and branched, thick-walled; hyphae of context of 2 types: 1) 2-5 microns wide, "brown in KOH, thick-walled, distinct, with rare branching, aseptate", 2) 2-3 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled with occasional simple septa, very indistinct; hyphae of trama similar, 2-3 microns wide, (Gilbertson), spores 5.0-6.5 x 4.5-6 microns, broadly oval to nearly round, walls thick and colorless; generative hyphae indistinct, skeletal hyphae 2-5 microns wide, walls brown and thick; setae hymenial, 12-25 x 4-9 microns, "ventricose to subulate", (Ginns(28)), spores 5-7 x 4-6 microns, round or nearly round, smooth, (Arora), spores 5-7 x 4-6 microns, nearly round, smooth, colorless, slightly thick-walled, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  means "pertaining to fire"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Fomes fomentarius can be similar to very young and (rarely) older P. igniarius when the cap surface may be smooth and gray, but F. fomentarius has spores 12-20 microns long and lacks setae, (Ginns(28)). Phellinus lundellii has spores 4.5-6 x 4-5 microns, and setae 8-20 x 4-7 microns, and if a cap is present it rarely projects as much as 2cm, (Ginns(28)). See also SIMILAR section of Fomes fomentarius, Phellinus arctostaphyli, Phellinus pomaceus, Phellinus prunicola, and Phellinus tremulae.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Ginns(28)*, Zhou, L.-W.(1), Arora(1)*, Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Courtecuisse(1)* (as Ochroporus), Breitenbach(2)*, Sept(1)*, Bacon(1)*, Buczacki(1)*, Volk(2)* (with information from Diane Pleninger)

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phellinus laevigatus  (Fr.) Bourdot & Galzin^  Hym. France. p.264. 1928;  Polyporus laevigatus  Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include flat growth on hardwood especially birch, firm attachment and woody consistency, a dull reddish brown pore surface, small, thick-walled pores, and microscopic characters including small spores, parallel tramal hyphae, and setae. There is some molecular evidence suggesting that this species does not occur in North America, despite the extensive records noted in Gilbertson(1).^ Ginns(28) says (with Latin names italicized), "The most reliable characters of P. laevigatus are the small pores, small basidiospores, and the parallel arrangement of the tramal hyphae. The features of P. betulinus and P. prunicola are similar to, or overlap, those of P. laevigatus, making it difficult to name with confidence some collections." The spores are given as 3-4 x 2.5-3.0 microns for P. laevigatus and 3.9-4.5 x 2.8-3.6 microns for P. betulinus, (Ginns(28)). In the Ginns(28) key, P. laevigatus with pores 8-10 per mm and setae 6-9 microns in diam is separated from P. betulinus with pores 6-9 per mm and setae 5.0-6.8 microns in diam. However, in the Zhou, L.-W.(1) DNA study, nine closely related collections separated into three from Europe on Betula (designated P. laevigatus), four from North America on Betula, Magnolia, and Fagus (designated P. betulinus), and two from China on Betula (designated P. parmastoi which is a new name for P. betulinus var. orienticus). Gilbertson lists P. laevigatus for many North American locations (but not P. betulinus), with growth on dead hardwoods, especially birch, pores 8-10 per mm, spores 3-4 x 2.5-3 microns, and setae 17-30 x 6-9 microns, the same measurements as in Ginns(28). RANGE Phellinus laevigatus has been reported from British Columbia, Washington, Oregon, Idaho, Alberta, Nova Scotia, Ontario, Alabama, Arkansas, Florida, Kentucky, Louisiana, Maine, Minnesota, Missouri, Montana, New York, North Carolina, Oklahoma, Pennsylvania, Texas, Virginia, and West Virginia, (Gilbertson), and Europe including the British Isles (Buczacki), and Switzerland (Breitenbach). Many of the North American collections may be Phellinus betulinus, some may be Phellinus prunicola, and Phellinus laevigatus has not been proven for North America by molecular means.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, becoming widely spread out, woody, firmly attached; margin up to 0.2cm wide, yellowish brown, tomentose^, (Gilbertson), growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, "woody, adnate"; margin up to 0.2cm wide, "yellowish brown, tomentose", (Ginns(28)), forming patches several centimeters to several decimeters across, growing flat on wood (but on vertical surfaces sometimes knot-like or with a tendency to form narrow extended brackets), attached +/- loosely; marginal zone sterile, lighter, with a tendency to detach from the wood, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  up to 0.2cm thick, yellowish brown, not zoned^, (Gilbertson), up to 0.2cm thick, yellowish brown, (Ginns), about 0.1cm thick, corky, tough; tobacco brown, (Breitenbach)
PORES  8-10 per mm, circular, thick-walled, dull reddish brown, surface "often becoming deeply cracked into angular blocks with age or drying"; tube layers indistinctly stratified, paler than flesh, each layer up to 0.1cm thick^, (Gilbertson), 8-10 per mm, round, "edges thick, entire", pore surface "dull reddish brown, becoming cracked into angular blocks"; tubes "indistinctly stratified", each layer up to 0.1cm deep, "paler than context", (Ginns), 5-8 per mm, round; tobacco brown to cinnamon brown, later dark gray-brown; tube layer 0.1-0.4cm thick ("tube length 1-4mm"), distinctly stratified, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
TASTE  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, on dead hardwoods, especially Betula (birch), also reported on Picea (spruce), associated with white laminated rot of dead hardwoods^, (Gilbertson), perennial, on hardwoods, preferring Betula, causing a white laminated rot, (Ginns), all year (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 3-4 x 2.5-3 microns, oval, smooth, inamyloid, colorless to pale golden yellow in older specimens^; basidia 2-4-spored, 7-11 x 4-5 microns, nearly round to pear-shaped, simple-septate at base; setae abundant, 17-30 x 6-9 microns, subulate [awl-shaped] to ventricose, dark brown in KOH, thick-walled; hyphae of context of 2 types: 1) 2.5-5 microns, "dark brown in KOH, thick-walled, rarely branched, aseptate", 2) 2-3.5 microns thick, pale yellowish to colorless in KOH, "thin-walled, simple-septate, with frequent branching"; hyphae of trama similar, with parallel arrangement, (Gilbertson), spores 3-4 x 2.5-3.0 microns, oval, walls colorless to pale golden yellow, "typically slightly thickened"; hyphal system dimitic, generative hyphae 2.0-3.5 microns wide, walls thin, pale yellow to colorless, skeletal hyphae 2.5-5.0 microns wide; setae abundant, 17-30 x 6-9 microns, "subulate to ventricose"; tramal hyphae parallel, (Ginns), spores 4-5 x 3.5-4 microns, nearly round, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, thick-walled; setae in hymenium 10-20 x 4-8 microns, dark-brown, thick-walled, setae absent from trama, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  means "smooth"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Phellinus prunicola is very similar but has slightly larger pores and spores, (Gilbertson). P. prunicola grows on Prunus, pores are 6-9 per mm, spores are larger using the Parmasto measurement for P. prunicola, and setae are 16-22 x 4.5-6.8 microns wide, (Ginns(28)). Phellinus betulae is limited to Betula occidentalis, pores are 6-9 per mm, spores are 3.9-4.5 x 2.8-3.6 microns, and setae are 13.2-18.6 x 5.3-6.8 microns, (Ginns(28)).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Ginns(28)*, Breitenbach(2)*, Buczacki(1)*, Zhou, L.-W.(1)

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phellinus lundellii  Niemela^  Ann. bot. fenn. 9: 51. 1972
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  This is a member of the Phellinus igniarius complex according to Zhou, L.-W.(1). Features include 1) a growth pattern ranging from completely flat growth on wood to having a projecting elongated cap that projects up to 1.5cm, 2) when present the cap becoming blackish, bald, shallowly grooved, smooth to cracked, 3) thin, reddish brown, tough-fibrous flesh, 4) a pore surface that is reddish brown with round pores 5-6 per mm, 5) growth on dead birch or alder, and 6) microscopic characters. One collection is known from the Pacific Northwest.^ RANGE Phellinus lundellii has been recorded from BC, QC, and NC (Gilbertson(1)). In BC, it is known from one collection on Alnus rubra on Vancouver Island, (Ginns).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  growing flat on wood, to narrowly reflexed, may have a more distinct, elongated cap on vertical surfaces, upper surface projecting up to 1.5cm, some becoming blackish and bald, crust-like, shallowly sulcate (grooved), smooth to rimose (cracked), often with a wavy margin^, (Gilbertson), adnate; cap when present rarely projecting as much as 2cm, "frequently elongated", (Ginns)
FLESH  thin, usually less than 0.1cm thick, "dark reddish brown, tough-fibrous, fissile"^, (Gilbertson)
PORES  5-6 per mm, circular, with thick walls, pore surface "smooth, becoming dark reddish brown"; "tube layers indistinctly stratified, hard and woody", in aggregate up to 1.5cm thick, "older tubes becoming stuffed with whitish mycelium and old layers appear streaked"^, (Gilbertson), pore surface reddish brown; tubes indistinctly stratified, each layer up to 0.25cm thick, (Ginns)
STEM  
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, on dead Betula (birch) and Alnus (alder) in North America, causing a uniform white rot, but on a few other genera in Europe^, (Gilbertson)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4.5-6 x 4-5 microns, oval to broadly elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, with thickened walls^; basidia 4-spored, 10-12 x 5-6 microns, "broadly clavate to ellipsoid", simple-septate at base; hyphal system dimitic, context generative hyphae 2-3 microns wide, thin-walled, colorless, "simple-septate, with occasional branching", context skeletal hyphae 2-4 microns wide, "thick-walled, yellowish brown, with some simple septa, rarely branched"; "tramal hyphae similar, interwoven"; setae "abundant in hymenium", 8-20 x 4-7 microns, "subulate to slightly ventricose, thick-walled, brown in KOH, often flexuous", "mostly rather sharp pointed", (Gilbertson), spores 4.5-6.0 x 4-5 microns, walls colorless, "thin to slightly thickened"; generative hyphae colorless to pale brown; setae walls yellowish brown, (Ginns(28))
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  Phellinus igniarius has wider setae, up to 9 microns wide, and spores up to 6.5 microns wide, (Ginns(28)). Phellinus laevigatus has smaller pores (8-10 per mm) "and the tramal hyphae have a parallel arrangement (not interwoven)", (Ginns(28)).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Ginns(28)*, Zhou, L.-W.(1)

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phellinus pomaceoides  L.W. Zhou & Y.C. Dai^  in Zhou, Vlasak, Qin & Dai, Mycologia 108: 199. 2016
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include bracket-like or shelf-like fruitbodies that are light gray to light grayish brown to blackish on upper surface, with small, grayish brown to yellowish brown to reddish brown pores, and growth on living peach trees, cherry trees, plum trees, and other Prunus species.^ The preceding summary here represents what has been called Phellinus pomaceus in North America. Zhou, L.-W.(1) presented molecular evidence supported by spore sizes that some to all of the Phellinus "pomaceus" collections in North America is a separate species called Phellinus pomaceoides. Zhou, L.-W.(1) describe Phellinus pomaceoides as a new species with pores 6-9 per mm and spores (3.5)4-5 x 3-4(4.5) microns. They say that is resembles P. pomaceus which has pores 4-7 per mm (Niemela 1977) and spores 5.8-6.4 x 4.6-5.0 microns (Niemela 1977). Those measurements for P. pomaceus in Gilbertson & Ryvarden are quite different: 7-9 per mm and 4-5 x 3-4.5 microns which resemble the measurements for the new species P. pomaceoides. The description below is derived from Zhou, L.-W.(1). RANGE Zhou, L.-W.(1) say that the collections of P. pomaceus from Europe, North America, and eastern Asia were dispersed into 3 clades strictly corresponding to their geographic distribution (P. pomaceus in Europe, P. pomaceoides in North America, P. orientoasiaticus in eastern Asia), the last two newly described in the article.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  effused reflexed or forming a cap that is "triangular in longitudinal section", projecting up to 2cm, 6cm wide, and 2cm thick at the center, with obtuse margin; cap surface light gray; minutely velutinate [velvety] to bald, indistinctly crustose, becoming cracked when old
FLESH  up to 0.7cm thick, woody hard; dark brown to fuscous
PORES  pores 6-9 per mm, round to sinuous, with distinctly thick dissepiments, "the thickness almost as the diameter of pores", pore surface grayish brown, slightly glancing, margin of pore surface up to 0.15cm wide and cinnamon to yellowish brown; tubes up to 1.3cm long, cinnamon, woody hard, tube layers stratified, white mycelial strands frequently present in old tubes
STEM  
ODOR  none
TASTE  none
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, single, on dead Prunus
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores (3.5)4-5 x 3-4(4.5) microns, elliptic, smooth, inamyloid (IKI-), colorless, slightly thick-walled^; basidia 4-spored, 8-10 x 5-6.5 microns, barrel-shaped, basidioles similar in shape to basidia; cystidioles absent; setae infrequent in hymenium, 13-20 x 4-6 microns, "subulate, dark brown, thick-walled"; hyphal system dimitic, context generative hyphae 2-3 microns wide, colorless to pale yellowish, thin-walled to slightly thick-walled, "rarely branched, frequently septate"; context skeletal hyphae 2.5-4.5 microns wide, "dominant, rust-brown, thick-walled with a wide to narrow lumen, unbranched, occasionally septated, straight, loosely interwoven", trama generative hyphae 2-3 microns wide, colorless to pale yellowish, thin-walled to thick-walled, "frequently branched, frequently septate", trama skeletal hyphae 2-3.5 microns, "dominant, rust-brown, thick-walled with a narrow lumen, unbranched, occasionally septate, straight, parallel along the tube"
NAME ORIGIN  referring to the similarities with Phellinus pomaceus
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Zhou, L.-W.(1)

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phellinus pomaceus  (Pers.: Gray) Maire^  Fungi Catal. I. p.37. 1932;  Polyporus pomaceus  Pers.;  Fomes pomaceus  (Pers.: Gray) Lloyd
ENGLISH NAMES  cushion bracket
NOTES  Features include bracket-like or shelf-like fruitbodies that are light gray to light grayish brown to blackish on upper surface, with small, grayish brown to yellowish brown to reddish brown pores, and growth on living peach trees, cherry trees, plum trees, and other Prunus species. Zhou, L.-W.(1) presented molecular evidence supported by spore sizes that some to all of the Phellinus "pomaceus" in North America is a separate species called Phellinus pomaceoides.^ Zhou, L.-W.(1) describe Phellinus pomaceoides as a new species with pores 6-9 per mm and spores (3.5)4-5 x 3-4(4.5) microns. They say that it resembles P. pomaceus which has pores 4-7 per mm (Niemela 1977) and spores 5.8-6.4 x 4.6-5.0 microns (Niemela 1977). Those measurements for P. pomaceus in Gilbertson & Ryvarden are quite different: 7-9 per mm and 4-5 x 3-4.5 microns which resemble the measurements for the new species P. pomaceoides. Zhou, L.-W.(1) say that the collections of P. pomaceus from Europe, North America, and eastern Asia were dispersed into 3 clades strictly corresponding to their geographic distribution (P. pomaceus in Europe, P. pomaceoides in North America, P. orientoasiaticus in east Asia), the last two newly described in the article. The description below is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where noted. RANGE Phellinus pomaceus has been reported from Washington, Oregon, Idaho, Manitoba, Ontario, Colorado, Georgia, Iowa, Indiana, Kansas, Maine, Maryland, Michigan, Minnesota, Mississippi, Missouri, Montana, Nebraska, New Mexico, North Carolina, North Dakota, Ohio, Pennsylvania, South Carolina, South Dakota, Tennessee, Virginia, Vermont, West Virginia, Wyoming, and Europe, (Gilbertson(1)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  bracket-like or bent outward from pore surface to form shelf-like cap, cap hoof-shaped, 5cm x 5cm x 6cm, at first light grayish brown, becoming blackened; at first smooth, lightly tomentose, becoming cracked and bald
FLESH  up to 1cm thick, woody; zoned, yellowish brown to reddish brown, shining
PORES  7-9 per mm, circular, thick-walled; light yellowish brown to reddish brown; tube layers distinctly stratified, yellowish brown to reddish brown, "tubes dull, becoming white within", (Gilbertson), 4-7 per mm (Zhou, L.-W.(1) following Niemela 1977)
STEM  none
ODOR  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
TASTE  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, virtually restricted to Prunus, reported on Crataegus, associated with uniform white rot of the heartwood of living fruit trees^, (Gilbertson), all year (Buczacki), single or in small groups "on living plum trees", (Desjardin)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white to yellowish^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4-5 x 3-4.5 microns, oval to broadly elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 9-10 x 5-5.6 microns, broadly clavate, simple-septate at base; setae rare, 14-17 x 4.5-7 microns, subulate [awl-shaped] to ventricose, thick-walled; hyphae of context of 2 types, mostly the first: 1) 2.5-3.5 microns wide, "brownish in KOH, thick-walled, with rare to frequent branching, simple-septate", 2) 2-3 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled, simple-septate; hyphae of trama usually similar, some thin-walled, pale brownish to colorless, (Gilbertson), spores 5.8-6.4 x 4.6-5.0 microns according to Niemela 1977, (Zhou, L.-W.(1))
NAME ORIGIN  means "of fruit trees"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Phellinus pomaceoides has pores 6-9 per mm and spores (3.5)4-5 x 3-4(4.5) microns, whereas P. pomaceus has pores 4-7 per mm according to Niemela 1977 and spores 5.8-6.4 x 4.6-5.0 microns according to Niemela 1977, (Zhou, L.-W.(1)). Note that the pore and spore sizes given for Phellinus pomaceus by Gilbertson(1) are closer to those given for Phellinus pomaceoides by Zhou, L.-W.(1).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Buczacki(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Zhou, L.-W.(1) (discussing Phellinus pomaceoides), Ginns(28)
166
FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phellinus prunicola  (Murr.) Gilb.^  Mycotaxon 9:73. 1979
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include a resupinate, woody fruitbody that has small, circular, reddish brown pores, a margin that is yellowish brown at first becoming blackened, flesh that is not zoned, perennial growth on dead wood of Prunus, and microscopic characters including abundant setae^. Ginns(28) notes that significantly different spore sizes reported for this species suggests that two species have been labeled P. prunicola. The range given by Gilbertson(1) is 3.5-5 x 2.5-3.5 microns and the range given by Parmasto 2007 is 5.2-6.4 x 3.9-4.6 microns. The description here is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where noted. RANGE A collection from BC at DAOM in Ottawa was reidentified by Parmasto while studying the Phellinus laevigatus group as Phellinus prunicola, (J. Ginns, pers. comm.). Collections are noted on the range map from NL, MT and PA, but it probably occurs throughout the northern U.S. and southern Canada without being reported, perhaps because of its similarity to Phellinus laevigatus, (Gilbertson). 
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  growing flat on wood, up to 22cm across, woody, not readily separable, perennial; margin at first pale yellowish brown, becoming blackened, bald and rimose [cracked] when old
FLESH  up to 0.1cm thick, woody; reddish brown, not zoned
PORES  6-8 per millimeter, circular, with thick, entire dissepiments; reddish brown ("snuff brown" to "Saccardo's umber"); "tube layers distinctly stratified, becoming whitish within", each layer up to 0.25cm thick, (Gilbertson), pores 6-9 per mm (Ginns)
STEM  
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  on dead wood of Prunus, causing a uniform white rot
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5.2-6.4 x 3.9-4.6 microns (Parmasto 1987 according to Ginns(28)), 3.5-5 x 2.5-3.5 microns, oval to nearly round, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, (Gilbertson(1))^; basidia 4-spored, 11-12 x 5-6 microns, without clamp connection at base; setae abundant, mostly ventricose, thick-walled, 15-18 x 5-6.5 microns; context hyphae mostly dark reddish brown in KOH, thick-walled to thin-walled, with rare branching, aseptate, 2.5-4 microns wide, some pale brownish to colorless, thin-walled, with frequent branching, 2-3 microns wide, septa without clamp connections; trama hyphae similar, arrangement of skeletals semiparallel, (Gilbertson(1)), generative hyphae 2-3 microns wide, thin-walled, "hyaline to pale brown", skeletal hyphae 2.5-4.0 microns wide, thin- to thick-walled, dark reddish brown; setae abundant, mostly ventricose, 16-22 x 4.5-6.8 microns, (Ginns(28)).
NAME ORIGIN  means 'living on Prunus'
SIMILAR  Phellinus laevigatus has a wider host range and prefers Betula - spores are smaller than the Parmasto measurement for P. prunicola, pores are 8-10 per mm, and setae are 6-9 microns in diam (Ginns(28)). Resupinate specimens of Fomitiporia robusta have larger, dextrinoid spores and few or no setae, (Gilbertson). Phellinus pomaceus is restricted to live as well as dead Prunus, and has sessile or reflexed fruitbodies with small (14-17 x 4.5-7.0 microns), rare setae (Ginns(28)). Phellinus pomaceoides, which represents some to all of what has been called P. pomaceus, has sessile or reflexed fruitbodies and infrequent setae in the hymenium (measuring 13-20 x 4-6 microns).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (colors in quotation marks from Ridgway(1)), Ginns(28)*
167
FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phellinus tremulae  (Bondartsev) Bondartsev & Borissov^  in Bondartsev, Polyporaceae Eur., USSR and Caucasia. p. 358. 1953;  Fomes igniarius forma tremulae  Bondartsev
ENGLISH NAMES  aspen bracket, false tinder conk
NOTES  This is a member of the Phellinus igniarius complex according to Zhou, L.-W.(1). Features include 1) a bracket-like fruitbody on living aspen, attached to branch scars by a granular core of tissue, 2) the upper surface (at about 45 degrees) pale brown tomentose, becoming black, crusted and cracked, 3) the pore surface at about 45 degrees and purplish brown with small circular pores, 4) tubes that are stuffed with white mycelium, and 5) microscopic characters.^ RANGE Phellinus tremulae has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NT, NB, NF, NS, ON, PQ, SK, YT, AK, AZ, CA, CO, CT, IA, IL, IN, MA, MD, ME, MI, MN, MT, ND, NH, NM, NV, NY, OH, PA, SD, UT, VA, VT, WI, WV, and WY, (Gilbertson). It also occurs in Europe (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 20cm wide and 15cm thick, bracket-like, with pore surface and upper surface at about 45 degrees, woody, attached to wood "by a granular core of tissue that is continuous into the decayed branch stub"; pale brown near margin, soon becoming blackened; finely tomentose at first, soon crust-like and cracked^, (Gilbertson), 5-12cm along wood and projecting out 5-10cm, bracket-like to hoof-shaped, sometimes shelf-like or completely flat; gray to black; zoned, smooth, crustose, cracked when old; margin lighter and sharp, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  woody, with a granular core at the place of attachment; dark reddish brown^, (Gilbertson), 0.2-0.5cm thick, corky; dark brown; where attached with "dark brown, white-marbled, friable mycelial core" 1-3cm in size, which is partly embedded in the wood, (Breitenbach)
PORES  5-7 per mm, circular, thick-walled; purplish brown; tube layers indistinctly stratified (each layer up to 0.2cm thick), tubes becoming stuffed with white mycelium^, (Gilbertson), 5-6 per mm, rounded-angular, gray-brown to tobacco brown or dark brown; tube layer up to 2.5cm, tubes distinctly layered, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  sweet wintergreen, (Gilbertson), similar to methyl salicylate (Breitenbach from Romagnesi), indistinct (Buczacki)
TASTE  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, developing at branch scars, only on Populus tremuloides (Quaking Aspen) and P. grandidentata (Bigtooth Aspen), associated with a white trunk rot of living trees, and "a major decay fungus throughout the range of aspen"^, (Gilbertson), mostly on standing aspen or on other Populus species, according to literature rarely on Alnus, Quercus, Sorbus, (Breitenbach for Europe), all year (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4.5-5 x 4-4.5 microns, nearly round, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, "with thickened wall at maturity"^; basidia 4-spored, 8-10 x 6-7 microns, broadly ovoid, simple-septate at base; setae few to numerous, 12-30 x 6-7.5 microns, projecting to 15 microns, ventricose to subulate [awl-shaped], brown in KOH, thick-walled; hyphae of context of 2 types: 1) 2-3 microns wide, pale yellowish to almost colorless, thin-walled, simple-septate, 2) 4-6 microns, dark reddish brown, thick-walled; granular core "composed of densely interwoven, branching hyphae with clusters of thick-walled, dark reddish brown, irregularly shaped and contorted cells"; hyphae of trama with parallel arrangement, (Gilbertson), spores 4.8-5.6 x 3.5-5 microns, nearly round, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, thick-walled; setae common, 16-25 x 7-9 microns, brown, thick-walled, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  means "growing on quaking aspen (Populus tremula)"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Phellinus igniarius differs "primarily by a non-decurrent tube layer, somewhat larger spores, and occurrence on other hardwoods", (Breitenbach). See also SIMILAR section of Phellinus pomaceus.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Breitenbach(2)*, Buczacki(1)*, Ginns(28)*, Zhou, L.-W.(1)

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phellopilus nigrolimitatus  (Romell) Niemela, T. Wagner & M. Fisch.^  in Niemela, Wagner, Fischer & Dai, Ann. bot. fenn. 38(1): 54. 2001;  Phellinus nigrolimitatus  (Romell) Bourdot & Galzin;  Polyporus nigrolimitatus  Romell;  Fomes nigrolimitatus  (Romell) Egeland
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) blackish brown to yellowish or reddish brown shelf-like caps, (sometimes the pore surface entirely flat on wood), 2) a marginal zone in the first year that is tomentose and light brown, in succeeding years becoming blackish, distinct, and wider, 3) small cinnamon pores, 4) black layers in the context, and 5) microscopic characters including setae and distinctive carrot-shaped spores.^ RANGE Phellopilus nigrolimitatus has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, AK, AZ, CA, CO, MT, NM, UT, and WY, (Gilbertson). It also occurs in Europe (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  shelf-like cap bent outward from pore surface growing flat on wood, or entirely flat on wood, caps irregular in shape, more or less elongated, 5cm x 10cm x 4cm, "upper surface blackish brown to yellowish or reddish brown, finely tomentose to bald, very irregular, often soft and spongy"; margin up to 0.3cm, colored as pore surface or sometimes lighter^, (Gilbertson), growing flat on wood, more rarely shelf-like, with upper surface dark red-brown to black, concentrically undulating, verrucose, tuberculate; marginal zone of 1 year old pore surface sterile, tomentose, light brown, blackish brown in those several years old, marginal zone becomes increasingly broad and sharply bounded as each subsequent pore surface smaller in extent, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  up to 1cm thick, corky to soft-fibrous, "dark yellowish brown, with one or more thin black layers that appear as black lines in longitudinal sections, often zonate"^, (Gilbertson), up to 0.5cm thick, corky, fragile, light in weight when dry; red-brown to rust-brown, with a black-brown undulating line in cross-section, (Breitenbach)
PORES  5-7 per mm, circular to angular, sometimes daedaloid [maze-like] in parts; thick-walled; cinnamon; smooth; tube layers indistinct, each layer up to 0.2cm thick, lighter brown than flesh^, (Gilbertson), 5-6 per mm, rounded, gray-brown to chestnut brown or red-brown; tube layers up to 0.5cm thick, total up to 2cm with barely visible layering, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, on conifers, especially Picea (spruce)^, associated with white pocket rot of conifer logs, and also a butt rot of living trees, the rot being distinctive because of the large size of the pockets (up to 2.5cm long) with firm wood between, (Gilbertson), single to gregarious, on very rotten wood of Picea (spruce), usually on the underside of fallen trunks, and other conifers, also on wood used in construction, (Breitenbach)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-10 x 2-2.5 microns, cylindric, straight, tapering toward the apex, ("carrot-shaped"), smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 10-12 x 5-6.5 microns, broadly clavate, simple-septate at base; setae abundant, 25-37 x 6.5-8.5 microns, subulate [awl-shaped] to ventricose, straight, dark brown in KOH, thick-walled; hyphae 2.5-6 microns wide, brown to pale yellow or almost colorless in KOH, thick-walled or thin-walled, with frequent branching, simple-septate; "black layers about 20-80 microns thick, composed of very closely interwoven and agglutinated hyphae"; hyphae of trama similar to those of lower context, (Gilbertson), spores 5.5-6.5 x 1.8-2.5 microns, cylindric with laterally spurred apiculus, smooth, inamyloid, colorless; setae 25-30 x 6-7 microns, with bent base, thick-walled, exserted beyond hymenium, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  means "bounded or limited by a black line or layer"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Phellinus nigrolimitatus), Breitenbach(2)* (as Phellinus nigrolimitatus), Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Meruliaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phlebia tremellosa  (Schrad.: Fr.) Nakasone & Burds.^  Mycotaxon 21: 245. 1984;  Merulius tremellosus  Schrad.
ENGLISH NAMES  gelatinous woodcrust, trembling Merulius
NOTES  Also listed in Crusts category. Features include 1) resupinate growth (often partly reflexed to form caps) on wood, more often hardwood, 2) caps when present shelf-like, projecting 1-5cm, often shingled, sometimes fused into formless clusters, soft-cartilaginous (horny when dry), the upper surface whitish to yellowish or pinkish, azonate or somewhat zonate, and tomentose to hairy, 3) a spore-bearing surface that is waxy, soft, gelatinous, translucent, pale orange-yellow to deep orange-red, and merulioid, with folds narrow, radial, branching to less prominent side ridges, sometimes forming nearly rectangular pore-like pits, 1 or 2 per millimeter, 4) spores that are cylindric to allantoid, smooth, inamyloid, and colorless, 5) cystidioles that are cylindric, projecting, and often encrusted, and 6) a monomitic hyphal system, the hyphae with clamp connections. RANGE It is common from the southern US to the tree line in Canada (Ginns(12)). Phlebia tremellosa has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NB, NS, ON, PQ, SK, AR, CA, CT, FL, GA, IA, IN, LA, MA, MD, MI, MN, MO, MT, NC, NY, OH, TN, VA, WI, and WV, (Ginns(5)). Besides the United States and Canada, it is widespread in Europe, and also found in Brazil, Uruguay, Morocco, China (including Tibet), India, Japan, Siberia in Russia, and Pakistan, (Ginns(12)). It occurs in Scandinavia (Eriksson) and Switzerland (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  flat on substrate or partly reflexed to form shelf-like caps, often imbricate [shingled], separable when fresh, often confluent, usually 5cm x 3cm but up to 25cm x 10cm, and 0.5cm thick, where caps present soft and cartilaginous (drying horny) and upper surface white to pallid, azonate, and tomentose to hirsute^, (Ginns(12)), resupinate, orbicular [circular] and confluent, spreading out and reaching a size of several decimeters across, or dimidiate-pileate white caps 1-5cm broad in radial direction, "often elongated lengthwise, now and then imbricate, carnose tremellose when alive and wet", shrunken and with a horny spore-bearing surface when dry; "upperside white, tomentose-strigose, often somewhat zonate", the margin tomentose or hispid [bristly], (Eriksson), "on vertical and laterally extending substrates semipileate to pileate", the caps usually in band-like rows several centimeters to decimeters long, projecting 2-4cm from substrate, sometimes also fused into formless clusters, upper surface white to yellowish with a pink tint, hairy to hispid (from white tomentum), undulating, margin sinuous, white to somewhat translucent, (Breitenbach) 
FLESH  context up to 0.3cm thick, cartilaginous and water-soaked when fresh, (when dry cottony), white to pallid, often striated with waxy hyphal strands^, (Ginns(12)), consistency soft, elastic, gelatinous, (Breitenbach)
PORES  spore-bearing surface translucent, waxy, when young or fresh pale orange-yellow to deep orange-red, (when dried or in older specimens usually blood red), the folds narrow, 0.05-0.15cm deep, "continuous nearly to the margin, radiating, branching to acute side ridges or dichotomously, occasionally forming elongated and nearly rectangular pits", 1 or 2 per millimeter; margin of resupinate part up to 0.2cm wide, waxy, fimbriate [fringed], translucent, pale yellow to orange-red, occasionally darker^, (Ginns(12)), spore-bearing surface watery grayish or sallowly ochraceous when young and fresh, turning reddish when mature, (when dried dark ochraceous, sometimes orange or even red), "reticulately plicate (merulioid) with irregular alveoles", radial ridges often dominating over the tangential ridges, in fully developed specimens more or less poroid with fertile ridges between the pores; margin radially fibrillose when resupinate, (Eriksson), spore-bearing surface merulioid, reticulate-porose, or radially ribbed, "yellow, orange, to salmon-pink, dark orange when old", (Breitenbach)
STEM  
ODOR  insignificant^ (Breitenbach)
TASTE  insignificant^ (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  saprophytic on a wide variety of hardwoods and conifers, associated with a white rot, common in fall^, (Ginns(12)), on decayed stumps and fallen logs of hardwood (normally) or coniferous wood, (Eriksson), commonly on stumps and fallen trunks, (Breitenbach), on birch, maple, and other hardwoods, rarely on conifer wood; July to January, (Lincoff(2))
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Lincoff(2))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 3.5-4.5 x 1-1.5(2) microns, cylindric, in side view allantoid to reniform (kidney-shaped), smooth, IKI-, colorless, very pale blue in lactic-blue, thin-walled, some containing 2 droplets^; basidia 17-28 x 3-5 microns, of the chiastobasidium type, narrowly clavate; subhymenium and hymenium of some specimens with granular deposits throughout; hyphae monomitic, context of 2 layers, 1) the abhymenial layer with hyphae 2-5.5 microns wide, randomly oriented, rather loosely woven, colorless, rather thin-walled to thick-walled, with clamp connections, lacking granular deposits, with interspersed waxy strands, 2) abruptly (closer to hymenium) the hyphae become "closely packed, parallel, horizontally oriented", this hyphal layer extending to the hymenium; "at the base of the folds the hyphae are randomly oriented, serpentine, more widely spaced, becoming flexuous and loosely woven in the folds", these hyphae having walls rather thin and with colorless, apparently gelatinous droplets up to 2 microns in diameter, scattered over their exterior, (Ginns(12)), spores 4-4.5 x 1-1.5 microns, allantoid, smooth, thin-walled, often with 2 droplets; basidia 4-spored, 18-25 x 4-4.5 microns, narrowly clavate, in a very dense palisade, with a basal clamp connection (new basidia born from the clamp connection); cystidia not present, but often there are thin-walled hyphae in the hymenium, projecting 20-30 microns, often covered by pieces of excreted resinous matter; hyphae monomitic, with clamp connections; context of 2 layers: 1) the abhymenial layer white and cottony in section, with distinct hyphae 4-5 microns wide, much branched in all directions, with thickened walls, "forming a net-like tissue, penetrated by strands of parallel hyphae", and 2) layer closer to the hymenium with hyphae 2-3(4) microns wide, thin-walled, irregularly intertwined into a ceraceous context (forming a layer of parallel hyphae instead in the cap trama); subhymenium thickening, formed by hyphae 2-3 microns wide, thin-walled, richly branched, in an almost pseudoparenchymatic tissue, (Eriksson), spores 3.5-4 x 1(1.5) microns, cylindric, allantoid, smooth, iodine negative, colorless, some with droplets; basidia 4-spored, 20-25 x 3-4 microns, narrowly clavate, with basal clamp connection; cystidioles 30-45 x 3-4 microns, cylindric, some incrusted; hyphae monomitic, 2-4 microns wide, with clamp connections; hairs of upper surface 2-3.5 microns wide, colorless, with clamp connections, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin meaning "trembling", because of gelatinous appearance^, (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  Byssomerulius corium lacks clamp connections (Ginns(12)). Phlebia rufa has spores 2-3 microns wide, and gloeocystidia, (Ginns(12)). Gelatoporia dichroa can rarely be similar but has narrower spores and the spore-bearing surface is usually distinctly poroid and pale purple, the pores 5-7 per millimeter, (Ginns(12)). Phlebia incarnata (Schwein.) Nakasone & Burdsall, found in eastern North America as far west as Texas and Oklahoma, has 1) spores 2-3 microns wide, 2) resinous or other deposits in the abhymenial hyphal layer of the context, 3) a few refractive hyphae in the context, 4) heavy deposits of yellowish granules at the base of the folds, and 5) a comparatively broad granule-impregnated zone adjoining the subhymenium, (Ginns(12)). Byssomerulius ambiguus favors conifers, (Lincoff(2)). Most Phlebia species lack a pore-like surface, (Lincoff(2)). Serpula species have dark spores (Lincoff(2)).
SOURCES  Ginns(12) (as Merulius tremellosus), Eriksson(4) (as Merulius tremellosus), Breitenbach(2)* (as Merulius tremellosus), Lincoff(2)* (as Merulius tremellosus), Lincoff(1)* (as Merulius tremellosus), Courtecuisse(1)* (as Merulius tremellosus), Ginns(5), McKnight(1)* (as Merulius tremellosa), Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phylloporia ribis  (Fr.) Ryvarden^  Polyp. N. Europe 2: 371. 1978;  Polyporus ribis  Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include bracket-like fruitbodies growing on living Ribes (currants and gooseberries), the upper surface yellowish to rusty brown to blackish, the flesh including a black line, and small pores that are cinnamon to fulvous or rusty brown.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1). RANGE Phylloporia ribis has been found in WA, OR, ID, NS, ON, AZ, CA, IN, KS, LA, MD, MN, MT, ND, NJ, NM, NY, OH, PA, UT, and WY, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  broadly bracket-like, semicircular and often enclosing the stem on which it grows, compound fruitbodies often arising when single fruitbodies fuse together, cap applanate [flattened] to nodular, soft when fresh but flexible to woody hard when dry; "when actively growing with a yellowish margin", when old "rusty brown and finally dark brown to almost black, frequently greenish in older parts because of algae"; surface with broad grooved zones, in fused fruitbodies more irregular with grooved bands, at first tomentose and spongy, later more compact and smoother; margin of pore surface lighter in color than pores and in growing specimens light yellowish and finely velvety
FLESH  dense; cinnamon to fulvous; shiny when broken; upper soft and spongy tomentum may be up to 0.8cm thick, is mostly lighter in color than flesh, and is separated from flesh by a distinct black line absent only in marginal area
PORES  6-7 per mm, round; cinnamon to fulvous or rusty brown; tube layer up to 0.5cm thick, indistinctly stratified, colored as pore surface
STEM  none
ODOR  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
TASTE  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  perennial, on living deciduous bushes and trees, most common on Ribes (currants and gooseberries), but also known from other genera, causing a white heartrot of living shrubs^, (Gilbertson), on wood of living trunks of spindle, currant, and occasionally other hardwood trees and shrubs, (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 3-4.5 x 2.5-3 microns, broadly elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, pale yellowish, slightly thick-walled^; basidia 4-spored, 10-15 x 4-5 microns, clavate, simple septate at base; setae none; hyphae monomitic, generative hyphae with simple septa, thin-walled to thick-walled and sparingly branched except in subhymenium, in the cap tomentum "2-5 microns wide, light yellowish to light rusty brown, sparingly branched and with scattered septa", in the context and trama 3-7 microns wide, dark rusty brown, more thick-walled, in the subhymenium 1.5-3 microns wide, colorless and thin-walled
NAME ORIGIN  means "on Ribes"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Meripilaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Physisporinus rivulosus  (Berk. & M.A. Curtis) Ryvarden^  Mycotaxon 20(2): 353. 1984 [as 'rivulosa'];  Ceriporiopsis rivulosa  (Berk. & M.A. Curtis) Gilb. & Ryvarden  North American Polypores p. 194. 1986;  Polyporus rivulosus  Berk. & M.A. Curtis;  Poria albipellucida  D.V. Baxter;  Obba rivulosa  (Berk. & M.A. Curtis) Miett. & Rajchenb.,  Mycol. Progr. 11(1): 142. 2012 (proposed new name)
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include flat growth on conifer wood with the pores exposed, a white pore surface, and microscopic characters including relatively large nearly round spores.^ It is the most important butt rot of mature Western Red-cedar in the coastal forests (Allen). The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where indicated. RANGE Physisporinus rivulosus has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AZ. CA, MI, MN, MT, NM, SC, and Cuba, (Gilbertson). It also occurs in AK (Ginns), and Europe.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, up to 0.3cm thick, brittle when dry, annual; margin white
FLESH  up to 0.1cm thick, ochraceous brown
PORES  3-4 per mm, angular; white, drying cream to pale ochraceous brown; tubes becoming resinous dense and partly translucent
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  slightly bitter
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on dead conifers and also in living Redwood, and Western Red-cedar, causes white ring rot, (Gilbertson), on live and dead Abies spp. (true fir), Thuja plicata (Western Red-cedar), and Pseudotsuga menziesii, causing a white ring rot, (Ginns)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5-6 x 4-5 microns, nearly round to oval, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 15-20 x 6-8 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia absent, but cystidioles present among the basidia, 15-20 x 5-8 microns, fusoid, colorless, smooth; hyphal system monomitic: "generative hyphae with clamps, irregularly branched, hyaline, 3-6 microns in diam, those of the context similar, often agglutinated and difficult to separate in preparations"
NAME ORIGIN  means "with fine, wavy channels or grooves"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Physisporinus sanguinolentus and Physisporinus vitreus also have cartilaginous fruitbodies, broadly rounded spores and fusoid cystidioles, but do not have clamp connections. P. sanguinolentus has pores 8-10 per mm stains rapidly rusty red to brown (also occurs in P. rivulosus but the discoloration faint). P. vitreus has pores 4-6 per mm and the pore surface is distinctly bluish white, bruising slowly weak reddish to pale brown or not at all. (Ginns(28)).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Ceriporiopsis rivulosa), Ginns(28)*, Allen(1)

FAMILY  Meripilaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Physisporinus sanguinolentus  (Alb. & Schwein.: Fr.) Pilat^  Atlas Champ. Europe 3: 247. 1940;  Polyporus sanguinolentus  Alb. & Schwein.: Fr.;  Rigidoporus sanguinolentus  (Fr.) Donk;  Podoporia sanguinolenta  (Alb. & Schwein.: Fr.) Hoehn.
ENGLISH NAMES  bleeding porecrust
NOTES  Physisporinus sanguinolentus forms a whitish pore surface with pores 8-10 per mm (growing flat on wood) that quickly shows rusty blotches after collecting or bruising. According to Ginns(28), the fruitbodies shrink significantly and discolor on drying to gray or black, the color change distinguishing this species from other polypores in BC that grow flat on wood.^ RANGE Physisporinus sanguinolentus has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, ON, AK, AZ, CA, FL, LA, MN, MT, NM, NY, and PA, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, spread over an area up to 20cm across, "soft to tough, cartilaginous and crisp when fresh, drying rigid, readily separable"; margin fertile or narrowly sterile, then up to 0.1cm wide, drying pale tan, and fimbriate [fringed]^, (Gilbertson), growing flat on wood, forming extensive patches, waxy and watery; margin distinctly bounded, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  less than 0.1cm thick, cartilaginous, "white when fresh, pale tan when dried"^, (Gilbertson)
PORES  8-10 per mm, circular to angular, thick-walled; "white or ivory when fresh, quickly showing bright rusty red blotches after collecting, eventually becoming brown, grayish to blackish on drying"; tube layer up to 0.5cm thick, "ivory to pale tan, brittle when dry"^, (Gilbertson), 3-5 per mm, rounded to angular; "white, when old cream-whitish, spotted, soon discoloring reddish when handled and later turning brown"; tube layer 0.1-0.2cm thick, (Breitenbach), rapidly bruising rusty red to brown or black (Ginns)
STEM  none
ODOR  weak^ (Breitenbach)
TASTE  mild^ (Gilbertson, Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, or reviving a second year, on dead wood of conifers and hardwoods, causing a white rot^, (Gilbertson), on moist dead wood of hardwoods and conifers, "commonly on stumps and then often growing over surrounding plant remains and soil", (Breitenbach), summer to fall (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5-6 x 4-4.5 microns, oval to nearly round, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 12-14 x 6.5-8 microns, broadly clavate, simple-septate at base; cystidia none, "fusoid cystidioles present, 15-19 x 5-6 microns, simple-septate at the base"; hyphae monomitic, hyphae of subiculum 3.5-6.5 microns wide, colorless in KOH, thick-walled to thin-walled, rarely branched, simple-septate, hyphae of trama 2-4 microns wide, similar, (Gilbertson), spores 5-6 microns in diameter, nearly round, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, with droplets; cystidioles inconspicuous, in the hymenium 12-25 x 5-7 microns, fusiform, smooth; "in addition occasional cystidialike hyphal ends with incrusted tips", 3-5 microns wide, thin-walled, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  means "bloody, full of blood"^, (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Physisporinus vitreus has a distinct bluish white semitransparent color, changes only slowly pale brown (or not at all) when bruised, and has larger pores, (Gilbertson). Physisporinus rivulosus has pores 3-4 per mm and clamp connections: it bruises rusty red to brown but the discoloration is faint, (Ginns). Dried fruitbodies of Rigidoporus crocatus "are sometimes similar but are much harder and not white to ivory when fresh and usually retain their pinkish coloration after drying", (Gilbertson).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Breitenbach(2)*, Ginns(28)*, Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Meripilaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Physisporinus vitreus  (Pers.: Fr.) P. Karst.^  Finl. Basidsv. p.324. 1889;  Polyporus vitreus  Pers.: Fr.;  Poria vitrea  Pers.;  Poria albirosea  Murrill;  Poria holoseparans  Murrill;  Rigidoporus vitreus  (Fr.) Donk;  Podoporia vitrea  (Fr.) Donk
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include a distinct bluish semitransparent pore surface growing flat on wood and often surrounding plant remains and soil, the surface not changing color when bruised or only slowly becoming pale brown.^ RANGE Physisporinus vitreus has found in BC, WA, ID, ON, AK, FL, GA, LA, MS, NC, NY, PA, and VA, (Gilbertson). It also occurs in Europe and Asia (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  growing flat on wood, widely spread out, up to 0.5cm thick, "waxy and soft when fresh, hard and cartilaginous when dry, often curled and partly shrunken"^, (Gilbertson), growing flat on wood, forming extensive patches, wax-like and cartilaginous, when dry corneous and hard, laterally spreading patches often with knot-like protuberances; sometimes fungus is bound with brown rhizomorphs, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  0.2-0.5cm thick, dense; pale brown when dry^, (Gilbertson)
PORES  4-6 per mm, round to angular (somewhat smaller when dried); "white to bluish white and translucent when fresh, ochraceous to pale pinkish brown when dry, slightly staining when touched or bruised, but reaction slow and variable"; tube layer up to 0.5cm thick, colored as pore surface^, (Gilbertson), 3-6 per mm, rounded, cream whitish becoming ocherish when old, not discoloring when handled; tube layer 0.2-0.4cm thick, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  unpleasant^ (Breitenbach)
TASTE  mild^ (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on dead wood of hardwoods, rarely conifers, associated with a white pocket rot^, (Gilbertson), on moist dead wood of hardwoods and conifers, "commonly on stumps and then often growing over surrounding plant remains and soil", (Breitenbach), summer to fall (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5-6 x 4-5 microns, oval to round, inamyloid, colorless, thin-walled, often with an oil droplet^; basidia 4-spored, 12-15 x 6-8 microns, broadly clavate, simple-septate at base; cystidia absent, "fusoid cystidioles present, 15-20 x 5-6 microns, simple-septate at the base"; hyphae monomitic, hyphae of subiculum 3-6 microns wide, colorless in KOH, thick-walled to thin-walled, sparingly branched, simple-septate; hyphae of trama 2-4 microns wide, similar, (Gilbertson), spores 4.5-5(5.5) microns in diameter, nearly round, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, with droplets; cystidia-like hyphal ends numerous, mostly toward the base of tubes, 4.5-5.5 microns wide, cylindric, thick-walled, with apical crystals, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  means "like glass, glassy"^, (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Physisporinus sanguinolentus "has a more normal whitish color when fresh and rapidly becomes reddish and then black when bruised", and has smaller pores^, (Gilbertson). P. sanguinolentus also has a softer consistency and somewhat larger spores, (Breitenbach). Physisporinus rivulosus has pores 3-4 per mm and clamp connections (Ginns).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Breitenbach(2)*, Ginns(28)*, Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Piptoporus betulinus  (Bull.: Fr.) P. Karst.^  Medd. Soc. Fauna Fl. Fenn. 6: 9. 1881;  Polyporus betulinus  Bull.: Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  birch polypore, birch conk, razorstrop fungus
NOTES  Piptoporus betulinus forms a kidney-shaped to round, whitish to brownish bracket with a curb-like extension of margin below the level of the pore surface. It is found only on birch and is common in that habitat.^ It has been used as tinder, as a razor strop, and as an anesthetic, (Phillips). According to Ginns(28) the medicinal properties attributed to it include boosting the immune system and actions against inflammation, viruses, and bacteria. RANGE Piptoporus betulinus has been found in BC, WA, ID, AB, MB, NB, NS, NT, ON, PE, PQ, SK, YT, AK, CT, IA, KS, MA, ME, MI, MN, MT, NC, NH, NJ, NY, PA, TN, VT, WI, and WV, and circumboreally, (Gilbertson). It also occurs in Europe and Asia (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  (2.5)5-25cm broad, 2-6(10)cm thick, shelf-like or hoof-like, kidney-shaped or nearly round in outline, convex to nearly flat, "tough or corky when fresh, rigid and hard when dry"; white to buff, tan, brown, or grayish brown; covered by thin, smooth or suede-like crust that "often breaks up into scales or flat patches or wears away, revealing the whitish undersurface"; margin thick, blunt, inrolled with curb-like projection down below level of pore surface^, (Arora), up to 15cm x 25cm x 6cm, usually dimidiate [roughly semicircular] or kidney-shaped; whitish to mouse-colored or brownish, not zoned; usually breaking up to appear pitted or scaly, bald; margin usually extending down below pore surface, (Gilbertson), 5-20(30)cm along wood, projecting 5-20cm, 2-5cm thick, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  thick, punky or corky; white^, (Arora), up to 5cm thick, "tough when fresh, drying soft-corky", azonate; white, (Gilbertson)
PORES  2-4 per mm, sometimes becoming tooth-like; white, when old becoming pale brown or grayish brown; tube layer 0.2-1cm thick^, (Arora), 3-5 per mm, circular to angular, walls thick but split and clump together when old to give a toothed appearance; white at first, becoming pale brownish when old; tube layer up to 1cm thick, easily separated from flesh when fresh, (Gilbertson)
STEM  absent or present as stubby extension of cap (lateral or attached to top)^, (Arora), if present, up to 6cm long and 5cm thick; whitish to brown; bald, often resinous, (Gilbertson)
ODOR  strong and pleasant, (Phillips), none (Lincoff(1)), pleasant (Breitenbach)
TASTE  slightly bitter^ (Phillips), bitterish (Lincoff(1)), somewhat sour and bitter (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  no (Phillips)^, edible when young according to McIlvaine, but tough, (Arora)
HABITAT  annual, single or in groups or columns on dead or sometimes living birch trees^, (Arora), on birch, especially in North America Betula papyrifera (Paper Birch) and B. alleghaniensis (Yellow Birch), associated with brown cubical rot of the sapwood of dead birch, (Gilbertson), parasitic on birch, rarely on beech, (Lincoff(1)), fruiting in spring, summer, and fall, (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5-6 x 1.5-1.7 microns, cylindric, slightly curved, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 10-12 x 5-6 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia absent; hyphal system di-trimitic, generative hyphae in context 2.5-4 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled, with clamp connections, rarely branched, skeletal hyphae of context 2.5-5 microns wide, colorless in KOH, "thick-walled, nonseptate, some much branched, others with rare or occasional branching", hyphae of trama similar, (Gilbertson), spores 3-6 x 1.5-2 microns, cylindric to sausage-shaped, smooth, (Arora)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'pertaining to birch'
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Arora(1)*, Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Ginns(28)*, Lincoff(1)*, Miller(14)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)*, Breitenbach(2)*, Bacon(1)*, Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Albatrellaceae, Order Russulales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Polyporoletus sublividus  Snell  Mycologia 28: 467. 1936  group^;  Polyporus canaliculatus  Overh.;  Polyporus sylvestris  Overh.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include dark purplish gray cap that fades to brownish, a non-separable pore surface that is purplish gray to avellaneous or olivaceous when old, a lateral to central brown to olivaceous reticulate stem, growth on ground, and spores that have a double layered wall with pillars or partitions between the walls^. |Polyporoletus sublividus was described from Tennessee in Snell(4). This name has been used for years in the Pacific Northwest based on Gilbertson(1). But Luther(3) summarizes a taxonomic uncertainty, "The original (type) collection for Polyporoletus sylvestris was found near Lake Cowichan, Vancouver Island, BC (Canada) in 1929. Polyporus sylvestris was invalidly published by Overholts (1941), who failed to provide a Latin description. Pouzar (1972) described it correctly and transferred it to Albatrellus as A. sylvestris Overholts ex Pouzar. It was listed as a synonym of P. sublividus by Gilbertson & Ryvarden (1986), but the current research by Audet (2010) provides evidence that it is a distinct species, which he transfers to Polyporoletus as P. sylvestris (Overh. ex Pouzar) Audet." |Audet(1) gives a chart differentiating the features of Polyporoletus sublividus and Polyporoletus sylvestris. In addition, Audet(1) describes the new species Polyporoletus bulbosus based on a WA specimen collected by A.H. Smith (30718 in 1948) In his key, Audet separates Polyporoletus bulbosus and P. sylvestris from Polyporoletus sublividus sensu lato on the basis of spore width: about 7.2-10.8 microns for the first two and 9.6-12.6 microns for the third taxon (spores for the holotype of P. sublividus (9)10.2-12 microns wide). Polyporoletus bulbosus is then separated from P. sylvestris on the basis of solitary habit and bulbous stem in the former and cespitose habit and non-bulbous stem in the latter. (Audet(1)). |Interestingly the description of Polyporus sylvestris in the original paper by Overholts involves not only the type but a second A.H. Smith collection (3189 in 1935): "Unfortunately the type collection was unaccompanied by notes descriptive of their fresh condition. That collection was turned over to Dr. Mounce at Ottawa who assisted in the diagnosis. Good notes by Smith on fresh condition of his plants have been incorporated here also." (Overholts(3)). |Overholts also described Polyporus canaliculatus from North Carolina and Tennessee (an invalid name because of an earlier homonym) which was synonymized by Lowe with Polyporus sylvestris. Spores were 8-12 x 8-10 microns, and the fruitbody was single with stem equal or tapering upward (Overholts(2)). |RANGE The range of Polyporoletus sublividus is given by Gilbertson(1) as WA, ID, NC, and TN, (Gilbertson), and the holotype of Polyporus sylvestris is from BC. Ginns(28) says that Polyporus sylvestris is known from BC, WA, OR, and ID.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 18cm across and 2.5cm thick, circular to kidney-shaped or lobed; dark purplish gray, fading to cinnamon buff to olivaceous tawny, not zoned, in some specimens almost black after drying; tomentose to fibrillose, in some specimens with fibrils becoming appressed in a reticulate pattern, in others bald^, (Gilbertson), 5cm across, nearly flat; livid olive, reddish purple on the margin; tomentose on the margin, fibrillose elsewhere, nearly bald on disc, (Snell)
FLESH  up to 1.5cm thick, soft and friable; not zoned, pinkish buff to ochraceous salmon^, (Gilbertson), fruitbody firm and corky; flesh "pale bluish or violaceous, more reddish-purplish under the cuticle"; in stem spongy, colored like cap flesh, (Snell), flesh is speckled with darker spots, like Oreo cookie crumbs in mild (N. Siegel, Facebook)
PORES  1-2 per mm, circular to angular, with thick walls that become thin and torn when old, decurrent; purplish gray to avellaneous or olivaceous when old; tube layer up to 1cm thick, sharply distinct from flesh but not separable, inner surface of tubes pinkish buff, tissue between the tubes darker^, (Gilbertson), pores large, irregular or more or less uneven, tubes very short, 0.1-0.2cm long, "hardly if at all separable, long-decurrent, at first yellow or ochraceous, later brownish with a purplish tinge", (Snell), gray pores that flash different colors when you move it back and forth (N. Siegel, Facebook)
STEM  up to 10cm x 4cm, lateral to central, cylindric with a bulbous base up to 5.5cm wide; wood brown to olivaceous; reticulate [with net-like pattern], tomentose to finely hispid [bristly]^, (Gilbertson), 4cm long, 1.8-2.5cm thick, eccentric [off-center], almost lateral; "livid to violaceous-purplish, covered with a yellowish-olivaceous velvetiness in places"; basically nearly bald, coarsely reticulate from walls of tubes, in part to base of stem, (Snell)
ODOR  
TASTE  pleasant^ (Snell)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, single, on ground under hardwoods, "apparently not a wood-rotting fungus"^, (Gilbertson), on soil in oak-pine woods (Snell)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 10-12 x 8-10 microns, nearly round to broadly elliptic, "appearing slightly rough, with a double wall separated by interwall pillars or partitions", "outer walls and pillars apparently sloughing off some spores which appear smooth and single walled", inamyloid, colorless, with a large spherical droplet^; basidia 2-4-spored, 30-45 x 11-14 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia none; hyphae monomitic, hyphae of context of 2 types, mostly the first, 1) 5-10 microns wide, colorless, "mostly thin-walled, with clamps and simple septa, with occasionally branching", 2) larger, up to 15 microns wide, unevenly thickened walls; hyphae of trama 2.5-5 microns wide, thin-walled, with clamp connections, also some gloeoplerous hyphae in trama; large globules of dark brown material exude from all tissues, (Gilbertson), spores 7-10 microns in diameter, round, colorless, minutely short-verrucose or echinulate; cystidia scarce, 35-40 x 8-10 microns, clavate, a few slightly rostrate, colorless, (Snell)
NAME ORIGIN  means "somewhat bluish"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Snell(4), Overholts(3), Overholts(2), Luther(3), Audet(1), Ginns(28)* (as Polyporoletus sylvestris)
