FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Polyporus arcularius  Batsch: Fr.^  Syst. Mycol. 1: 342. 1821;  = Lentinus arcularius (Batsch) Zmitr., International Journal of Medicinal Mushrooms 12 (1): 88. 2010
ENGLISH NAMES  spring polypore, fringed polypore
NOTES  Features include a straw to dark brown circular fruitbody with a ciliate margin, large radially elongated hexagonal pores that are cream to buff, central stem, and growth on hardwoods.^ The radially aligned, hexagonal, relatively large pores, centrally depressed cap, central stem, and thin, tough fruitbodies distinguish Polyporus brumalis (see SIMILAR) and this species from other polypores, (Ginns). The online Species Fungorum, accessed September 3, 2018, gives the current name as Lentinus arcularius (Batsch) Zmitr., International Journal of Medicinal Mushrooms (Redding) 12(1): 88 (2010). MycoBank, accessed the same day, gives that name as a synonym of Polyporus arcularius Batsch: Fr. RANGE Polyporus arcularius has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, MB, ON, AL, AZ, CO, CT, DE, FL, GA, IA, IL, IN, KS, KY, LA, MD, MI, MN, MO, MS, MT, NC, NE, NJ, NM, NY, OH, OK, PA, SC, SD, TN, TX, VA, WI, and WV, and it occurs circumglobally, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 2.5cm across and 0.3cm thick, circular; straw-colored to dark brown, not zoned; bald, smooth to rugose [wrinkled]; margin acute, sterile underneath, ciliate [fringed with fine hairs]^, (Gilbertson), 1-8cm, round, convex becoming depressed or umbilicate; golden brown to dark brown; dry, usually minutely scaly; margin often ciliate, (Arora), sometimes rather indented laterally (Breitenbach)
FLESH  less than 0.1cm thick, tough; whitish to buff, not zoned^, (Gilbertson), thin, tough; white, (Arora)
PORES  1-2 per mm, hexagonal, radially aligned, with thin walls that become torn; cream to buff; dull, rough; tube layer up to 0.2cm, colored as flesh and continuous with it^, (Gilbertson), 1-2 per mm, angular or hexagonal, sometimes decurrent; white or yellowish; tubes shallow, (Arora)
STEM  up to 3.5cm x 0.4cm, central, colored as cap; bald^, (Gilbertson), 2-6cm x 0.2-0.4cm, "usually central or slightly off-center, more or less equal"; dark brown to yellowish brown; "smooth or minutely scaly, the base sometimes hairy", (Arora)
ODOR  mushroomy (Lincoff(1)), weak (Breitenbach), none (Miller)
TASTE  mild (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  too small and tough (Arora)
HABITAT  annual, single on dead hardwood, also on juniper and Douglas-fir decking, associated with a white rot of dead hardwoods, rarely on conifer wood^, (Gilbertson), single or in small groups on dead hardwoods (Arora), on "dead branches and sticks in woodland or on the ground growing from buried wood", (Lincoff(1)), fruiting in summer and fall (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-9 x 2.5-3 microns, cylindric, straight or slightly curved, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 25-35 x 5-6 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia none; hyphal system dimitic, generative hyphae in context 2.5-5 microns wide, colorless in KOH, "thin-walled, often branched, with abundant clamps", skeletal hyphae in context 2-11 microns wide, thick-walled, nonseptate, with occasional branching; hyphae of cap surface 1-1.5 microns wide, thin-walled, with clamp connections; hyphae of trama similar, (Gilbertson), spores 7-11 x 2-3 microns, cylindric, smooth, (Arora)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin "vaulted", referring to pore shape^, (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  Neofavolus alveolaris usually differs in being laterally stemmed or with stem that is not well formed, and in having larger spores, (Gilbertson). N. alveolaris also has radially elongated pores but the stem is typically lateral or not well formed, the cap is a reddish yellow to pale brick red, and the spores are 11-14 microns long, (Ginns). Polyporus brumalis "also has more or less radially elongated, diamond-shaped pores, but they are much smaller than those of P. arcularius" as well as less radially elongated, and cap is darker, (Gilbertson). P. brumalis has a much darker cap, smaller and less radially elongated pores (angular but less strikingly hexagonal), and smaller spores, (Ginns).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Arora(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Lincoff(1)*, Miller(14)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)*, Breitenbach(2)*, Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Polyporus badius  (Pers.: Gray) Schwein.^  Trans. Am. Phil. Soc. II 4: 155. 1832;  Royoporus badius  (Pers.) A.B. De  Mycotaxon 65: 471 (1997);  Grifola badia  Pers.: Gray;  Polyporus picipes  Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  black-footed polypore, black-leg
NOTES  Features include tough, thin, circular chestnut brown to blackish cap that becomes depressed, small white to pale buff pores, central to lateral stem that is brown at top and black at base or entirely black, and microscopic characters including generative hyphae without clamp connections.^ Polyporus badius is the only North American Polyporus without clamp connections, but generative hyphae and their septa can be difficult to find, (Ginns). The online Species Fungorum, accessed February 21, 2012, gives the current name as Royoporus badius (Pers.) A.B. De, Mycotaxon 65: 471 (1997), but by September 3, 2018 had changed to Picipes badius (Pers.) Zmitr. & Kovalenko, International Journal of Medicinal Mushrooms (Redding) 18(1): 35 (2016). MycoBank, accessed the same day in 2018, gave the current name as Polyporus badius. The separation of Royoporus is based on morphology - while Polyporus is apparently not monophyletic, the issue will likely be decided by molecular studies. RANGE Polyporus badius is found in BC, WA, OR, ID, also AB, MB, NT, NB, NF, NS, ON, PQ, SK, AK, AL, AR, AZ, CA, CO, CT, IA, IL, IN, KS, KY, MA, MD, ME, MI, MO, MT, NC, NH, NY, OH, PA, TN, VT, WI, and WV, (Gilbertson), and Europe, Asia, Africa, and Australia, (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 15cm, circular or fan-shaped; "light chestnut brown to dark blackish brown, often darker in center", not zoned; bald, smooth or wrinkled on drying^, (Gilbertson), 4-20cm, convex becoming depressed or umbilicate; tan to chestnut-brown to dark reddish brown or often becoming blackish when old; margin usually paler and often lobed, (Arora), "radially striate, pale chestnut brown or dark red brown, almost black when old, azonate, glabrous, smooth or rugose", (Ginns)
FLESH  up to 1.5cm thick, corky, not zoned; pale buff^, (Gilbertson), very thin, tough when fresh, rigid when dry; white becoming brownish, (Arora)
PORES  5-8 per mm, circular to angular, decurrent; white to pale buff; tube layer up to 0.1cm, white when young to slightly darker than flesh^, (Gilbertson), 4-7 per mm, adnate to somewhat decurrent; white when fresh, brownish when old; tube layer thin, (Arora)
STEM  up to 5cm thick [sic], lateral to central; brown at top, black at base; bald at top, minutely tomentose at base^, (Gilbertson), 1-6cm x 0.3-1.5cm, central to lateral, tough, more or less equal; pallid in upper part and black in lower part, or more often black throughout, (Arora)
ODOR  pleasant^ (Breitenbach), none (Miller)
TASTE  mild^ (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  too tough (Arora)
HABITAT  annual, single or clustered, on dead wood of hardwoods and conifers^, associated with a white rot, (Gilbertson), single or in groups on rotting hardwoods or occasionally conifers, (Arora), on stumps and logs of hardwoods, on ground on buried wood, also reported on conifer wood, (Lincoff(2)), found in summer and fall (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7.5-9 x 3.3-5 microns, cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 20-30 x 7-9 microns, clavate with narrow base; simple-septate at base; cystidia absent, fusoid cystidioles present, 17-19 x 5-7 microns, septate at base, hyphal pegs usually present; hyphae dimitic, generative hyphae of context 3-5 microns wide, thin-walled, simple-septate, inconspicuous in mature specimens, skeletal hyphae of context "thick-walled, aseptate, 2-7 with occasional branching", binding hyphae of context 3-5 microns wide, "thick-walled, aseptate, much branched"; hyphae of trama similar, (Gilbertson), spores 5-9 x 3-4 microns, cylindric to elliptic, smooth, (Arora)
NAME ORIGIN  means "chestnut-colored, brown"^, (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Polyporus elegans has a paler and often smaller cap, does not have an entirely black stem, and is more common on branches and sticks as opposed to P. badius which often grows on logs and stumps, (Arora). P. elegans can have an entirely black stem, but its cap is not radially striate, pores are 5-7 per mm, and spores measure 7.5-10 x 2.5-3 microns, (Ginns). P. elegans is tan to chestnut brown, and generative hyphae have clamp connections, (Gilbertson). Polyporus varius is intermediate in color between P. badius and Polyporus elegans, "is often strikingly radially streaked with lighter colours", and has relatively large spores, (Ginns). P. varius has a radially streaked cap and is intermediate in color between P. badius and P. elegans, (Arora). P. varius has a cap with radial striations and generative hyphae have clamp connections (Gilbertson). Polyporus melanopus often fruits on the ground, and has generative hyphae with clamp connections, (Gilbertson). P. melanopus is similar in color but pores are larger at 3-4 per mm, and it fruits on the ground, (Ginns).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Arora(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Miller(14)*, Breitenbach(2)*, Sept(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Polyporus brumalis  Pers.: Fr.^  Syst. Mycol. 1: 348. 1821;  Lentinus brumalis  (Pers.) Zmitr.  International Journal of Medicinal Mushrooms (Redding) 12(1): 88. 2010
ENGLISH NAMES  winter polypore
NOTES  Features include 1) a bronze to purplish brown fruitbody with a ciliate margin, 2) radially elongated, diagonal, whitish pores, 3) a central stem, and 4) growth on hardwoods.^ It is common in the east but rare in the west, (Gilbertson). The online Species Fungorum, accessed September 3, 2018, gave the current name as Lentinus brumalis (Pers.) Zmitr., International Journal of Medicinal Mushrooms (Redding) 12(1): 88 (2010), but MycoBank gave that name as a synonym of Polyporus brumalis (Pers.) Fr. RANGE Polyporus brumalis has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, MB, NB, NS, NF, ON, PQ, SK, AR, CT, DE, IA, IN, KS, KY, MA, ME, MI, MN, MT, NH, NJ, NY, OH, PA, TN, VT, WI, and WV, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 6cm across and 0.5cm thick, usually single but sometimes several from branched stem base; bronze to purplish brown, not zoned; "shiny, with clusters of short, stiff dark hairs", margin becoming reflexed [turned up], "often appearing finely fringed or ciliate"^, (Gilbertson), 1.5-10cm,"circular, convex or depressed with an inrolled margin; yellow-brown to reddish brown or blackish brown; dry, densely hairy when young becoming almost smooth", (Phillips), yellow brown to dark brown, fading to tan, (Lincoff), cap surface finely tomentose, squamose, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  up to 0.3cm thick, corky; white, not zoned^, (Gilbertson), 0.1-0.2cm thick, white, (Phillips)
PORES  3-4 per mm, slightly decurrent, angular, with thin walls that become torn; whitish or ivory; glancing [showing a change in appearance from dull to lustrous when the orientation to light is changed], smooth; tube layer up to 0.2cm thick, ivory^, (Gilbertson), 2-3 per mm, circular to angular, slightly decurrent; whitish; tube layer 0.1-0.3cm thick, (Phillips)
STEM  up to 4cm x 0.5cm, central, lighter in color than cap^, (Gilbertson), 2-6cm x 0.1-0.5cm, "central or off-center, grayish or brownish; minutely hairy or smooth", (Phillips)
ODOR  fungoid^ (Breitenbach)
TASTE  mild^ (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  no (Phillips)
HABITAT  annual, on dead hardwood^, associated with a white rot, (Gilbertson), on dead hardwood, especially birch, (Phillips), fall, winter, early spring (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Phillips)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6-7.5 x 2-2.5 microns, cylindric, slightly curved, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 16-22 x 5-6.5 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia absent; hyphae dimitic, generative hyphae of context 4-10 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled, with clamp connections, occasionally branched, binding hyphae of context with swellings up to 13 microns wide but mostly 4-10 microns wide, with dendritic branching and branches tapering to 1-2 microns wide, colorless, thick-walled, nonseptate, hyphae of trama 2.5-6 microns, similar, less frequently branched, (Gilbertson), spores 5-7 x 1.5-2.5 microns, cylindric to sausage-shaped, smooth, (Phillips)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin "brumalis" meaning "of the winter or shortest day"^, (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
SIMILAR  Polyporus arcularius has a generally paler cap and larger pores that are more elongated radially, and larger spores, (Gilbertson). Neofavolus alveolaris has a paler cap, more lateral stem, and larger pores and spores, (Gilbertson).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Ginns(28)*, Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)*, Breitenbach(2)*, Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Polyporus elegans  Bull.: Fr.^  Epicr. Syst. Mycol. p. 440. 1838;  Cerioporus leptocephalus  (Jacq.) Zmitr.  International Journal of Medicinal Mushrooms (Redding) 18(1): 33. 2016 (proposed new name)
ENGLISH NAMES  elegant polypore, black-foot, black-footed polypore
NOTES  features include tough thin circular tan to chestnut brown cap that becomes depressed, small pale buff pores, central to lateral stem that is brown at top and black at base, and microscopic characters including generative hyphae with clamp connections^; the species has been regarded as a synonym of Polyporus varius, (MycoBank, accessed September 6, 2017, has Polyporus elegans (Bull.) Zmitr. Ginns(28) uses the name Polyporus leptocephalus (Jacq.) Fr. RANGE Polyporus elegans has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NS, ON, PQ, YT, AK, AL, AR, AZ, CA, CO, CT, DE, GA, IA, KY, MA, ME, MI, MN, MT, NC, ND, NE, NH, NJ, NY, OH, PA, TN, UT, VA, VT, WI, WV, and WY, (Gilbertson).

ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  too tough (Arora)
HABITAT  annual, usually single, on dead hardwoods, occasionally on conifers^, associated with white rot of dead hardwoods, (Gilbertson), single or several together "on decaying hardwood sticks, branches and debris (only rarely on conifers)", (Arora)

MICROSCOPIC  spores 7.5-10 x 2.5-3 microns, cylindric, slightly curved, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 15-20 x 6-7 microns, broadly clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia none; hyphae dimitic, generative hyphae of context 2.5-4 microns wide, thin-walled, with clamp connections, binding hyphae of context 2.5-5 microns, thick-walled, nonseptate, much branched; hyphae of trama similar, (Gilbertson), spores 6.5-10 x 2.5-4 microns, cylindric, smooth, (Arora)

P. badius has a dark brown or blackish brown cap, it is usually larger, and it lacks clamp connections, (Gilbertson). Polyporus varius has a radially streaked cap and is intermediate in color between P. elegans and P. badius, (Arora). P. varius has a radially streaked cap (Gilbertson). Polyporus melanopus has "a velvety stem and velvety-scurfy cap, at least when young" and "grows from the ground or from buried wood", (Arora). P. melanopus is generally larger, has a black velvety stem, and grows on the ground, (Gilbertson).

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Polyporus melanopus  Fr.^  Syst. Mycol. 1: 347. 1821;  Picipes melanopus  (Pers.) Zmitr. & Kovalenko  International Journal of Medicinal Mushrooms (Redding) 18(1): 36. 2016 (proposed new name)
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) a tough, thin, circular, reddish brown to blackish brown cap that becomes depressed, 2) small whitish pores, 3) a central to lateral stem that is brownish black, and velvety in upper part, 4) growth on the ground from buried wood, and 5) microscopic characters including generative hyphae with clamp connections.^ The online Species Fungorum, accessed September 3, 2018, gave the current name as Picipes melanopus (Pers.) Zmitr. & Kovalenko International Journal of Medicinal Mushrooms (Redding) 18(1): 36. (2016), but MycoBank, accessed the same day, gave that name as a synonym of Polyporus melanopus (Pers.) Fr. RANGE Polyporus melanopus has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NS, ON, PQ, YT, AK, AL, AR, AZ, CA, CO, CT, DE, GA, IA, KY, MA, ME, MI, MN, MT, NC, ND, NE, NH, NJ, NY, OH, PA, TN, UT, VA, VT, WI, WV, and WY, (Gilbertson), and Europe, Asia, and Australia, (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 10cm across and 5cm thick, circular; reddish brown to smoky blackish brown, not zoned; "smooth, finely scurfy"; margin often wavy or turned up^, (Gilbertson), 3-10cm, convex then funnel-shaped or flattish with wavy margin; bay brown or purplish brown with pale yellowish lines; "smooth and velvety, then very wrinkled, especially in the middle", (Phillips), 2-10cm, circular, convex to flat and slightly funnel-shaped; "light leather-brown to gray-brown"; smooth, very finely velvety, "dull to somewhat innately squamose-radially fibrillose", margin sharp, thin, wavy, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  up to 0.5cm thick, firm, rather friable when dry, not zoned; white^, (Gilbertson), about 0.1cm thick, white to cream, (Phillips), 0.1-0.2cm thick, corky, tough; white, (Breitenbach)
PORES  6-8 per mm, circular to angular, thick-walled; whitish; tube layer up to 0.05cm, slightly darker than flesh, distinct from it and separated by a faint brownish layer^, (Gilbertson), 4-7 per mm, circular, decurrent in specimens with lateral stem; whitish to cream then pale straw-colored; tube layer up to 0.3cm thick, cream to straw-colored, (Phillips), 3-4 per mm, irregularly rounded, sometimes decurrent onto one side of stem; white, cream to brownish; tube layer 0.1-0.2cm thick, (Breitenbach)
STEM  up to 11cm x 2cm, central, underground part root-like; blackish in upper part, dark brownish black in lower part; velvety in upper part, bald in lower part^, (Gilbertson), 1-5cm x 0.2-0.8cm, "central or lateral; dark brown then black; velvety becoming longitudinally wrinkled", (Phillips), 1.5-5.5cm x 0.3-1.5cm, cylindric, apex set off from pore layer; brown-black; "smooth to longitudinally wrinkled, velutinous", (Breitenbach)
ODOR  pleasant^ (Breitenbach)
TASTE  mild^ (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  no (Phillips)
HABITAT  annual, on ground, presumably developing from buried wood of hardwoods and conifers^, (Gilbertson), on ground or on buried wood (Phillips), single to gregarious, on buried dead wood of hardwoods and conifers, apparently only on soil, according to literature also on roots of hardwoods, (Breitenbach), late summer to fall (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-9 x 3-3.5 microns, cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 18-28 x 6-8 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia none, cystidioles present, 16-21 x 4-5 microns, fusoid, with basal clamp; hyphae dimitic, generative hyphae of context 3-5 microns wide, colorless in KOH, thin-walled, with clamp connections, rarely branched, binding hyphae of context 2-7 microns wide, colorless, thick-walled, nonseptate, with occasionally branching; hyphae of trama similar, (Gilbertson), spores 6-8 x 3-4 microns, cylindric to oblong elliptic, inamyloid, (Phillips), spores 7-8 x 3-3.5 microns, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  means "with dark or black feet"^, (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Polyporus badius is found on wood and has a bald stem, and the generative hyphae lack clamp connections, (Gilbertson). P. badius has similar colors but it has smaller pores (5-8 per mm), it fruits "on fallen branches and logs on the ground", and the stem is typically bald, (Ginns). Polyporus varius is found on wood, has a streaked cap, and has a bald stem, (Gilbertson). Polyporus elegans is usually much smaller and becomes whitish when old, besides being black only in the lower part of the stem, (Gilbertson). P. elegans (as P. leptocephalus) is typically smaller and occurs "on fallen branches and logs lying atop the ground", (Ginns). See also SIMILAR section of Polyporus radicatus which has an underground, root-like structure.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Phillips(1)*, Breitenbach(2)*, Trudell(4)*, Ginns(28)*, Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Polyporus radicatus  Schwein.^  Trans. Am. phil. Soc., New Series 4(2): 155 (1832) [1834]
ENGLISH NAMES  rooting polypore
NOTES  Features include 1) a circular, flat to depressed cap that is pale yellow to dark brown, and at first fibrillose to scaly, 2) dense white flesh, 3) a white pore surface with angular pores 2-3 per mm, 4) a scurfy to finely scaly stem that is elongated into a black root, 5) growth on the ground, and microscopic characters. It is rare in the Pacific Northwest.^ RANGE Polyporus radicatus is known in BC from a single collection on Vancouver Island (Ginns(28)). It is also recorded from AB, ON, AL, IA, IN, MO, NE, OH, KS, MD, NY, PA, TN, WI, and VT, (Gilbertson). It also occurred in IL.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  3-20cm across, 0.3-1.0cm thick, "circular, flat to depressed"; "yellowish to sooty brown"; "fibrillose to finely scaly or scurfy", becoming bald^, (Gilbertson), pale yellow to blackish brown [illustrated as orange brown]; finely scaly, (Ginns), 3-10cm but occasionally up to 30cm plus; flat-convex to flat or, when old, shallowly sunken, usually round in outline from above but sometimes lobed; "dark yellowish brown to reddish brown"; dry, "velvety or with appressed fibrils and small scales", with a 0.1cm sterile margin when young, (Kuo)
FLESH  dense, white, may be 1.0cm thick^, (Gilbertson), fruitbody consistency fleshy; context in cap tough, white, (Ginns), soft but very tough; white, (Kuo)
PORES  2-3 per mm, angular, surface "white when fresh, drying ochraceous to pale yellowish brown"; tubes decurrent, "cartilaginous and brittle when dry", darker than the flesh^, (Gilbertson), tubes 0.1-0.3cm deep, edges even, (Ginns), 1-2 per mm, round to slightly angular; decurrent; whitish, or when old dingy orangish, not bruising; tubes 0.1-0.5cm long, (Kuo)
STEM  5-15cm x 0.5-2cm, central, "elongated below the ground to a black root", colored like the cap or darker; "scurfy to squamulose"^, (Gilbertson), "pale yellow to blackish brown, below ground entirely black", (Ginns), 3-6cm long above ground, 0.5-1cm wide, "more or less equal", or widening slightly toward base, with a 4-10cm, 'tapering, black "root" extending underground'; "dirty orangish to slightly brownish above ground"; finely pubescent near the top, (Kuo)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Kuo)
TASTE  slightly bitter^ (Kuo)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on the ground, "but probably from buried roots", probably causing a white rot in hardwoods^, (Gilbertson), typically single (Ginns), single or scattered, on the ground (but actually fruiting from buried wood), "in grassy and open areas," "causing a white rot", (Kuo)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Kuo)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 12-15 x 6-8 microns, oblong elliptic to fusiform, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 25-35 x 8-12 microns, clavate, with basal clamp connection; cystidia or other sterile hymenial elements absent; hyphal system dimitic: generative hyphae thin-walled and colorless, with clamp connections which may be difficult to observe, binding hyphae "sparingly branched and of variable thickness and diameter and may in segments be collapsed while other parts are thick-walled", in the trama 2-6 microns wide, in the context up to 12 microns wide in the main stem; "context composed almost entirely of binding hyphae, so sparingly branched that they easily may be taken as segments of skeletal hyphae", (Gilbertson), generative hyphae 4.5-6.5 microns wide; cystidioles up to 60 x 13 microns, fusoid, (Ginns), spores 11-14 x 6-7 microns, "widely subfusiform to subfusiform", smooth, colorless to faintly yellowish-refractive in KOH, "with one, large, irregularly shaped oil droplet"; hymenial cystidia not found; clamp connections present, (Kuo)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'with a root'
SIMILAR  Polyporus melanopus is usually somewhat smaller, and features a rooting stem that becomes black and velvety above ground, (Kuo).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Ginns(28)*, Kuo(10)*

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Polyporus rhizophilus  Pat.^  J. Bot., Paris 8(12): 219. 1894;  Polyporus cryptopus  Ellis & Barth.  Erythea 4: 79. 1896;  Picipes rhizophilus (Pat.) J.L. Zhou & B.K. Cui, in Zhou, Zhu, Chen & Cui, PLoS ONE 11(8): e0159495, 16 (2016) (proposed current name)

RANGE Polyporus rhizophilus has been found in WA, CO, KS, MT, and ND, (Gilbertson).

FLESH  up to 0.2cm, soft-corky; whitish, not zoned
PORES  2-3 per mm, angular, with thin walls that are intact or slightly torn; ivory to tan; tube layer up to 0.2cm thick, colored as flesh
STEM  up to 2cm x 0.5cm, central, usually simple; brown or blackish, colored as cap or darker
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on ground in pastures or prairie habitats^, according to Overholts attached to dead grass roots, according to Weir a specimen was attached to bark at the base of a dead lodgepole pine, (Gilbertson)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-11 x 3.5 microns, cylindric, fusiform, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 25-38 x 7-8.5 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia absent, hyphal pegs rare; hyphae dimitic, generative hyphae of context 3-7 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled, with clamp connections, occasionally branched, binding hyphae of context 2-5 microns wide, colorless, thick-walled, nonseptate, some with frequent branching; hyphae of trama similar
NAME ORIGIN  means "with a secret or hidden foot"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Polyporus cryptopus), Ginns(28)

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Polyporus squamosus  (Huds.) Fr.^  Syst. Mycol. 1: 343. 1821;  Cerioporus squamosus  (Huds.) Quel.  Enchir. fung. (Paris): 167. 1886. (reproposed old name)
ENGLISH NAMES  dryad's saddle, scaly polypore, pheasant's back polypore
NOTES  Features of Polyporus squamosus are 1) a large, tough, buff-colored to pale yellow cap with coarse dark scales, 2) thick flesh, 3) a buff to light brown pore surface that is decurrent on the stem, 4) a thick, hard, brown to black, velvety lower stem, 5) large angular pores, 6) growth on hardwood, and 7) large basidia and spores.^ The online Species Fungorum, accessed September 3, 2018, gave the current name as Cerioporus squamosus (Huds.) Quel. but MycoBank, accessed the same day, gave that name as a synonym of Polyporus squamosus (Huds.) Fr. RANGE Polyporus squamosus is found in BC, WA, ID, AB, NB, NS, ON, PQ, AR, AZ, CO, CT, IA, IL, KS, KY, MA, MD, ME, MI, MO, NC, NE, NJ, NY, OH, PE, TN, VT, WI, and WV, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 18cm across and 5cm thick, dimidiate [roughly semicircular], kidney-shaped, or circular; pale buff with thin blackish brown pellicle [skin] "that breaks up to form dark, scale-like patches", not zoned, occasional specimens are not scaly^, (Gilbertson), 5-30cm, at first circular or fan-shaped; whitish to ochraceous cream; covered with concentric dark brown hairy scales, (Phillips), up to 5(8)cm across, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  up to 4cm thick, corky, not zoned; pale buff^, (Gilbertson), 1-3cm thick, "succulent when fresh, drying corky; white", (Phillips)
PORES  1-2 per mm, angular, walls becoming torn, decurrent on stem down to black part at base; buff to light brown; tube layer up to 1cm thick, colored as flesh^, (Gilbertson), irregular and angular, decurrent; whitish to ochraceous cream; tube layer 0.2-0.8cm thick, (Phillips), 1-2 per mm (Breitenbach), 1-2 mm across (Courtecuisse)
STEM  lateral, black and minutely tomentose at base, rest of stem usually covered by decurrent tube layer^, (Gilbertson), 1-5cm x 1-4cm, lateral or occasionally off-center, stubby; blackish toward base, (Phillips), 3-10cm x 1-6cm, (Breitenbach), 3-8cm x 1-5cm, central or more usually lateral, (Lincoff(1))
ODOR  strongly of meal^ (Phillips), farinaceous (Breitenbach), like watermelon rind (Lincoff(2)), cheese-like then mealy, (Lincoff(1))
TASTE  mild and pleasant when young^ (Phillips), farinaceous (Breitenbach), like watermelon rind (Lincoff(2), Lincoff(1))
EDIBILITY  yes (Phillips), the tender edges of caps can be pickled, sauteed or fried, (Lincoff(2)), sometimes boiled to add flavor to soup stock, then discarded, (Lincoff(1)), yes but older caps too tough (McKnight)
HABITAT  annual, single or several from branched base, on living hardwoods, also often found on stumps or dead standing or fallen trees^, associated with white heartrot of living and dead hardwoods, (Gilbertson), single or "in overlapping clusters on the wounds of dead trees", (Phillips), spring and summer (Bacon)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Phillips)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 16-20 x 6-9 microns, broadly cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 60-85 x 9-12 microns, clavate with narrow base, with basal clamp; cystidia absent; hyphae dimitic; generative hyphae of context 3-4.5 microns wide, thin-walled, with clamp connections, binding hyphae of context 4-7.5 microns wide, colorless, thick-walled, "nonseptate, with occasional branching, some dentrically branched with branches tapering to 1-2 microns"; hyphae of trama similar; hyphae of dark tomentum of base of stem "composed of thick-walled hyphae that are reddish brown in Melzer's reagent and in KOH, compactly arranged and difficult to separate", (Gilbertson), 10-16 x 4-6 microns, oblong elliptic, smooth, (Phillips), spores 11-15.5 x 4.5-5.5 microns, basidia 35-40 x 7-9 microns, (Breitenbach), spores 10-12 x 4-5 microns, (Lincoff(1)), spores 10-16 x 4-6 microns, (Lincoff(2))
NAME ORIGIN  means "scaled"^ (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  Polyporus tuberaster has similar cap scales but 1) arises from a blackish sclerotium, 2) normally has a thinner, less robust fruitbody, and 3) has scales that are raised and tufted rather than rounded and agglutinated, (Gilbertson).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Phillips(1)*, Ginns(28)*, Lincoff(2)*, Lincoff(1)*, Courtecuisse(1)*, McKnight(1)*, Breitenbach(2)*, Bacon(1)*, Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Polyporus tuberaster  Jacq.: Fr.^  Syst. Mycol. 1: 347. 1821;  Polyporus decurrens  Underw.
ENGLISH NAMES  Canadian tuckahoe, stone fungus, tuberous polypore
NOTES  Polyporus tuberaster has a yellowish brown cap with darker scales, white to pale tan decurrent pores, and a central to lateral stem with a black base. It arises from a large, rubbery to rock hard, blackish sclerotium that incorporates debris and dirt.^ The sclerotium is sold in European markets, and if well watered will produce fruiting bodies for eating (Lincoff). According to Paul Kroeger (pers. comm.) the sclerotium has been mistaken for old pemmican. RANGE Gilbertson(1) show records on a map from CA and AZ, but give distribution as "Western United States south to Arizona in the aspen zone and Southern Canada, widespread in the northern boreal zone", in Canada this fungus occurs in the aspen parkland belt of the prairies, (Schalkwijk-Barendsen who illustrates it from AB, and mentions its occurrence in Netherlands, Italy, and Switzerland), known from BC, AB, SK, WA, OR, CA, and AZ, in western North America, (Ginns). It also occurs in Eurasia (Breitenbach). 
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 15cm across, 0.5-1.5cm thick, simple when on wood, often more compound when on the ground, circular to semicircular or even fan-shaped in compound fruitbodies, depressed in center, fleshy when fresh; whitish, ochraceous to pale yellowish brown; "covered with small tan to dark brown scales with a broad base and especially towards margin, split and fibrillose, in pale specimens the scales are pale and thus not especially distinct", when old becoming more bald from center as scales partly wear away, partly are glued to surface; margin thin and mostly finely ciliate [fringed with fine hairs] or torn^, (Gilbertson), 4-15cm, convex to flat with depressed or umbilicate center, "margin often indented or lobed"; tan to brown or ocher, darker when old; dry, developing darker brown fibrils or fibrillose scales often radially arranged, (Arora)
FLESH  up to 1cm thick, "fleshy-tough when fresh, drying rigid and brittle"; white^, (Gilbertson), thin, rigid when dry; pallid, (Arora)
PORES  1-2mm long and 0.5-1mm wide, decurrent, "often lacerate to dentate, angular, often somewhat radially elongated"; white to pale tan; tube layer up to 0.5cm thick, colored as pores^, (Gilbertson), 1-3 per mm, usually decurrent; white to pale tan; tube layer 0.1-0.3cm thick, (Arora)
STEM  0.5-6cm x 0.5-1.5cm, central to lateral, straight or curved at base; white to ochraceous except "at base with white hairs under which there is a thin black cuticle which may extend a short distance above the tomentum"; sclerotium "normally present in the ground, round to oval or irregular, heavy, up to 30 lbs, but normally far less than that, fleshy and tough when fresh, shrinking considerably on drying and becoming hard and brittle, surface ochraceous to dark dirty brown, densely mixed with sand, stones and roots, often in considerable quantities so it looks like ground material has been penetrated by mycelial strands and white spots of hyphae"^, (Gilbertson), 2.5-10(20)cm x 1-2.5(4)cm, "more or less equal, central or off-center", solid, tough; brown or cap-colored; arising from a large brown to black underground sclerotium that is rubbery when fresh but rock hard when dry, exterior of sclerotium "rough and irregular", "interior often marbled with blackish-brown and paler areas and usually full of dirt and debris", (Arora), underground sclerotium round to irregular, black, fleshy, tough, heavy (up to 13 kg), and large (about 15cm in diameter), (Ginns), sclerotium gristly and rubbery, with a black and gray pattern, (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
ODOR  anise (Schalkwijk-Barendsen), resembling cheese (Lincoff), fungoid (Breitenbach)
TASTE  resembling cheese (Lincoff), mild (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  edible, "but tough unless young, fresh and thoroughly cooked", sclerotium inedible because full of dirt, (Arora)
HABITAT  annual, on hardwoods or on the ground from a large blackish sclerotium (even on wood, there is often a connection through wood to underground sclerotium)^, causing a white rot of hardwoods, (Gilbertson), spring, late summer, fall, (Bacon)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 10-16 x 4-7 microns, cylindric to oblong elliptic, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 25-40 x 6-10 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia absent; hyphae dimitic, generative hyphae 3-9 microns wide, with clamp connections; hairs at base of stem and the scales mostly of wide generative hyphae; "binding hyphae of the Bovista type with tapering sidebranches, thick-walled to solid, sparingly branched, up to 12 microns in the main stem"; "sclerotium mainly with binding hyphae, in parts very finely branched and very thin, in most parts sparingly branched, thick-walled and variable in diameter, 3-10 microns wide, in some cases with apical swellings", (Gilbertson), spores 10-16 x 3.5-6 microns, cylindric, smooth, (Arora)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin, "tuberous", (Lincoff(1)), from Latin, "like a truffle", (Schalkwijk-Barendsen), "like a little potato" (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Polyporus squamosus has similar cap scales but 1) it has no sclerotium, 2) it normally has thicker more robust fruitbody, and 3) scales "are normally rounded and more agglutinated and not raised and tufted as in P. tuberaster", (Gilbertson). Polyporus umbellatus fruitbodies sometimes develop from a "branched, elongated, finger-shaped, black" sclerotium 1-2cm in diameter, (Ginns(28)). Polyporus radicatus and Polyporus melanopus develop root-like underground structure.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Arora(1)*, Ginns(28)*, Lincoff(1)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)*, Breitenbach(2)*, Siegel(2)* (as Polyporus tuberaster group), Bacon(1)*, Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Polyporus umbellatus  Fr.^  Syst. Mycol. 1: 354. 1821;  Dendropolyporus umbellatus  (Pers.: Fr.) Julich
ENGLISH NAMES  umbrella polypore
NOTES  Polyporus umbellatus forms numerous light brown, circular caps arising on central stems from a compound stem, often from an underground sclerotium, angular cream to straw-colored pores covering much of stems as well as the underside of the caps. It grows near hardwoods stumps.^ RANGE It has been found in WA, ID, CT, MT, IA, KS, KY, MA, MI, NY, OH, PA, TN, and TX, (Gilbertson). It also occurs in Europe (Courtecuisse).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  with numerous more or less circular caps arising from a strongly branched stem, total width and height up to 50cm, individual caps 1-3(4)cm across, flat, fleshy when fresh, hard and brittle when dry, partly overlapping with other caps; ochraceous to light brown; smooth (wrinkled when dry), bald or with minute appressed scales giving a spotty appearance under hand lens; margin thin^, (Gilbertson), 1-4cm, white to smoky; dry, fibrous or smooth, usually in clusters 7.5-20cm wide, but can reach 50cm in width, (Lincoff(2)), 1-4cm, convex then umbilicate with wavy margin; fairly light brown; radially fibrillose, pruinose, or slightly scaly, (Lincoff(1))
FLESH  up to 0.1cm thick, dense; white to cream^, (Gilbertson), soft and juicy, slightly fibrous; white, (Lincoff(1))
PORES  1-3 per mm, angular, elongated toward stem, on stem more irregular, split and partly sinuous, up to 2 mm wide; white, cream or straw-colored; tube layer up to 0.2cm thick, colored as pores^, (Gilbertson) 
STEM  thick at base, up to 3cm wide, thinner toward caps and richly branched, individual stems central; "white to cream or pale straw-colored"; in upper parts covered by pores "which in the lower parts are visible only as shallow pits, smoother toward base"^, (Gilbertson), 2.5-7.5cm x 2-3cm, "short, strongly branched above ground", "rising from hard, blackish, tuberlike underground structure with white interior", (Lincoff(2)), divided into many cylindric branches; "white or reddish brown"; branches pruinose; often originating from large underground sclerotium, (Lincoff(1)) 
ODOR  slightly mealy (Lincoff(1)), pleasant (Breitenbach)
TASTE  sweet (Lincoff(1)), mild with bitter component (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  choice (Lincoff(2))
HABITAT  annual, on ground, close to stumps of hardwoods, reported also on Picea^, causing a white rot in hardwoods, (Gilbertson), on ground on buried wood, around stumps or hardwood trees, (Lincoff(2)), summer and fall, (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Lincoff(2))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7.5-10 x 3-4 microns, cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 18-25 x 5-8 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia absent; hyphae dimitic, generative hyphae in context up to 12 microns wide, with clamp connections, thin-walled to slightly thick-walled, width very variable, "partly narrow, partly large and wide with conspicuous clamps", generative hyphae in trama more narrow and rarely over 6 microns wide, with clamp connections, "dendrinoid binding hyphae scattered and restricted to the trama", up to 17 microns wide tapering down to about 2 microns at tips, colorless, solid; illustration also shows gloeoplerous hypha from context, (Gilbertson), spores 8-10 x 2.5-3.5 microns, (Lincoff(2)), spores 7-10 x 3-4 microns (Lincoff(1))
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin meaning "with umbrellas"^, (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  Grifola frondosa "has individual, spoon-shaped caps, compound base, and lacks underground tuberous structure", (Lincoff(2)). Polyporus tuberaster can develop from a buried, round to irregular sclerotium that is black, fleshy, tough, heavy (up to 13 kg), and large (about 15cm in diameter), (Ginns). See also SIMILAR section of Bondarzewia mesenterica and Meripilus sumstinei.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Ginns(28), Lincoff(2)*, Lincoff(1)*, Courtecuisse(1)* (as Dendropolyporus umbellatus), Breitenbach(2)* (as Dendropolyporus umbellatus), Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Polyporus varius  (Pers.) Fr.^  Syst. Mycol. 1: 352. 1821;  Cerioporus varius  (Pers.) Zmitr. & Kovalenko  International Journal of Medicinal Mushrooms (Redding) 18(1): 33. 2016 (proposed new name)
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) a tough, thin, circular, pale buff cap that has darker radial striations, and that becomes depressed, 2) small pale buff pores, 3) a central to lateral black stem, and 4) microscopic characters including generative hyphae with clamp connections.^ The online Species Fungorum, accessed September 6, 2017, gave the current name as Cerioporus varius (Pers.) Zmitr. & Kovalenko, but MycoBank, accessed the same day, gave the current name as Polyporus varius (Pers.) Fr. The description is derived from Gilbertson(1). RANGE Polyporus varius is found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NT, NB, NS, ON, YT, AK, AZ, CA, CO, IA, MA, MI, MN, MO, MT, NC, NH, NM, NY, TN, UT, VT, WV, and WY, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS

FLESH  up to 0.2cm thick, corky, not zoned; buff
PORES  7-9 per mm, circular to angular, usually decurrent down to black part of stem; pale buff; tube layer colored as flesh
STEM  up to 2cm x 0.7cm, central to lateral; black; minutely tomentose at the base
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on dead wood of hardwoods and conifers, associated with a white rot

SIMILAR  Polyporus elegans does not have striate cap surface, (Gilbertson). Polyporus badius has a dark brown or blackish brown cap, usually larger size, and lacks clamp connections, (Gilbertson). Polyporus melanopus has a black velvety stem, and grows on the ground, (Gilbertson).

FAMILY  Uncertain, Order Uncertain, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Polypus dispansus  (Lloyd) Audet^  Mycotaxon 111: 443. 2010;  Albatrellus dispansus  (Lloyd) Canf. & Gilb.  Mycologia 63: 965. 1971;  Polyporus dispansus  Lloyd;  Polyporus illudens  Overh.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include large numbers of yellowish to yellowish buff petal-like caps arising from a much branched white base, and small white pores^. RANGE Polypus dispansus is rare: it has been found in ID, AZ, Japan, (Gilbertson), and also WA (Ginns(1)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  multicapped up to 35cm across, with individual caps 2-6cm wide, densely imbricate [shingled]; yellow at first, tinted orange when old, not zoned; dry, finely pubescent to tomentose^, (Bessette), up to 20cm, from base that branches into petal-like caps that are densely imbricate [shingled], and confluent; each cap 1-4cm, yellowish to yellowish buff, not zoned; finely tomentose or scurfy, becoming rugose [wrinkled] when old or dry, (Gilbertson), deep yellow to tan (Ginns(1)), overlapping, sometimes fused, often irregularly shaped caps; yellowish; dry, tomentose to scaly, (Trudell)
FLESH  "firm, dry, soft and pliant to brittle when fresh but very hard when dried"^, (Bessette), from less than 0.1cm thick at margin to 1cm thick near base of branches, firm, brittle; white, (Gilbertson)
PORES  2-3 per mm, angular; white, not bruising^, (Bessette), 2-3 per mm, angular, with thin walls; white drying faintly vinaceous, (Gilbertson), white to pale cream (Ginns(1))
STEM  broad, central, short, up to 7cm wide, branching; white; smooth^, (Bessette), fruiting body arises from broad shallowly rooted base up to 7cm broad, branching into large numbers of petal-like caps, (Gilbertson), 10-20 stems arising from common base (Ginns(1), describing Washington collection)
ODOR  fragrant, like pears^ (Bessette)
TASTE  mild^ (Bessette)
EDIBILITY  no (Bessette)
HABITAT  annual; associated with conifers and Rocky Mountain Maple in Idaho, with Ponderosa Pine, Douglas-fir and Southwestern White Pine in Arizona^, (Gilbertson), under conifers, sometimes at the base of tree or stump, Canfield concluded that it is "a lignicolous species (one basidiome was traced to buried wood) and caused a brown rot", (Ginns(1)), "in montane mixed conifer forests, sometimes associated with woody debris or buried wood", (Trudell)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Bessette)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4-5 x 3-4 microns, oval, inamyloid, colorless, thin-walled^; basidia usually 2-spored, some 4-spored, 17-20 x 5-6 microns, clavate; cystidia absent; pileipellis of puzzle-like cells 6.5-20 microns wide, slightly thick-walled, colorless in 3% KOH, cap trama similar but light reddish in 3% KOH, pore trama densely interwoven, mostly filamentous; clamp connections absent, (Bessette), spores 4-5 x 3-4 microns, oval to oblong, smooth, inamyloid, colorless; basidia mostly 2-spored, some 4-spored, 17-20 x 5-6 microns, clavate, simple-septate at base; cystidia absent; hyphae monomitic, hyphae of context 5-30 microns wide, "thin-walled, very contorted and lobed, with irregular vesicular expansions and constrictions, with occasional simple septa, tramal hyphae more uniformly 3-6 microns in diam, thin-walled, also occasionally simple-septate, sections red in KOH", (Gilbertson), spores 4.0-4.5 x 3.1-3.6 microns, nearly round, some broadly elliptic, smooth, colorless, thin-walled; basidia 4-spored; hyphae monomitic, context in KOH with numerous red oily droplets, hyphae simple-septate, irregularly inflated, some swelling spherical 40 microns wide, walls colorless and thin to irregularly thickened, (Ginns(1) who also notes that Canfield found clamp connections on some hyphae, especially in the margins of fruiting bodies)
NAME ORIGIN  means "expanded or spread out"^, (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Albatrellus dispansus), Ginns(1), Trudell(4)* (as Albatrellus dispansus), Bessette(1)* (as Polyporus dispansus), Audet(1), Ginns(28)

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Porodaedalea chrysoloma  (Fr.) Fiasson & Niemela^  Karstenia 24(1): 25. 1984;  Phellinus chrysoloma  (Fr.) Donk  Proc. K. Ned. Akad. Wet. (C) 74: 39. 1971;  Polyporus chrysoloma  Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  golden spreading polypore
NOTES  Features include shelf-like to flat growth on wood, a dull reddish brown upper surface that is grooved, zoned, and tomentose or with stiff erect hairs, the upper tomentum separated by a thin dark layer from the lower flesh, the pore surface bright yellowish brown before darkening, and microscopic characters including hymenial setae.^ RANGE Porodaedalea chrysoloma has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NB, NF, NS, NWT, ON, PQ, SK, YT, AK, AZ, CA, CO, MA, ME, MI, MN, MT, NH, NV, NM, NY, PA, UT, VT, WI, and WY, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  bent outwards to form cap from pore surface growing flat on wood, or shelf-like, up to 5cm wide, often entirely flat during early stages, shingled in clusters on standing trees, often in rows on fallen trees, caps thin and applanate (flattened), dimidiate [roughly semicircular] to elongate; upper surface dull reddish brown to bright yellowish brown at the margin; tomentose to hispid [with stiff erect hairs], grooved, zoned; margin usually wavy, slightly lobed, sharp, narrowly sterile underneath^, (Gilbertson), 1-8cm, thin, flat, crust-like, with sharp margin; "orangy brown to russet brown with a paler margin, becoming dark brown to blackish"; with concentric rings and hairy ridges, (Phillips)
FLESH  tough-fibrous; reddish brown, with a thin black line separating the soft upper tomentum from the lower context, dense lower layer up to 0.3cm thick, tomentum up to 0.1cm thick^, (Gilbertson), 0.1-0.3cm thick, "tawny or dingy yellow, separated from cap hair by black line", (Phillips)
PORES  1-3 per mm in most, 4-6 per mm in others, angular to slightly daedaleoid [maze-like]; bright yellowish brown at first, darkening; glancing [showing a change in appearance from dull to lustrous when the orientation to light is changed]; walls thin, entire to torn, entire tube layer up to 1cm thick, indistinctly stratified, inner surface ochraceous, paler than trama and flesh^, (Gilbertson), 2-5 per mm, angular to elongate; bright ocher-tawny; tube layer up to 0.5cm, accumulating layers annually, (Phillips)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  no (Phillips)
HABITAT  perennial or sometimes annual, on living and dead conifers in all genera of Pinaceae, causing a white pocket rot, "pathogenic in true firs, killing sapwood, and causing stem cankers in which the basidiocarps develop"^, (Gilbertson), sometimes perennial, "in dense overlapping clusters or partly fused rows on decaying or living conifer trunks", (Phillips)
SPORE DEPOSIT  light brown^ (Phillips)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4-5.5 x 4-5 microns, oval to nearly round, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, becoming pale yellowish brown, becoming slightly thick-walled^; basidia 4-spored, 10-12 x 5-6 microns, clavate, simple-septate at base; setae abundant, 25-60 x 7-10 microns, subulate, thick-walled, bright reddish brown in KOH; hyphal system dimitic: hyphae of fibrous context 2-4 microns wide, "thin-walled and almost hyaline to thick-walled and bright reddish brown in Melzer's reagent, darker brown in KOH, septate, occasionally to often branched, closely interwoven into a compact tissue", hyphae of upper tomentum 2-5 microns wide, "thin-walled and yellowish to thick-walled and bright reddish brown, septate, with rare branching", "dark layer between lower context and tomentum composed of dark, closely interwoven hyphae"; trama hyphae 2-3 microns wide, colorless to yellowish brown, thin-walled to slightly thick-walled, septate, with rare to occasional branching, (Gilbertson), spores 4.5-5.5 x 4-5 microns, nearly round, smooth, (Phillips)
NAME ORIGIN  means "with a gold-colored fringe or margin"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Porodaedalea pini has context up to 3cm thick, margin is typically blunt, pores are typically 2-3 per mm, setae are mostly 10-14 microns in diameter, and skeletal hyphae are 3.5-7.5 microns in diameter, whereas P. chrysoloma has context up to 0.4cm thick, margin is typically acute, pores are typically 4-6 per mm but some 1-3 per mm, setae are mostly 7-10 microns in diameter, and skeletal hyphae are 2-4 microns in diameter, (Ginns(28)). See also SIMILAR section of Fuscoporia viticola.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Phellinus), Ginns(28)*, Phillips(1)* (as Phellinus), Lincoff(2)* (as Phellinus)
189
FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Porodaedalea pini  (Brot.) Murrill^  Bull. Torrey bot. Club 32(7): 367. 1905;  Phellinus pini  (Thore: Fr.) A. Ames;  Fomes pini  (Thore: Fr.) P. Karst.;  Daedalea pini  Thore: Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  conifer maze polypore
NOTES  Features include a light reddish brown to blackish, rough and/or concentrically grooved, tough-fleshed fruitbody that is shelf-like, bracket-like, or growing flat on wood with the pore surface exposed, pores that are circular to angular or maze-like and yellowish brown to reddish brown, tough flesh that is yellowish brown to reddish brown, growth on conifers, and microscopic characters including setae in the hymenium. It is a major cause of timber loss.^ RANGE Porodaedalea pini is found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NWT, NB, NF, NS, ON, PE, PQ, SK, YT, AK, AL, AR, AZ, CA, CO, DE, FL, GA, IA, IL, IN, KS, KY, LA, MA, MD, ME, MI, MN, MS, MT, NC, NH, NJ, NM, NV, NY, OH, PA, SC, SD, TN, TX, UT, VA, VT, WI, WV, and WY, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  cap tissue blackening in KOH (Arora), all parts black in 3% KOH (Miller)
CAP  2-20cm broad and 1-15cm thick, shelf-like, hoof-like, or bracket-like, may be fan-shaped; tough or woody; tawny to rusty brown becoming brown to reddish brown or brownish black when old, margin sometimes brighter; "surface hard, often crusty but not shiny", "rough or cracked, minutely hairy or roughened (like sandpaper) at first", often concentrically grooved when old^, (Arora), bracket-like, bent outward from pore surface flat on wood to form shelf-like cap, or entirely flat on wood, caps up to 9cm x 13cm x 8cm, hoof-shaped or applanate [flattened]; upper surface light reddish brown to blackish; hirsute [hairy] toward margin, becoming bald and incrusted when old, zoned and grooved; margin reddish brown and hirsute, or sometimes yellowish brown and tomentose, rounded, (Gilbertson), margin acute (Allen)
FLESH  less than 1cm thick, tough; tawny to rusty reddish or ocher^, (Arora), up to 3cm thick, corky; reddish brown or yellowish brown, "lustrous on cut surface", "usually with one or more thin black layers, in younger specimens often appearing duplex because of the hirsute upper layer", (Gilbertson)
PORES  "2-5 per mm, round to irregularly sinuous"; ochraceous tawny to rusty brown becoming brown; tube layer 0.2-0.5cm thick^, (Arora), 2-3 per mm, circular to angular or daedaleoid [maze-like], yellowish brown, thick-walled; tube layers indistinctly stratified, each up to 0.6cm thick, light colored within a ground that is the same color as the flesh above it and continuous with the flesh, (Gilbertson)
STEM  none
ODOR  none^ (Miller)
TASTE  unknown^ (Miller)
EDIBILITY  no (Arora)
HABITAT  perennial, single or more often in rows or columns up and down living or recently fallen conifers, mostly on older trees^, (Arora), single or imbricate [shingled] on living conifers, a few reports on hardwoods, associated with a white pocket rot of the heartwood of living conifers, (Gilbertson), conks visible year round (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Arora), pale brown (Miller)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4.5-7 x 3.5-5 microns, oval, smooth, inamyloid, colorless or becoming slightly yellowish in older hymenia^; basidia 4-spored, 12-14 x 5-6 microns, broadly clavate, simple-septate at base; setae abundant, 40-50 x 10-14 microns, subulate [awl-shaped] to ventricose, thick-walled; hyphae of context of two types: 1) 3.5-7.5 microns wide, brown in KOH, thick-walled or thin-walled, rarely branched, simple-septate, 2) 2-3 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled, rarely branched, simple-septate, (Gilbertson), "spores 4-6 x 3.5-5 microns, round or nearly round, smooth", (Arora)
NAME ORIGIN  means "on pines [Pinus]"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Porodaedalea chrysoloma has context up to 0.4cm thick, margin is typically acute, pores are typically 4-6 per mm but some 1-3 per mm, setae are mostly 7-10 microns in diameter, and skeletal hyphae are 2-4 microns in diameter, whereas P. pini has context up to 3cm thick, margin is typically blunt, pores are typically 2-3 per mm, setae are mostly 10-14 microns in diameter, and skeletal hyphae are 3.5-7.5 microns in diameter, (Ginns(28)). See also SIMILAR section of Phellinopsis overholtsii.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Phellinus pini), Arora(1)* (as Phellinus pini), Phillips(1)* (as Phellinus pini), Miller(14)* (as Phellinus pini), Allen(1) (as Phellinus pini), Trudell(4)*, Ginns(28)*, Buczacki(1)* (as Phellinus pini), Siegel(2)* (as Porodaedalea pini group)

FAMILY  Porotheleaceae, Order Agaricales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Porotheleum fimbriatum  (Pers.) Fr.: Fr.^  Obs. Mycol. 1: 272. 1818;  == Poria fimbriata  Pers.;  Stromatoscypha fimbriata  (Pers.) Donk;  Porotheleum papillatum  Peck
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Porotheleum fimbriatum forms a whitish tube layer with rhizomorphs at the margin, developing from papillae that rupture at the top. If a well-developed pore layer is examined under a lens it is usually possible to see areas near the margin where there are still isolated cups (Reid). RANGE Porotheleum fimbriatum has been found in WA, ID, MB, NS, ON, AL, AR, AZ, CA, CO, CT, FL, GA, IL, IN, KY, LA, MA, MD, ME, MI, MN, MT, NH, SC, NC, NY, OH, PA, TN, TX, VA, VT, WI, WV, and circumglobally, (Gilbertson). It has also been recorded from PE, PQ, MO, Brazil, Guadeloupe, Venezuela, Austria, Czechoslovakia, France, Germany, Luxembourg, Portugal, Russia, Sweden, Switzerland, and the United Kingdom, (Cooke).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  pores developing by the formation of "an apical pore in isolated papillae which later become crowded or confluent to form typical tube layer"; margin conspicuously rhizomorphic, often up to 2-3cm wide^, (Gilbertson), receptacles at first papillate, becoming punctate, superficial to sunken, 0.03-0.1cm across, 0.03-0.1cm high, opening into crowded, often confluent cups, new papillae may be inserted among older cup; margin white, fimbriate [fringed] or torn, (Cooke), layer easily separable from wood; white margin up to 1cm and either byssoid or fringed with conspicuous rhizomorphic strands, (Reid)
FLESH  up to 0.1cm thick, soft-fibrous, not zoned; white to cream^, (Gilbertson), up to 0.05cm thick (Cooke)
PORES  3-5 per mm in mature specimens, circular to angular, thick-walled; tube layer up to 0.05cm thick, distinct from flesh, ivory to pale tan^, (Gilbertson), toward center 4-5 cups per mm, toward the margin 2-3 cups per mm, cream toward center, fading to "Naples yellow", tan, brown, or resinous brown, (Cooke), white or cream when fresh, more deeply colored when dried, (Reid)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  mild^ (Gilbertson)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on dead conifers and hardwoods, associated with a spongy white rot^, (Gilbertson), on dead wood of hardwood and coniferous trees and rarely even on bare soil, (Reid)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Reid)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4.5-5 x 2-3 microns, short-cylindric to elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 20-30 x 5-6 microns, cylindric to narrowly clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia absent; hyphae dimitic, generative hyphae of subiculum 2.5-3.5 microns wide, colorless in KOH, "thin-walled, rarely branched, with clamps", skeletal hyphae of subiculum 1.5-2.5 microns wide, "thick-walled, nonseptate, rarely branched"; hyphae of trama similar, (Gilbertson), spores 3-5(6) x 1.5-3.5 microns, elliptic, flattened on one side, smooth, colorless, apiculate; basidia 4-spored, 17-19 x 4.5-5.5 microns, clamp at base; cystidia not found; hymenium 20-25 microns thick; subiculum of tightly packed, colorless, "septate hyphae, rarely incrusted, arranged parallel to irregularly interwoven"; clamp connections on hyphae of subhymenial tissue, not seen on hyphae of subiculum, (Cooke), spores 4-5.2 x 2.2-3.2 microns from spore deposit, elliptic, colorless, thin-walled, with oil droplet; generative hyphae bear clamp connections, these hyphae may be difficult to demonstrate but most easily seen near cups and near growing margins, (Reid)
NAME ORIGIN  means "fringed, with a border of hairs"^, (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Ceriporia excelsa also develops from isolated pores, (Breitenbach).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Stromatoscypha), Cooke(1) (as Stromatoscypha), Reid(4) (as Stromatoscypha fimbriata), Ginns(5), Breitenbach(2)* (as Stromatoscypha)
191
FAMILY  Phanerochaetaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Porpomyces mucidus  (Pers.) Julich^  Persoonia 11(4): 425. 1982;  Ceriporiopsis mucida  (Pers.: Fr.) Gilb. & Ryvarden  Mycotaxon 22: 364. 1985;  Polyporus mucidus  Pers.: Fr.;  Poria mucida  Pers.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) flat growth on wood with the pore surface exposed, 2) soft texture when fresh, 3) a pale yellow pore surface, 4) rhizomorphs that are often present, 5) small nearly round spores, and 6) a monomitic hyphal system.^ The online Species Fungorum, accessed October 25, 2015, gave the current name as Ceriporiopsis mucida (Pers.) Gilb. & Ryvarden. The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where noted. RANGE Porpomyces mucidus has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, ON, AK, AR, CO, FL, MT, NY, and VA (Gilbertson). It is common in Europe, and circumpolar in distribution (Gilbertson), so presumably also found in Asia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, "separable, soft when fresh, friable when dry", up to 0.3cm thick, annual; margin "wide to narrow, with or without rhizomorphs, white to pale cream"
FLESH  subiculum about 0.1cm thick; white
PORES  3-5 per mm when regular angular, but in parts slightly sinuous and irregular, pores thin-walled; cream to pale chrome yellow or straw-colored, (Gilbertson), pore surface cream to pale yellow (Ginns)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on rotten wood of hardwoods and conifers, causing a white rot
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 2.5-3.5(4) x 2-2.5 microns, broadly elliptic to nearly round, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 10-12 x 4-6 microns, clavate; cystidia none; hyphal system monomitic, generative hyphae 2.5-4 microns wide, "with clamps, smooth or with small crystals, especially in the margin and the subiculum"
NAME ORIGIN  means "musty" or "moldy"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Anomoloma myceliosum is morphologically similar but has spores with amyloid walls (Ginns).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Ceriporiopsis mucida), Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia amara  (Hedgc.) M.J. Larsen & Lombard^  Mycotaxon 26: 272. 1986;  Tyromyces amarus  (Hedgc.) J. Lowe  Mycotaxon 2(1): 20. 1975;  Oligoporus amarus  (Hedgc.) Gilb. & Ryvarden  Mycotaxon 22: 364. 1985;  Polyporus amarus  Hedgc.
ENGLISH NAMES  Incense-cedar polypore
NOTES  Postia amara forms a large, hoof-shaped, tan to dark brown polypore with a yellow pore surface, specifically growing on living Incense-cedar.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1). RANGE Postia amara has been found in OR and CA, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  fruiting body hoof-shaped, cap 7-30cm broad and 4-20cm thick, convex; "whitish or buff to pale brown, sometimes darkening or becoming more ochraceous as it dries"; downy when young, usually bald when old but often becoming rough, wrinkled, or shallowly fissured, surface quite hard when old^, (Arora), up to 25cm x 18cm x 13cm, ungulate [hoof-shaped], tan to dark brown; tomentose to short hispid [bristly], smooth, not zoned, rugose [wrinkled] on drying; margin colored as upper surface, (Gilbertson)
FLESH  thick, soft and watery when fresh but very hard when dry; yellowish to pale brown^, (Arora), up to 11cm thick, tan, not zoned, becoming very hard on drying, (Gilbertson)
PORES  "2-3 per mm, (but may fuse to form larger pores), yellowish to bright lemon-yellow when fresh, often discoloring with age or handling"; tube layer 0.5-1.5cm thick^, (Arora), 1-3 per mm, circular to angular, with thin walls that become torn when old; "tan to brown on drying"; tube layer up to 2cm thick, colored as flesh or slightly darker, brittle when dry, (Gilbertson)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  mild^ (Gilbertson)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Arora)
HABITAT  annual, causes brown cubical pocket rot of heartwood of living Libocedrus decurrens (Incense-cedar), and "is the most important cause of volume loss in that host", fruiting bodies "deteriorate rapidly in the field and are usually quite high on the trunk of older trees"^, (Gilbertson)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6-7.5 x 3.5-5 microns, elliptic, smooth^, (Arora), spores 6-8.5 x 3.5-4.5 microns, elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless; basidia 4-spored, 31-60 x 7-9 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia absent; hyphae monomitic, generative hyphae in context 2.5-9 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled to thick-walled, rarely branched, with clamp connections, hyphae of trama 2.5-4 microns wide, mostly thin-walled, with abundant clamp connections, gloeoplerous hyphae also present, (Gilbertson)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'bitter'
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Oligoporus amarus), Arora(1) (as Tyromyces amarus)

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia angulopora  (M.J. Larsen & Lombard) M.J. Larsen & Lombard^  Mycotaxon 26: 272. 1986;  Oligoporus anguloporus  (M.J. Larsen & Lombard) Gilb. & Ryvarden  North American Polypores p.462. 1987;  Fibroporia angulopora  M.J. Larsen & Lombard;  Gilbertsonia angulopora  (M.J. Larsen & Lombard) Parmasto
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include flat growth on dead Douglas-fir wood, cream to buff pores of irregular size, and microscopic characters that include distinctive contorted thick-walled hyphae in trama.^ The online Species Fungorum, accessed December 11, 2013, gave the current name as Gilbertsonia angulopora (M.J. Larsen & Lombard) Parmasto. The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where noted. RANGE Postia angulopora has been found in BC and OR.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 20cm, growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, soft and fragile when fresh, hard and brittle when dry
FLESH  subiculum less than 0.1cm thick, chalky and brittle; white to cream-colored
PORES  pores highly variable, some over 1 mm and others up to 4 per mm, angular, with thin walls that become torn; cream to buff; tube layer up to 0.6cm thick, colored as pores or becoming pale buff
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, known only on dead fallen Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir)^, where it causes a brown cubical rot and cream-colored mycelial felts develop in shrinkage cracks
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5.5-7 x 4-5 microns, broadly elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 25-40 x 7-10 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia absent; hyphae appearing dimitic, generative hyphae of trama 3-6 microns wide, thin-walled, some with frequent branching, with clamp connections, other hyphae in trama are thick-walled, firm-walled to almost solid, often contorted and lobed or branched, colorless, mostly 3-6 microns wide but with some inflated parts up to 10 microns wide, in lower subiculum and mycelial felts of decayed wood the thick-walled hyphae are more uniform, 2-4 microns wide, most with rare branching, (Gilbertson), generative hyphae 3-6 microns wide, binding hyphae of 2 types a) in the trama, 3-6 microns wide, some segments swollen to 10 microns wide, "contorted, frequently branched, the tips broadly rounded (not attenuated)", and b) in the context next to the tube layer and in mycelial felts in the wood, 2-4 microns wide, "infrequently branched, walls parallel, thick", (Ginns)
NAME ORIGIN  means "having angular pores"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Fibroporia vaillantii is rather similar macroscopically and microscopically, and also causes a brown rot in Douglas-fir, but P. angulopora "differs mainly in the distinctive contorted thick-walled hyphae in the trama and in its lack of rhizomorphs", (Gilbertson). Antrodia gossypium sometimes has rhizomorphs and it lacks "the swollen, contorted, thick-walled binding hyphae", (Ginns).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Oligoporus anguloporus), Ginns(28)* (as Postia angulipora)

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia balsamea  (Peck) Julich^  Persoonia 11(4): 423. 1982;  Oligoporus balsameus  (Peck) Gilb. & Ryvarden  Mycotaxon 22: 364. 1985;  Polyporus balsameus  Peck;  Polyporus basiliaris  Overh.;  Tyromyces carbonarius  Murrill;  Tyromyces cutifractus  Murrill
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Postia balsamea produces a white fruitbody that is bracket-like, or flat with the pore surface exposed and bent outward to form a semicircular or elongated shelf-like cap. It fruits on living conifers. Microscopically there are fusoid cystidioles, typically embedded, sometimes rare, and spores measuring 3.5-4.5 x 2.5-3.0 microns.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where noted. RANGE Postia balsamea has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NB, NF, NS, ON, PQ, SK, AZ, CA, MI, MN, MT, NC, NH, NM, NY, PA, TN, WI, and Europe, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  1cm x 5cm x 0.5cm, bracket-like, or flat with pore surface exposed and bent outward to form semicircular or elongated shelf-like cap; whitish when fresh, usually becoming pale brownish when dry, faintly zoned; finely radially fibrillose, smooth or shallowly grooved; margin bent back or wavy, colored as cap surface
FLESH  up to 0.3cm thick, corky; white to buff, not zoned
PORES  5-6 per mm, angular, walls thin and soon torn; whitish when fresh, usually becoming cream to pale buff when dry; tube layer up to 0.5cm thick, colored as flesh or becoming darker
STEM  none
ODOR  pleasant, nut-like
TASTE  slightly resinous
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on living conifers, also dead conifer wood, rarely on hardwoods, causing brown cubical butt or trunk rot^, (Gilbertson), late summer to fall (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 3.5-4.5 x 2.5-3 microns, oblong to short cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 15-20 x 4-4.5 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia numerous to rare, embedded or rarely projecting, 11-22 x 5-7 microns, fusiform, non-incrusted to apically crusted, with basal clamp; hyphae monomitic, hyphae of context colorless in KOH, thin-walled to thick-walled, with clamp connections, some with frequent branching 2-3 microns wide, others with frequent branching, rather long, nonseptate, thick-walled fragments frequently present, 3-7 microns wide, (Gilbertson), cystidioles 11-22 x 5-7 microns, fusoid, typically embedded, sometimes rare, (Ginns)
NAME ORIGIN  means "inhabiting the balsam fir"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Oligoporus balsameus), Ginns(28)*, Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia caesia  (Schrad.) P. Karst.^  Revue mycol., Toulouse 3 (no. 9): 19. 1881;  Oligoporus caesius  (Schrad.: Fr.) Gilb. & Ryvarden  Mycotaxon 22: 365. 1985;  Polyporus caesius  Schrad.: Fr.;  Tyromyces caesius  (Schrad.: Fr.) Murrill;  Postia caesia  (Schrad.: Fr.) Karst.
ENGLISH NAMES  blue cheese polypore, conifer blueing bracket
NOTES  Postia caesia is a soft bracket or soft shelf that is white to gray, but usually tinged bluish, and has a bluish spore deposit.^ RANGE Postia caesia is found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NT, NB, NF, NS, ON, PE, PQ, SK, YT, AK, AL, AZ, CA, CO, CT, DE, IA, KY, LA, ME, MI, MO, MT, NC, NJ, NM, NY, OH, PA, TN, UT, VA, WI, WV, WY, and circumglobal, (Gilbertson), and Europe and Asia, (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  1-5(8)cm broad, shelf-like or bracket-like, fan-shaped to semicircular in outline, "soft and spongy or watery when fresh, tougher when dry"; "white to gray, but usually tinged or mottled bluish or blue-gray", not zoned; covered with soft whitish hairs^, (Arora), up to 5cm x 6cm x 1.5cm, bracket-like or growing flat with pore surface exposed and bent outward to form cap that is semicircular or narrow; "whitish, often with a tint of blue, sometimes bruising intensely blue"; finely tomentose to strigose (coarsely hairy), sometimes bald, (Gilbertson), 1-8cm across, 1-4cm wide, 0.3-1cm thick, semicircular, convex to flat, margin sharp; whitish, becoming blue-gray with age or hardening; covered with fine long hairs, (Phillips), 1-5(7.5)cm along wood, projecting outward 1-4cm, semicircular, fan-shaped, narrowly attached at center, sometimes rather decurrent onto wood, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  up to 1cm thick, spongy when fresh; "whitish or aging gray to yellowish"^, (Arora), up to 1cm thick, soft; white to bluish, (Gilbertson), "soft, spongy, watery when fresh"; white, gray or yellowish when old, (Phillips)
PORES  2-4 per mm, white or colored like cap; tube layer 0.2-0.8cm thick^, (Arora), 3-6 per mm, angular with thin walls that become torn; white to bluish; dull; tube layer up to 0.6cm thick, soft, fragile when dry, white, (Gilbertson), "2-4 per mm, circular to angular or elongated"; white, becoming grayish or gray-blue when old; tube layer 0.2-0.8cm thick, white becoming gray-blue, (Phillips), pores unequal, maze-like or aculeiform, (Lincoff(1))
STEM  none, although Lincoff(1) says sometimes with short stem
ODOR  often sweetish^ (Arora), often fragrant (Phillips), indistinct (Buczacki) 
TASTE  soapy^ (Phillips), sweet (Lincoff(1))
EDIBILITY  unknown^ (Arora), not edible (Phillips)
HABITAT  annual, usually single, on dead conifers and hardwoods^, (Gilbertson), single or in small groups on decaying hardwood and conifer wood, associated with a brown carbonizing decay, (Arora), summer to fall, (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  pale ashy-blue^ (Arora), bluish (Gilbertson), "pale blue or whitish with a gray-blue tinge", (Phillips)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5.5-7.5 x 1-2 microns, cylindric to allantoid [curved sausage-shaped], smooth, inamyloid or very weakly amyloid in masses, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 16-25 x 4.5-7 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia absent; hyphae monomitic, hyphae of context 2.5-7 microns wide, thin-walled to thick-walled, colorless, often branched, with abundant clamp connections; gloeoplerous hyphae also present, (Gilbertson), spores 3-6 x 1-2 microns, cylindric or sausage-shaped, smooth, (Arora), spores 4-5 x 0.7-1.0 microns, sausage-shaped, smooth, amyloid, (Phillips), spores 4-5.5 x 1.5-1.7 microns, cylindric-elliptic, allantoid, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, with droplets, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin, "blue-gray"^, (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  Postia perdelicata "is similar in most features but differs in lacking the bluish tints and having slightly shorter basidiospores" (Ginns). P. perdelicata also has pale bluish spores, but tends to be smaller (up to 2cm) and has a bitter taste, (Arora).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Oligoporus caesius), Ginns(28)*, Phillips(1)* (as Oligoporus caesius), Arora(1) (as Tyromyces caesius), Lincoff(2)* (as Tyromyces caesius), Lincoff(1)* (as Tyromyces caesius), Breitenbach(2)*, Courtecuisse(1)* (as Oligoporus caesius), Buczacki(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia ceriflua  (Berk. & M.A. Curtis) Julich^  Persoonia 11(4): 423. 1982;  Oligoporus cerifluus  (Berk. & M.A. Curtis) Ryvarden & Gilb.  Mycotaxon 22(2): 365. 1985;  Tyromyces cerifluus  (Berk. & M.A. Curtis) Murrill;  Spongiporus cerifluus  (Berk. & M.A. Curtis) A. David;  Postia minusculoides  (Pilat ex Pilat) Boulet;  Oligoporus minusculoides  (Pilat ex Pilat) Gilb. & Ryvarden  N. Amer. Polyp., Vol. 2: 476. 1987;  Leptoporus minusculoides  Pilat;  Tyromyces minusculoides  (Pilat ex Pilat) Bondartsev
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include small annual, pendent fruitbodies growing in crevices of rotten conifer logs, the fruitbody cream to pale ochraceous, soft, and fragile, the pores white to cream, and microscopic characters including a monomitic hyphal system.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) for Oligoporus minusculoides except where noted. Gilbertson mentions fusoid cystidioles but these were not found in the BC collection. In the BC collection, the generative hyphae were found to be dextrinoid en masse. RANGE Postia ceriflua has been found in WA, ON, NY, PA, TN, and Europe, (Gilbertson). In BC it is known from a single collection on Vancouver Island (Ginns(28)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 0.7cm wide, "pendent, with a narrowed basal attachment or rarely sessile, in crevices and hollows of very rotten logs", inconspicuous unless logs torn apart; cream to pale ochraceous, "hymenophore consisting of as few as 2 to 20-30 tubes", entire fruitbody "very soft and fragile and easily shattered into fragments when dry"
FLESH  less than 0.1cm thick, soft-fibrous
PORES  2-4 per millimeter, circular to irregular, white to cream; tube layer up to 0.3cm thick, colored as context
STEM  
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, in crevices and hollows of very rotten conifer logs, in North America on Tsuga (hemlock) and Pinus (pine), in Europe on Picea (spruce); associated generally with a brown cubical rot
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4.5-5.5 x 2-2.5 microns, oblong to short-cylindric, smooth, IKI-, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 25-36 x 5-6.5 microns, narrowly clavate, with basal clamp connection; cystidia none, fusoid cystidioles 22-35 x 5-6 microns, non-projecting, thin-walled, with basal clamp connection; hyphal system monomitic, context hyphae 3-5 microns wide, thin-walled, colorless, with clamp connections, rarely branched, tramal hyphae similar, (Gilbertson), spores (4.4)4.6-5.6 x (1.6)2.0 microns, cylindric, narrowly elliptic and suballantoid, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, thin-walled; basidia 4-spored, 15-23 x 4.5-5.0 microns, cylindric to narrowly clavate; cystidia and cystidioles lacking; generative hyphae 2-6 microns wide, with clamp connections, walls thin to 1.4 microns thick, dextrinoid en masse, (Ginns)
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Tyromyces cerifluus and Oligoporus minusculoides), Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia floriformis  (Quel.) Julich^  Persoonia 11(4): 423. 1982;  Oligoporus floriformis  (Quel.) Gilb. & Ryvarden  Mycotaxon 22: 365. 1985;  Polyporus floriformis  Quel.;  Tyromyces floriformis  (Quel.) Bondartsev & Singer
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include small, white fruiting bodies of variable shape, sometimes forming rosettes, with firm flesh, small angular pores, a bitter taste, and microscopic characters.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where noted. RANGE Postia floriformis is found in WA, OR, ID, MB, NS, ON, AZ, CA, CO, NC, TN, MA, MI, NH, NY, and PA, (Gilbertson). It is also known from BC (Ginns(28)) and Europe.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 2cm x 3cm x 0.4cm, laterally substipitate to bracket-like or bent outward to form shelf-like cap from pore surface growing flat on wood, usually imbricate [shingled], semicircular or tapering to a narrow base, petal-like, often laterally fused or in a rosette; white when fresh, drying straw-color, not zoned; bald
FLESH  up to 0.2cm thick, firm-corky; white, drying to ivory, not zoned
PORES  6-8 per mm, angular, with thick walls that become torn when old; white drying yellowish; tube layer up to 0.2cm thick, white becoming yellowish
STEM  none or lateral
ODOR  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
TASTE  bitter
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on dead hardwoods and conifers, causing brown cubical rot^, (Gilbertson), late summer to fall, (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 3.5-4.5 x 2-2.5 microns, oblong to short-cylindric, often slightly curved, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 11-12 x 4-5 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia absent, hyphal pegs rare; hyphae monomitic, hyphae of context 2.5-5.5 microns wide, colorless in KOH, thin-walled to thick-walled, occasionally branched, with clamp connections, hyphae of trama up to 3 microns wide, similar 
NAME ORIGIN  means "with the appearance of a flower"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Antrodiella semisupina has mild taste, slightly smaller spores, and a dimitic hyphal system, (Gilbertson).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Oligoporus floriformis), Ginns(28)*, Buczacki(1)*
