FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia fragilis  (Fr.) Julich^  Persoonia 11(4): 423. 1982;  Oligoporus fragilis  (Fr.) Gilb. & Ryvarden  Mycotaxon 22: 365. 1985;  Polyporus fragilis  Fr.;  Tyromyces fragilis  (Fr.) Donk;  Spongipellis sensibilis  Murrill
ENGLISH NAMES  staining cheese polypore
NOTES  {See also Amylocystis lapponica, Leptoporus mollis, Postia fragilis Table.} Features of Postia fragilis are the bracket-like or shelf-like, whitish to buff fruitbodies that turn reddish brown on bruising or drying, and its microscopic characters.^ RANGE Postia fragilis is found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, NB, NS, ON, PQ, AK, AZ, CA, CO, FL, MA, MD, ME, MI, MN, MT, NC, NH, NJ, NM, NY, PA, TN, UT, VA, WI, and WY, (Gilbertson), and Europe, Asia, and Australia, (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 4cm x 6cm x 1cm, bracket-like or bent outwards to form cap from pore surface growing flat on wood, dimidiate [semicircular] or elongated; whitish to buff, turning reddish brown on bruising or drying, not zoned; tomentose to bald^, (Gilbertson), 2-10cm broad, shelf-like or bracket-like, elongated or fan-shaped, "soft and spongy or watery when fresh, rigid and brittle when dry"; white, becoming reddish to pinkish red when old, "staining yellowish, then rusty to reddish when handled"; covered with soft white hairs, (Arora), 2-6cm along wood, projecting 2-4(5)cm, 0.5-1cm thick at attachment, bracket-like to fan-shaped; "yellow-ocher to orange-brownish", spotting brownish when handled, "shallowly undulating-tuberculate, somewhat radially fibrillose, finely tomentose and not or weakly zoned"; margin somewhat wavy, +/- sharp, white to ocherish, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  up to 1.5cm thick, fibrous; white, drying brownish, not zoned^, (Gilbertson), soft when fresh; white, discoloring like cap surface, (Arora), flesh 0.2-0.8cm thick, "succulent, fibrous, elastic", (Breitenbach)
PORES  5-6 per mm, circular to angular, with walls that become thin and torn; whitish to buff, becoming reddish brown on bruising or drying; tube layer up to 0.4cm thick, darker than flesh^, (Gilbertson), 3-5 per mm, "white, quickly bruising yellowish, then rusty-reddish"; tube layer 0.2-0.8cm thick, (Arora), 2-4 per mm, angular to labyrinthine [maze-like], usually decurrent onto wood; white, spotting brownish when handled; tube layer 0.2-0.5cm thick, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  strong (Phillips), insignificant (Breitenbach)
TASTE  mild^ (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Arora)
HABITAT  annual, single, on dead conifers, rarely on Populus^; associated with brown cubical rot of dead conifer logs and slash, (Gilbertson), single "or in groups or fused clusters on rotting conifers", (Arora), summer to fall (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  whitish^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4-5 x 1-1.5 microns, cylindric, mostly curved, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 13-20 x 4.5-5.5 microns, clavate; cystidia none; hyphae monomitic, hyphae of context 3-7 microns wide, colorless in KOH, thin-walled or thick-walled, occasionally branched, with clamp connections, hyphae of trama 2.5-4 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled or thick-walled, often branched, with clamp connections, (Gilbertson), 4-5 x 1-2 microns, cylindric to sausage-shaped, smooth, (Arora), spores 4.5-6 x 1.5-2 microns, cylindric, somewhat allantoid, smooth, colorless, with droplets, (Breitenbach), spores 4-5 x 1.0-1.5 microns, allantoid, hyphae 3-7 microns wide, walls thin to thick, hyphae on cap surface "mostly thin-walled in agglutinated pale yellow to pale brown strands", (Ginns(28))
NAME ORIGIN  means "brittle, fragile, easily broken"^, (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Amylocystis lapponica fruitbodies "resemble robust specimens of O. fragilis and also show the reddish-brown color change on bruising. The capitately incrusted amyloid cystidia of A. lapponica make it distinctive microscopically" - spore size is larger and pore size is slightly larger for A. lapponica, (Gilbertson). A lapponica has cylindric spores 8-11 x 2.5-3.5 microns, clamp connections, and amyloid metuloids, Leptoporus mollis has allantoid spores 5-6 x 1.5-2 microns, no clamp connections, and no cystidia, and Postia fragilis has allantoid spores 4-5 x 1-1.5 microns, clamp connections, and no cystidia, (Ginns(25)). Sarcoporia polyspora also stains reddish brown - it has elliptic to cylindric spores 5-7 x 2.5-4.0 microns, has clamp connections, and lacks cystidia. Postia lateritia, rarely reported, has larger pores (3-4 per mm), soft context, slightly larger pores measuring 4.5-6.3 x 1.0-1.8 microns, thick-walled cap hyphae (thin-walled in P. fragilis), and scattered gloeoplerous hyphae (Ginns(28)). See also SIMILAR section of Postia leucomallella.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Oligoporus fragilis), Arora(1)* (as Tyromyces fragilis), Phillips(1)* (as Oligoporus fragilis), Lincoff(2)* (as Tyromyces fragilis), Breitenbach(2)*, Ginns(25) (as Oligoporus fragilis), Buczacki(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Ginns(28)*, Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia guttulata  (Sacc.) Julich^ Persoonia 11(4): 423. 1982;  Oligoporus guttulatus  (Peck) Gilb. & Ryvarden  Mycotaxon 22: 365. 1985;  Polyporus guttulatus  Peck in Sacc.;  Tyromyces substipitatus  Murrill
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Postia guttulata forms a thin white bracket or shelf, occasionally with an attachment somewhat like a lateral stem, often with shallow circular depressions on the cap (0.1-0.3cm in diameter). There is an exudation of drops of liquid on most fresh fruitbodies. The pore surface is whitish, with a faint greenish cast, and the tube layer has a pale greenish tint when fresh. Taste is bitter.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where noted. RANGE Postia guttulata has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, MB, NF, NS, ON, PE, PQ, AZ, CA, IN, KY, MA, ME, MI, MN, MT, NC, NH, NY, OH, PA, TN, VA, VT, and WI, (Gilbertson(1) who also mention AK and Europe as part of range).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 9cm x 10cm x 2cm, bracket-like, bent outward to form shelf-like cap from pore surface growing flat on wood, or laterally substipitate [with attachment somewhat like lateral stem], dimidiate [semicircular] to fan-shaped, flat on upper surface; white when fresh, drying buff or pale brown, not zoned or faintly zoned; bald, smooth, pelliculose, often with small shallow circular depressions 0.1-0.3cm across^, (Gilbertson), there is an exudation of drops of liquid on most fresh fruitbodies (Ginns)
FLESH  up to 1.5cm thick, firm-fibrous; white to cream, not zoned
PORES  4-6 per mm, circular to angular, with thin walls that become torn when old; white to cream, drying pale buff; tube layer up to 0.5cm thick, with pale greenish tint when fresh, drying cream colored, continuous with flesh and colored like it, (Gilbertson), pore surface with a faint greenish cast, (Ginns)
STEM  none or with attachment somewhat like lateral stem
ODOR  
TASTE  bitter
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, single or imbricate [shingled], primarily on dead conifer wood, but also on hardwoods^, associated with a brown cubical rot, also associated with a brown butt rot of living Picea (spruce)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4-5 x 2-2.5 microns, short-cylindric to oblong, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 15-20 x 5-6 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia none, cystidioles 13-18 x 4-5 microns, fusoid, not projecting beyond basidia, with basal clamp; hyphae monomitic, hyphae of context with clamp connections, some very thick-walled with a narrow lumen, occasionally nodose-septate, commonly branching opposite the clamp, 4-8(12) microns wide, some with thin to partially thickened walls, with abundant clamp connections and occasional branching, 3-6 microns wide, gloeoplerous hyphae also present, 5-10(14) microns wide, "staining bright red in phloxine, thin-walled, with occasional distorted clamps, sinuous and with constrictions and swellings", hyphae of trama mostly of the thin-walled non-staining type
NAME ORIGIN  means "sprinkled with dots of resin or oil"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Postia stiptica is similar in appearance and microscopically and also grows on conifers and hardwoods, but it has a rough cap that "usually has small, black dots on the surface", (Ginns(28)). Tyromyces chioneus has a mild taste, and typically lacks these features of Postia guttulata: saucer-shaped depressions, drops of liquid on most fresh fruitbodies, and a faint greenish cast on the pore surface, (Ginns(28)).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Oligoporus guttulatus), Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia hibernica  (Berk. & Broome) Julich^  Persoonia 11(4): 423. 1982;  Oligoporus hibernicus  (Berk. & Broome) Gilb. & Ryvarden  Mycotaxon 22: 364. 1985;  Polyporus hibernicus  Berk. & Broome;  Polyporus subsericeomollis  Romell;  Leptoporus simani  Pilat
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Postia hibernica forms a thin, white pore surface growing flat on surface of wood, with bitter taste, allantoid spores, and often hyphoid cystidia with apical crystals.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where noted. RANGE Postia hibernica has been found in ID, AZ, and AB, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 0.15cm thick, growing flat on wood, separable, when old often with a loosened margin, widely spread out or small, often elongated along the wood grain, "soft when fresh, fragile to slightly tough when dry"; margin wide to narrow, white and finely pubescent [downy]
FLESH  very thin, white
PORES  "variable in size, in parts 3-4 per mm, but also some larger and 2-3 per mm, a few even about 1 mm wide", round to angular; "white to cream on drying"; tube layer up to 0.3cm thick, colored as pores
STEM  none
ODOR  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
TASTE  bitter
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on dead conifers, reported on Abies (fir) and Picea (spruce), causing a brown rot^, (Gilbertson), late summer to fall (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4-6(7) x 1-1.5 microns, allantoid [curved sausage-shaped], smooth, inamyloid, colorless, thin-walled^; basidia 4-spored, 10-15 x 4-5 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia variably present, 10-25 x 3-5 microns, embedded or projecting, clavate to hyphoid, smooth or with a small apical crown of crystals ("most easily observed in Melzer's reagent in very thin sections which should be mounted without tapping as the apical crown falls off very easily, in KOH the crystals dissolve rapidly"); hyphae monomitic, generative hyphae 2-4(5) microns wide, colorless, thin-walled, with numerous clamp connections
NAME ORIGIN  means "of Ireland"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Postia sericeomollis has oblong to narrowly elliptic spores measuring 4-5 x 2.0-2.5 microns, (Ginns(28)).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Oligoporus hibernicus), Buczacki(1)*, Ginns(28)

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia lateritia  Renvall^  Karstenia 32(2): 44. 1992;  Oligoporus lateritius  (Renvall) Ryvarden & Gilb.  Syn. Fung. (Oslo) 7: 417. 1993;  Postia lateritia  Renvall.  Karstenia 32: 44, 1992.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  {See also Amylocystis lapponica, Leptoporus mollis, Postia fragilis Table.} Features include narrow elongated shelves (may also grow flat) on pine wood that are soft when fresh, cream-colored at first but soon developing brownish to rusty brown stains, pores 3-4 per millimeter, and microscopic characters including allantoid spores, cystidioles, monomitic hyphal system, and pigmented thick-walled cap surface hyphae.^ The description derived from Ryvarden(2). RANGE Postia lateritia is known in western North America from one collection in BC (Ginns(28)), and it occurs also in North Europe (Ryvarden(2)). "This polypore was recognized in 1992; before that, specimens of P. lateritia may have been mislabelled as either P. fragilis or P. leucospongia." (Ginns(28), with Latin names italicized)
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  "resupinate, effused-reflexed, or sessile, usually with a narrow elongated pileus" [growing flat with pore surface exposed, or bent back to form a shelf, or as a bracket, usually a narrow elongated bracket], up to 10cm along the substrate, rarely above 1cm wide, and up to 1cm thick at the base, usually single, more rarely compounded and fused with adjacent fruitbodies, soft when fresh, fragile when dry; upper surface soft, tomentose, azonate, at first cream coloured, soon developing brownish to rusty brown spots or bruising rusty brown where touched; margin floccose, white and distinct, especially in resupinate specimens
FLESH  up to 0.2cm thick, soft; white to cream
PORES  (2)3-4 per millimeter, circular to angular and somewhat variable, white to cream, "soon becoming reddish to rusty brown in spots and also bruising rusty brown when touched"; rather thin dissepiments become deeply lacerate when old; tube layer up to 0.5cm thick, colored as pore surface
STEM  
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on Pinus (pine); causes a brown rot
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4.5-6 x 1.2-1.6 microns, allantoid, colorless, inamyloid^; basidia 4-spored, 13-22 x 3-5.5 microns, clavate to cylindric, with basal clamp connection; cystidia absent, some hyphoid cystidioles may occur in the hymenium, these 13-22 x 3-4.5 microns; hyphal system monomitic, contextual generative hyphae 2-5 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled to thick-walled, "with clamps although some scattered simple septa may occur"; gloeoplerous hyphae scattered throughout the fruitbody; hyphae of the cap surface slightly pigmented and usually thick-walled, (Ryvarden), spores 4.5-6.3 x 1.0-1.8 microns (Ginns(28))
NAME ORIGIN  refers to the brick-red color of the staining
SIMILAR  Postia fragilis is much more commonly reported, and has fibrous context and microscopic differences including slightly smaller spores measuring 4-5 x 1-1.5 microns and cap surface hyphae that are mostly thin-walled.
SOURCES  Ryvarden(2) (as Oligoporus lateritius), Renvall(1) (as Oligoporus lateritius), Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia leucomallella  (Murrill) Julich^  Persoonia 11(4): 423. 1982;  Oligoporus leucomallellus  (Murrill) Gilb. & Ryvarden  Mycotaxon 22: 364. 1985;  Tyromyces leucomallellus  Murrill
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Postia leucomallella forms a small, narrow. elongated cap along the upper edge of its decurrent tube layer. Microscopically there are usually light yellow gloeocystidia in the hymenium.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1). RANGE Postia leucomallella has been found in ID, MI, MN, NY, and PA (Gilbertson). In BC it is known only from one collection (Ginns(28)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 5cm long and 1cm wide, up to 1cm thick at base measured vertically, mostly with narrow elongated cap along the upper edge of a decurrent tube layer, individual caps may fuse laterally to form a wavy, lobed or partly imbricate [shingled] fruitbody, soft when fresh, tough to brittle when dry; "white to cream, often discolored by drying to straw-colored or dirty brownish, often somewhat unevenly and in radial lines"; at first finely velvety to finely fibrillose, when old becoming finely scrupose [rough with small projecting fascicles of hyphae] to smooth, mostly not zoned or weakly zoned
FLESH  0.1-0.2cm thick, white
PORES  mostly 3-4 per mm; angular, thin-walled, "in parts somewhat larger when dry and in oblique parts of the pore surface partly split, sinuous to irregular"; white to dark cream; tube layer up to 1cm thick, colored as pore surface
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on dead conifers, causing a brown rot
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4.5-6 x 1-1.7 microns, allantoid [curved sausage-shaped], smooth, inamyloid, colorless, thin-walled^; basidia 4-spored, 12-18 x 4-6.4 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; gloeocystidia "rare to abundant, apparently in some cases lacking completely", arising in subhymenium, mostly embedded, rarely projecting, 10-35 x 4-8 microns, cylindric to clavate and obtuse, colorless, thin-walled; hyphae monomitic, generative hyphae with clamp connections, colorless and 2-4.5 microns wide, "those of the subhymenium more narrow than those of the context, thin-walled to slightly thick-walled and with conspicuous clamps"
NAME ORIGIN  means "like a small white lock of matted wool"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Postia fragilis is similar but "becomes far more brown when touched or dried and lacks cystidia", (Gilbertson).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Oligoporus leucomallellus), Ginns(28)*
203
FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia leucospongia  (Cooke & Harkn.) Julich^  Persoonia 11(4): 423. 1982;  Oligoporus leucospongia  (Cooke & Harkn.) Gilb. & Ryvarden  Mycotaxon 22: 365. 1985;  Polyporus leucospongia  Cooke & Harkn.;  Tyromyces leucospongia  (Cooke & Harkn.) Bondartsev & Singer;  Spongipellis leucospongia  (Cooke & Harkn.) Murrill
ENGLISH NAMES  marshmallow polypore, white spongy polypore
NOTES  Postia leucospongia forms a whitish shelf or bracket with whitish pores growing on the underside of a tough white layer that is covered on the upper side with soft spongy cottony material that usually extends over the margin below the pore surface and often partly covers it. Fruitbodies develop under snowbanks in the spring on dead wood in the mountains.^ The online Species Fungorum, accessed October 25, 2015, gave the current name as Tyromyces leucospongia (Cooke & Harkn.) Bondartsev & Singer. The description is derived from Gilbertson(1). RANGE Postia leucospongia has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, YT, AK, AZ, CA, CO, MT, NM, NV, UT, and WY, (Gilbertson). It is also known from the Himalayas.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  2-10cm long, 1-5cm wide, shelf-like or bracket-like to somewhat irregular, usually elongated along wood, convex; "white to buff, grayish-buff, pinkish, or even brown or cinnamon; usually velvety and very soft or spongy to the touch, but sometimes papery and fairly firm if the velvety layer wears away; margin usually extending below the pore surface and sometimes partially covering it", ^(Arora), up to 3cm x 7cm x 3.5cm, flat on wood with pore surface exposed but bent back to form shelf-like cap, to bracket-like, dimidiate [roughly semicircular] to elongated; upper surface white, not zoned; smooth or rugose [wrinkled], tomentose to papery, soft; margin growing down and partially enclosing pore surface, rounded, sterile underneath, (Gilbertson), 3-10cm long, 1-4cm wide, 0.5-2.5cm thick, "somewhat rounded, soft and watery when fresh"; "white sometimes tinted dull cinnamon-pink"; smooth to slightly rugose [wrinkled]; "margin extends down partially over the pore surface and is sometimes reddish brown", (Bessette), single shelves, or elongated, or sometimes flat pore layers on the underside of logs, (Trudell)
FLESH  a rather tough white layer covered by a thick (up to 1.5cm), "soft, spongy layer of cottony tissue that is pallid to pinkish-buff or cinnamon"^, (Arora), up to 2cm thick, duplex: soft and cottony in upper part, firmer near the tubes; white, not zoned, (Gilbertson), up to 1.5cm thick, 2-layered, "with a soft, cottony upper layer and a firm zone just above the pores"; white, azonate, (Bessette), soft, cottony flesh gives light weight to the fruitbodies (Trudell)
PORES  2-3 per mm, angular, often becoming torn and tooth-like when old, "white when fresh, often discoloring (buff to tan, brownish, or cinnamon)" when old or when dried; tube layer 0.2-0.6cm thick^, (Arora), 2-4 per mm, circular to angular, white to pale buff; rough, walls at first thick, becoming thin and deeply torn; tube layer up to 0.5cm thick, pale buff, hard and brittle, (Gilbertson), "3-4 per mm, circular to angular, minutely roughened, surface soft, white to cream color", (Bessette)
STEM  absent or rudimentary^ (Arora)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette)
TASTE  mild^ (Gilbertson)
EDIBILITY  no (Arora)
HABITAT  annual, on dead conifer wood "that is under deep snow for several months, particularly fir and spruce, rarely on aspen", developing under snow and deteriorating rapidly with onset of snow melt and higher temperatures, found throughout the western mountains, associated with brown cubical rot^, (Gilbertson), single to gregarious on dead conifers, usually from fissures in bark, usually in the spring, often beginning development under the snow, but the fruiting bodies persist for months without decaying; causes a brown rot of both heartwood and sapwood, (Arora), on old logs and stumps of conifers at over 5000 feet altitude, found up to timberline, (Lincoff), single to several on decorticated [debarked] conifer logs, stumps, and limbs; Picea engelmannii (Engelmann Spruce) and Abies lasiocarpa (Subalpine Fir) are common hosts, but on other conifers as well; "fruiting on wood covered with snow, reaching maturity shortly after the snow recedes; from May to July", (Bessette)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4.5-6 x 1-1.5 microns, allantoid [curved sausage-shaped], smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 16-23 x 4-5 microns, clavate, with basal clamp connection; cystidia absent, hyphal pegs present; hyphae monomitic, hyphae of context 3-7 microns wide, "with abundant clamps, rarely with double clamps, thin- to thick-walled, with frequent branching", "some contorted, with short, nodular branches or swellings and appearing twisted", hyphae of trama similar to non-contorted type, (Gilbertson), spores 4-6 x 1-1.5 microns, more or less sausage-shaped, smooth, (Arora)
NAME ORIGIN  means "white and spongy"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Compare also with Postia ptychogaster.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Oligoporus leucospongia), Ginns(28)*, Arora(1)* (as Tyromyces leucospongia, he notes it is also called Spongiporus leucospongia and Spongipellis leucospongia), Lincoff(2) (as Spongipellis leucospongia, he notes it is also called Polyporus leucospongius, Bessette says the illustration is probably Oligoporus fragilis, treated in this program as Postia fragilis), Bessette(1)* (as Oligoporus leucospongia), Trudell(4)* (as Oligoporus leucospongia), Desjardin(6)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia mappa  (Overh. & J. Lowe) M.J. Larsen & Lombard^  Mycotaxon 26: 272. 1986;  Oligoporus mappus  (Overh. & J. Lowe) Gilb. & Ryvarden  Mycotaxon 22: 365. 1985;  Poria mappa  Overh. & J. Lowe
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) a white to pale buff pore surface growing flat on wood with a fringed margin, 2) flesh that is very thin, and 3) microscopic characters including large narrowly cylindric spores and abundantly clamped and branched hyphae.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1). RANGE Postia mappa has been found in BC, ID, NF, and NY, (Gilbertson(1) who give a range that includes AK). It is known in BC from one report in a reference published in 1946 (Ginns).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 10cm across, growing flat on wood, readily separable, soft when dry; margin up to 0.1cm wide, white to cream, fertile or sterile, soft, fimbriate [fringed]
FLESH  "often extremely thin with practically no context"
PORES  3-4 per mm, circular to angular, with finely tomentose walls that become thin when old; white to pale buff; tube layer up to 0.1cm thick, often much narrower, whitish to ivory or pale buff
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  mild
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, mainly on dead conifer wood, a few records on hardwood, rail fences and timber bridges are likely substrates; associated with brown cubical rot
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-12 x 2-3 microns, cylindric, slightly curved, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored 16-21 x 6.5-8 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia none; hyphae monomitic, generative hyphae of context 2.5-4 microns wide, colorless in KOH, thin-walled to thick-walled, often branched, with abundant clamp connections, hyphae of trama 2-2.5 microns wide, similar
NAME ORIGIN  means "like a napkin"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Oligoporus mappus), Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia perdelicata  (Murrill) M.J. Larsen & Lombard^  Mycotaxon 26: 272. 1986;  Oligoporus perdelicatus  (Murrill) Gilb. & Ryvarden  Mycotaxon 22: 365. 1985;  Tyromyces perdelicatus  Murrill;  Polyporus perdelicatus  (Murrill) Murrill
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include small white soft shelf-like to bracket-like fruitbodies on conifer wood, small pores, a pale bluish spore deposit according to one source, and microscopic characters.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where specified. RANGE Postia perdelicata has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, NT, AZ, CA, and MT, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 1cm x 3cm x 0.2cm, bent outward to form semicircular or elongated shelf-like cap from pore surface growing flat on wood, or bracket-like; dimidiate [semicircular] to elongated; white
FLESH  up to 0.1cm thick, soft to firm near tubes; white, not zoned
PORES  5-7 per mm, circular to angular, with thin walls that become torn when old; [presumably white or whitish as color of fruitbody given as pure white]; tube layer up to 0.1cm thick, colored as flesh and continuous with it
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  mild (Gilbertson), bitter (Lowe), bitter (Arora)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, single, on dead wood of conifers, rarely on hardwoods, associated with brown cubical rot of dead conifers

NAME ORIGIN  means "completely dainty, fastidious"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Postia caesia also has pale bluish spores, but usually has bluish tints in the fruiting body, is often larger, and lacks a bitter taste, (Arora, but note Gilbertson(1) give taste as mild for Postia perdelicata). P. caesia is rather similar but P. perdelicata is pure white and lacks the bluish tints typical of P. caesia, (Gilbertson).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Oligoporus perdelicatus), Ginns(28)*, Arora(1) (as Tyromyces perdelicatus), Lowe, J.L.(2)

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia placenta  (Fr.) M.J. Larsen & Lombard^  Mycotaxon 26: 272. 1986;  Oligoporus placenta  (Fr.) Gilb. & Ryvarden  Mycotaxon 22: 365. 1985 [as 'placentus'];  Rhodonia placenta  (Fr.) Niemela, K.H. Larss. & Schigel  in Niemela, Kinnunen, Schige & Larsson Karstenia 45(2): 79, 2005;  Tyromyces placenta  (Fr.) Ryvarden;  Ceriporiopsis placenta  (Fr.) Domanski;  Leptoporus placenta  (Fr.) Pat.;  Physisporus placenta  (Fr.) P. Karst.;  Polyporus placenta  Fr.;  Poria placenta  (Fr.) Cooke;  Ceriporiopsis incarnata  (Alb. & Schwein.) Domanski;  Ceriporia incarnata  (Pers.) Bondartsev;  Poria incarnata  Pers.;  Poria carnicolor  D.V. Baxter;  Poria microspora  Overh.;  Poria monticola  Murrill

NOTES  Features include a salmon-pink pore surface growing flat on wood, mild taste, and microscopic characters.^ The online Species Fungorum, accessed October 25, 2015, gave the current name as Rhodonia placenta (Fr.) Niemela, K.H. Larss. & Schigel, in Niemela, Kinnunen, Schige & Larsson, Karstenia 45(2): 79, 2005. The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where noted. RANGE Postia placenta has been found in BC, WA, ID, AB, ON, AZ, CO, MD, MI, MT, NM, NV, and NY, (Gilbertson).

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia ptychogaster  (F. Ludw.) Vesterh.^  in Knudsen & Hansen, Nordic Jl Bot. 16(2): 213. 1996;  Oligoporus ptychogaster  (F. Ludw.) Falck & O. Falck;  Leptoporus ptychogaster (F. Ludw.) Pilat;  Tyromyces ptychogaster  (F. Ludw.) Donk
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Postia ptychogaster may be found in its non-sexual stage (with chlamydospores) which typically precedes formation of the tube layer and pore surface (with basidiospores). The non-sexual (imperfect) stage is a soft, cream-colored cushion covered with minute spines, and its core becomes powdery gray-brown. The sexual fruitbody, up to several centimeters in diameter, often develops on the underside of this cushion and can form a semicircular or spatula-shaped cap or a spread-out layer on the substrate. There is sometimes a lateral stem. The cap when present is white and downy, while the pore surface is pallid.^ The description is derived from Ginns(28). RANGE Postia ptychogaster is known in western North America from BC, WA, OR, and CA.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  "effuse to pileate, annual, dimidiate, sessile, spathulate or laterally stipitate, only several centimetres in diameter, when dry fragile, frequently developing on the lower surface of the imperfect stage" which typically precedes the development of the tube layer; imperfect stage "sessile, cushion-shaped", up to 4cm x 5cm, "soft, cream, surface covered with minute spines", "core becoming powdery, grey brown"; cap surface "white, azonate, finely pubescent"
FLESH  context duplex, about 1.4cm thick with a dense, waxy, 0.4cm thick mycelial layer adjacent to tube layer, "from this dense layer hyphal strands permeate (like veins or roots) a soft, cottony mycelial layer" 1.0cm thick
PORES  2(3) per millimeter, angular, "edges thin, lacerate", pore surface pallid; tubes pallid, up to 0.3cm deep
STEM  none or lateral
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, in BC on a mossy log
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  basidiospores 3.8-4.4 x 2.4-2.8 microns, elliptic to nearly oblong, "some narrowed slightly over the apical one-third", smooth, inamyloid, colorless, walls about 0.3 microns thick, cyanophilic, apiculus minute^; chlamydospores 5.6-8.0(10.0) x 3.5-6.0 microns, elliptic to nearly oblong, "some with ends truncated", smooth, dextrinoid, pale yellow brown, cyanophilic, walls about 0.4 microns thick; hyphae with clamp connections, walls colorless and thin, of two types: 1) "in the cottony context typically curved", 2-3 microns wide, with "prominent clamp connections", and 2) "in the dense context adjacent to the tubes and in the strands, essentially parallel, straight", (2.4)3.0-5.0(8.0) microns wide; tramal hyphae "straight, vertically woven", 3-4 microns wide, walls typically 0.5 microns thick, "en masse weakly to moderately intensely dextrinoid"
NAME ORIGIN  Greek ptyx, ptychos means fold, leaf, layer or plate; Greek gaster means stomach, belly, pauch, or womb
SIMILAR  Postia leucospongia "also has a soft, spongy context but is often near receding snow banks", has allantoid spores only 1.0-1.5 microns wide, "and lacks chlamydospores", (Ginns(28)).
SOURCES  Ginns(28)*, Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia sericeomollis  (Romell) Julich^  Persoonia 11(4): 423. 1982;  Oligoporus sericeomollis  (Romell) Pouzar;  Polyporus sericeomollis  Romell;  Poria asiatica  (Pilat) Overh.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include flat growth on conifers, a whitish pore surface, extremely bitter taste, and microscopic characters including incrusted cystidia and a monomitic hyphal system.^ This species and Phellinidium weirii are the two most common causes of butt and trunk decay in Western Red-cedar (Allen(1)). The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where noted. RANGE Postia sericeomollis has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, NT, NB, NS, ON, AZ, CO, FL, MT, NC, NY, PA, SC, TN, WY, Europe, and Asia, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 15cm across, growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, readily separable, corky when dry; margin less than 0.1cm wide, fertile or narrowly sterile, whitish
FLESH  less than 0.1cm thick; whitish, not zoned
PORES  4-6 per mm, circular to angular, with thin walls; white or discolored yellowish or tan; tube layer up to 0.3cm thick, drying brittle, colored as flesh and continuous with it
STEM  none
ODOR  indistinct^ (Buczacki)
TASTE  extremely bitter
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on living and dead conifers, associated with a brown cubical pocket rot, said to be the main cause of volume loss in living Western Red-cedar in BC^, (Gilbertson), on live and dead conifers, especially in living Thuja plicata (Western Red-cedar), (Ginns(28) for BC), mainly summer, fall, winter, (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  whitish^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4-5 x 2-2.5 microns, oblong to cylindric-elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 18-20 x 5-6 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia occasional to abundant, barely projecting, 14-26 x 6-10 microns, "ventricose, very thick-walled, some apically incrusted", "developing from right-angle branches from tramal hyphae or as apical portions of unbranched tramal hyphae"; hyphae monomitic, hyphae of subiculum 2-4 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled to thick-walled, "abundantly nodose-septate, with occasional branching", hyphae of trama similar
NAME ORIGIN  means "silky, soft"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Postia hibernica has allantoid spores that measure 5-6 x 1.0-1.5 microns, (Ginns(28)).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Oligoporus sericeomollis), Ginns(28)*, Buczacki(1)*, Allen(1)

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia stiptica  (Pers.) Julich^  Persoonia 11(4): 424 1982;  Oligoporus stipticus  (Pers.: Fr.) Gilb. & Ryvarden  North American Polypores p.485. 1987;  Tyromyces stipticus  (Pers.) Kotl. & Pouzar;  Polyporus stipticus  Pers.;  Polyporus immitis  Peck
ENGLISH NAMES  bitter bracket
NOTES  Features include bracket-like or shelf-like whitish fruitbodies often with a rough upper surface, often with small black spots on upper surface, very bitter taste, growth on conifer wood and hardwood, and microscopic characters.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where noted. RANGE Postia stiptica has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, MB, NB, NF, NS, ON, AZ, CA, MA, MI, MT, NH, NY, OH, PA, TN, VT, and Europe, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  up to 6cm x 11cm x 2cm, bracket-like, or bent outward to form shelf-like cap(s) from pore surface growing flat on wood; dimidiate [semicircular] to elongated; ivory to pale buff, often with small black spots, not zoned; often rough, bald
FLESH  up to 1cm thick, fissile; white, not zoned
PORES  5-6 per mm, circular to angular, with thin walls that become torn when old; white to ivory; tube layer up to 0.8cm thick, colored as flesh and continuous with it
STEM  none
ODOR  strong, indefinite, unpleasant^, (Buczacki)
TASTE  very bitter
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, single or in small clusters, on dead wood of conifers and hardwoods, associated with brown cubical rot^, (Gilbertson), summer to fall (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 3.5-5 x 1.5-2 microns, "oblong to short-cylindric, some slightly curved", smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 12-19 x 4.5-5.5 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia absent; hyphae monomitic, hyphae of context 2-5 microns wide, (gelatinizing and swelling to 8 microns wide), colorless in KOH, occasionally branched, thin-walled to thick-walled, with clamp connections, hyphae of trama 2-2.5 microns wide, rather thin-walled, with clamp connections
NAME ORIGIN  means "bitter in taste" (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Postia guttulata is similar microscopically and the small white fruitbodies of P. stiptica with a coarse, usually rough upper surface are the principal basis of separation, (Gilbertson). P. guttulata has a weakly zoned cap with saucer-shaped depressions 0.1-0.3cm in diameter, an exudation of drops of liquid on most fresh fruitbodies, and a faint greenish cast to the pore surface, and it lacks the typical black dots, (Ginns(28)). Tyromyces chioneus has a mild taste, lacks the typical black dots, and is restricted to hardwoods, (Ginns(28)). Postia tephroleuca has longer more narrow slightly curved spores, (Gilbertson).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Oligoporus stipticus), Ginns(28)*, Breitenbach(2)*, Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Postia tephroleuca  (Fr.) Julich^  Persoonia 11(4): 424. 1982;  Oligoporus tephroleucus  (Fr.) Gilb. & Ryvarden  Mycotaxon 22: 365. 1985;  Polyporus tephroleucus  Fr.;  Tyromyces tephroleucus  (Fr.) Donk;  Polyporus lacteus  Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  greyling bracket
NOTES  Features include 1) small, white to mouse-gray, bracket-like or shelf-like fruitbodies that coarsely hairy on their upper surface, 2) usually concentrically zoned flesh, 3) growth on conifers and hardwoods and associated with a brown cubical rot, and 4) microscopic characters.^ RANGE Postia tephroleuca has been found in BC, ID, MB, ON, PQ, AZ, DE, IA, IN, KS, MD, ME, MO, MT, NC, NE, NH, NJ, NY, OH, PA, TN, VT, and WV, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  cap up to 2cm wide, bracket-like, or bent outward to form shelf-like cap from pore surface growing flat on wood, cream to mouse-gray; coarsely strigose [hairy]^, (Gilbertson), 4-10cm along wood, projecting 2-5cm, up to 4cm thick where attached, bracket-like, broadly attached, triangular in cross-section, "upper surface convex to flat and spread out"; "whitish at first, then gray-brownish"; "finely tomentose to smooth, somewhat undulating"; margin obtuse, more or less sharp when old, (Breitenbach)
FLESH  up to 1cm thick; whitish, usually concentrically zoned^, (Gilbertson), 1-1.5cm thick, soft, fragile, hard and brittle when dry; white, (Breitenbach)
PORES  3-4 per mm, walls thin and finely torn; whitish when fresh, yellowish on drying; tube layer up to 0.8cm thick, brittle on drying, white to cream^, (Gilbertson), 4-5 per mm, rounded-angular; white; tube layer up to 1cm thick, (Breitenbach)
STEM  none
ODOR  mild^ (Buczacki)
TASTE  mild^ (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on dead wood of conifers and hardwoods, associated with brown cubical rot^, (Gilbertson), single to shingled on dead hardwood, more rarely on conifers, (Breitenbach), summer to fall (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4.5-6 x 1-1.5 microns, cylindric, slightly curved, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 14-16 x 4-5 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia absent, hyphal pegs present; hyphae monomitic, hyphae of context 3-8 microns wide, thin-walled to moderately thick-walled, "abundantly nodose-septate, some with frequent branching", hyphae of trama 2-4 microns, mostly thin-walled, with clamp connections, (Gilbertson), spores 3.0-6.0 x 1.0-1.5 microns (Ginns), spores 4-5 x 1-1.5 microns, cylindric, allantoid [curved], smooth, inamyloid, colorless, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  means "a combination of ash-colored and white"^, (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Tyromyces chioneus can be confused with specimens of P. tephroleuca that do not have the mouse-gray color: T. chioneus has slightly wider spores, (mostly 4-5 x 1.5-2 microns), a dimitic hyphal system with characteristic branched generative hyphae, and produces a white rot, (Gilbertson) . See also SIMILAR section of Postia stiptica.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Oligoporus tephroleucus), Ginns(28)*, Breitenbach(2)*, Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Hymenochaetaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Pseudoinonotus dryadeus  (Pers.) T. Wagner & M. Fisch.^  Mycol. Res. 105(7): 781. 2001;  Inonotus dryadeus  (Pers.: Fr.) Murrill  North Am. Flora 9: 86. 1908;  Polyporus dryadeus  Pers.
ENGLISH NAMES  warted oak polypore, oak bracket
NOTES  Features include 1) large semicircular, flattened fruitbodies without a stem 2) growth mostly on true fir (also hemlock and in eastern North America oak), 3) an upper surface that is bumpy, pitted, and whitish (buff) to darker brown, 4) a thick, rounded margin colored as the upper surface or ivory-colored, 5) yellowish brown to reddish brown mottled flesh, 6) a whitish (buff) to darker brown pore surface with a sheen that varies with incident light, 7) exudation of amber water droplets from the upper surface and pore surface when fresh, and 8) microscopic characters, including nearly round, colorless spores and strongly ventricose, hooked setae.^ RANGE Pseudoinonotus dryadeus has been found in BC, OR, AR, AZ, CA, GA, IL, IN, KY, LA, MD, MO, NC, NM, NY, OH, OK, PA, TN, TX, VA, and WV, (Gilbertson). It also occurs in Europe, Asia, (Breitenbach)
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  flesh black with KOH (Lincoff)
CAP  7.5-40cm wide, "single or sometimes fused, convex to irregular, with thick rounded margin, exuding water drops when young; whitish to gray, becoming gray-yellow, red-brown, or blackish; flat-hairy, becoming smooth; uneven to warty or bumpy"^, (Lincoff), up to 23cm x 35cm x 15cm, without stem, single or imbricate [shingled], applanate (horizontally flattened), semicircular, annual; upper surface buff to dark brown, margin the same color or sometimes ivory; not zoned, very finely tomentose or bald, becoming cracked when old, (Gilbertson)
FLESH  1.5-5cm thick, soft and spongy when fresh; yellow-brown to rust-brown^, (Lincoff), up to 10cm thick, soft, fibrous, zoned, cut surface appearing mottled because of streaks of darker softer tissue; "bright yellowish-brown at first to reddish-brown in older specimens", (Gilbertson)
PORES  3-5 per mm, "round to angular; whitish to gray-brown, often with silvery sheen, becoming dark"; tube layer 0.5-3cm thick^, (Lincoff), 4-6 per mm, circular or angular, with thin entire walls; "buff, often with exuding droplets of amber liquid in fresh specimens, becoming dark brown and cracking with age"; tube layer up to 2cm thick, colored as flesh, (Gilbertson)
STEM  none or very short
ODOR  intensely unpleasant^ (Breitenbach)
TASTE  mild, somewhat acidic^, (Breitenbach)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, primarily on Abies (fir) in the Pacific Coast region, in eastern North America primarily on Quercus (oak); typically developing at ground line at the base of trees or from roots at some distance from base^, causes white rot of heartwood in butts and roots of living true firs and oaks, (Gilbertson), on live and dead Abies (true fir), Tsuga (hemlock), and Quercus (oak), causing a white rot, (Ginns)
SPORE DEPOSIT  yellow-white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6-8 x 5-7 microns, nearly round, smooth, dextrinoid^, cyanophilous, colorless, becoming thick-walled; basidia 4-spored, 14-16 x 9-11, broadly clavate to ovoid; setae usually frequent, rare in some specimens, 25-40 x 9-16 microns, ventricose, usually hooked; context hyphae 5-14 microns, "varying from pale brown in KOH and thin-walled to dark brown and thick-walled, with occasional branching, simple-septate", with a gummy incrustation in some areas, trama hyphae 5-9 microns wide, thin-walled to moderately thick-walled, uniformly pale brownish, simple-septate, with rare branching, (Gilbertson), spores 7-8.5 x 6.5-8 microns, round, smooth; setae present, (Lincoff)
NAME ORIGIN  means "similar to a dryad or wood nymph"^, (Gilbertson), "refers to association with oak" (Lincoff)
SIMILAR  Inocutis dryophilus also occurs on oaks but "has a hard, dark-brown mycelial core which is permeated with light veins, and in addition lacks setae", (Breitenbach). See also SIMILAR section of Inocutis dryophila and Onnia tomentosa.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Inonotus dryadeus), Ginns(28)*, Lincoff(2)* (as Inonotus dryadeus), Breitenbach(2)* (as Inonotus dryadeus), Buczacki(1)* (as Inonotus dryadeus), Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Pycnoporellus alboluteus  (Ellis & Everh.) Kotl. & Pouzar^  Ceska Mykol. 17: 174. 1963;  Fomes alboluteus  Ellis & Everh.;  Polyporus alboluteus  (Ellis & Everh.) Ellis & Everh.
ENGLISH NAMES  orange sponge polypore
NOTES  Features include an orange sponge-like fruiting body with large pores that often become tooth-like, and flat growth on the underside of conifer wood from which it peels off in a continuous layer. It is conspicuous in the spruce-fir zone of western mountains.^ RANGE Pycnoporellus alboluteus is found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, ON, AK, AZ, CA, CO, MI, MT, NM, NY, UT, and WY, (Gilbertson), and PQ (Lincoff).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  all tissues quickly cherry red in KOH, (Gilbertson)
CAP  often spread out up to 100cm or more, growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, or slightly bent outward to form shelf-like cap, soft and spongy, loosely strigose [hairy]; bright orange when fresh^, (Gilbertson), surface of cap when present bright orange but fading or weathering to whitish, (Arora)
FLESH  up to 0.2cm thick, soft-felty; pale orange, not zoned^, (Gilbertson)
PORES  mostly over 1mm wide, angular, walls splitting to form teeth; tube layer up to 2cm thick, colored as flesh and continuous with it^, (Gilbertson), 1-3mm or more wide, angular, the walls often jagged or splitting to form teeth; whitish or yellowish soon becoming orange (or tubes orange with white edges); tube layer 1-3cm, (Arora)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  not known (Phillips)
HABITAT  annual, on dead conifers, especially Picea (spruce), occasionally on aspen, associated with brown cubical rot of conifer and aspen logs covered by snow in winter^, (Gilbertson), on fallen logs of conifers, usually on undersides, often developing in snow, mainly in spring but persisting later in the year, (Arora), on conifers, especially Picea, infrequently on Populus, developing "in spring in association with receding snow banks", (Ginns)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 9-14 x 3-3.5 microns, cylindric, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 30-45 x 6-7 microns, clavate, simple-septate at base; cystidia frequent, 60-120 x 5-10 microns, cylindric, thin-walled to moderately thick-walled, colorless, not incrusted, simple-septate at base; hyphae monomitic, hyphae of context 2-10 microns, bright reddish then colorless in KOH, thin-walled to thick-walled, with frequent branching, appearing rough because of a thin incrustation, simple-septate, hyphae of trama 3-5 microns wide, similar, (Gilbertson), spores (7)10-14 x 3-4 microns, (Arora)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin words meaning "white" and "yellow"^, (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
SIMILAR  Pycnoporellus fulgens is commonly bracket-like or bent outward from pore surface to form cap, and has smaller pores, spores, and cystidia, (Gilbertson). P. fulgens is typically semicircular or distinctly reflexed (bent outward to form a cap) with smaller pores 2-3 per mm, smaller cystidia measuring 45-60 x 4-6 microns, and smaller spores measuring 6-9 x 2.5-4.0 microns, (Ginns).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Phillips(1)*, Arora(1) (as Phaeolus alboluteus), Lincoff(2)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)*, Trudell(4)*, Desjardin(6)*, Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Fomitopsidaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Pycnoporellus fulgens  (Fr.) Donk^  Persoonia 6: 216. 1971;  Hydnum fulgens  Fr.;  Polyporus fibrillosus  P. Karst.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include pale orange to rust-colored bracket-like to shelf-like fruitbodies that are tomentose or bald becoming bristly or radially fibrillose, often zoned, and pale orange pores and flesh that turn red with KOH.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1) except where noted. RANGE Pycnoporellus fulgens has been found in BC, WA, OR, ID, also AB, MB, NF, NS, ON, PQ, PE, AK, CA, CO, MA, ME, MI, MN, MT, NH, NY, PA, VT, WI, and WV, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  flesh and tubes red with KOH
CAP  up to 6cm x 9cm x 2.5cm, bracket-like or bent outward from pore surface flat on wood to form shelf-like cap, dimidiate [roughly semicircular] to long and narrow; "pale orange to rust-colored"; tomentose or bald when young to hispid [bristly] or radially fibrillose when old, often zoned
FLESH  up to 0.5cm thick, sometimes duplex: lower layer firm and corky and upper layer soft and fibrous; light orange
PORES  2-3 per mm, circular to angular, walls thin becoming torn when old; pale orange; tube layer up to 0.6cm thick, colored as flesh or sometimes paler orange
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, single or imbricate [shingled], "mainly on conifer logs and slash but also occasionally on hardwoods", associated with brown cubical rot of dead conifers and hardwoods, (Gilbertson), in a Haida Gwaii study, Kroeger(5) found that it was almost invariably associated with old fruitbodies of Fomitopsis pinicola complex, on some cases on the fruitbodies themselves
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6-7(9) x 2.5-4 microns, short-cylindric to oblong, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 25-30 x 5-5.5 microns, clavate, simple-septate at base; cystidia frequent, 45-60 x 4-6 microns, projecting up to 35 microns, narrowly cylindric, simple-septate at base; hyphae monomitic, hyphae of context of 2 types: 1) 4-9 microns wide, "pale reddish to brownish in KOH, mostly very thick-walled, with occasional branching, simple-septate", with a very narrow, sinuous lumen, 2) 2.5-4 microns wide, colorless in KOH, thin-walled to moderately thick-walled, with frequent branching, simple-septate
NAME ORIGIN  means "shining, glowing"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Pycnoporellus alboluteus generally grows flat on the wood surface, or may be slightly bent outwards to form cap, and microscopically has larger pores, spores and cystidia, (Gilbertson). P. alboluteus has fruitbodies that are typically spread out on the substrate, sometimes bent outward to form a slight cap, larger pores that are typically greater than 0.1cm in diameter, larger spores 9-14 microns long, and larger cystidia measuring 60-120 x 5-10 microns, (Ginns).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Ginns(28)*, Kroeger(5)*

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Pycnoporus cinnabarinus  (Jacq.: Fr.) P. Karst.^  Rev. Mycol. 3: 18. 1881;  Polyporus cinnabarinus  Jacq.: Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  cinnabar-red polypore, vermilion polypore
NOTES  Pycnoporus cinnabarinus is a red orange bracket-like or shelf-like fruiting body on hardwood, with a vermilion pore surface.^ RANGE It is found in BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NB, NF, NS, NT, ON, PE, PQ, AK, AL, AR, AZ, CA, CO, CT, DE, GA, IA, IL, IN, KS, KY, LA, MA, MD, ME, MI, MN, MO, MS, MT, NC, NH, NJ, NM, NY, OH, PA, SC, TN, UT, VA, VT, WI, WV, WY, and circumglobal, (Gilbertson) 
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  flesh becomes yellowish then colorless in KOH, (Gilbertson), flesh black with KOH (Phillips)
CAP  up to 7cm x 13cm x 4cm, bracket-like or bent outward from pore surface growing flat on wood to form shelf-like cap, dimidiate [roughly semicircular] to elongated, leathery when fresh; "ochraceous salmon to apricot orange", becoming pale when old or blackening, not zoned; bald^, (Gilbertson), 3-10cm across, semicircular to kidney-shaped; bright cinnabar red to orange; roughened, wrinkled, (Phillips), deep orange red, at first slightly pubescent [downy], fairly wrinkled, with faint zonation toward margin, (Lincoff(1)), orange to orange-red (Breitenbach)
FLESH  up to 1.5cm thick, soft, fibrous to corky, zoned; red to pale orange^, (Gilbertson), tough, leathery, (Phillips)

STEM  none; when wood broken open, orange-red mycelium is apparent, (Breitenbach)
ODOR  negligible^ (Lincoff(1))
TASTE  negligible^ (Lincoff(1))
EDIBILITY  no (Phillips)
HABITAT  annual, on hardwoods, also rarely on conifers^, associated with a white rot, (Gilbertson), on fallen timber, mainly oak, (Phillips), especially cherry and oak (Lincoff(2)), single, or several concrescent, (Breitenbach), fruiting in summer and fall (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Phillips)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6-8 x 2.5-3 microns, cylindric, slightly curved, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 18-25 x 5-7.5 microns, clavate, with basal clamp; cystidia absent, hyphal pegs frequent; hypha trimitic, generative hyphae of context 3-5 microns wide, "thin-walled, with clamps, rarely branched", skeletal hyphae of context 2.5-10 microns wide, "thick-walled, rarely branched, nonseptate", binding hyphae of context 1.5-5 microns wide, "much branched, thick-walled, nonseptate"; hyphae of trama similar, "hyphal contents in some areas of tramal tissue with strongly dextrinoid contents", (Gilbertson), spores 5-6 x 2-2.5 microns (Phillips, Lincoff(1), Lincoff(2)), spores 4-5.5 x 2-2.5 microns, elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, (Breitenbach)
NAME ORIGIN  from Greek meaning "cinnabar red or vermilion", (Schalkwijk-Barendsen), means pertaining to 'cinnabar', a red mineral mercury sulphide
SIMILAR

FAMILY  Meripilaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota

Dai, but the online Species Fungorum, accessed the same day, gives that name as a synonym of Rigidoporus crocatus (Pat.) Ryvarden. The description is derived from Gilbertson(1). PORES  5-7 per mm, circular to angular, thin-walled; "flesh-colored or very light pinkish or pinkish-brown, drying pinkish brown to smoky gray"; tube layer up to 0.3cm thick, hard, horny, distinct, darker, pinkish-tan

FAMILY  Polyporaceae, Order Polyporales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Sarcoporia polyspora  P. Karst.^  Hedwigia 33: 15. 1894;  Parmastomyces transmutans  (Overh.) Ryvarden & Gilb.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  {See also Amylocystis lapponica, Leptoporus mollis, Postia fragilis Table.} Features include 1) whitish fruitbodies that are flat on wood or shelf-like, soft when fresh, and that bruise or drying reddish brown, 2) growth on conifer wood and hardwood, and 3) microscopic characters including elliptic to short cylindric dextrinoid spores measuring 5-7 x 2.5-4.0 microns, the absence of cystidia and the presence of clamp connections.^ The description is derived from Gilbertson(1). RANGE Sarcoporia polyspora has been isolated from trunk rot in live Abies lasiocarpa (Subalpine Fir) in BC (Ginns(28)). Elsewhere in western North America, it is "widespread and common from NT to AZ" (Ginns(28)). It is found in the western mountains from NT to AZ, and also in eastern North American and Europe, (Gilbertson).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  resupinate, effused-reflexed or sessile [flat on wood with pores exposed, bent outwards to form a shelf-like cap, or entirely bracket-like]; white, bruising or drying reddish brown; soft and fleshy drying brittle; matted-strigose, azonate
FLESH  "context gelatinous next to the tubes, otherwise white, soft", in most specimens on conifers up to 0.5cm thick but in some specimens on hardwoods up to 1cm thick, "gelatinous layer darker and resinous on drying or on thicker basidiocarps darker resinous streaks scattered in context"; tube layers 0.1-0.3cm thick, "fragile, brittle and shattering easily when dry"
PORES  2-3(4) per millimeter, circular with thin dissepiments; white, "turning reddish brown on drying"
STEM  
ODOR  
TASTE  slightly acid
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  causes a brown cubical rot "of dead conifers and hardwoods and living black cherry"; common on Pinus ponderosa (Ponderosa Pine)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores (4)5-7 x 2.5-4 microns, elliptic to short-cylindric, smooth, strongly dextrinoid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 20-27 x 5-7 microns, clavate, with basal clamp connection; cystidia and other sterile elements absent from hymenium; hyphal system monomitic: context hyphae 3-6 microns wide, thin-walled to thick-walled, frequently branched, with clamp connections
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  Postia fragilis and Postia lateritia stain reddish but have narrower, allantoid spores. Leptoporus mollis stains reddish but has narrower spores and lacks clamp connections. Amylocystis lapponica stains reddish but has longer cylindric spores and has cystidia (amyloid metuloids). (Ginns(28)).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Parmastomyces transmutans), Ginns(28)*

FAMILY  Schizoporaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Schizopora flavipora  (Cooke) Ryvarden^  Mycotaxon 23: 186. 1985;  == Poria flavipora  Cooke;  = Polystictus subiculoides  Lloyd;  = Schizopora trichiliae  (Van der Byl.) Ryvarden;  = Tyromyces hypolateritia  (Berk.) Ryvarden;  = Schizopora carneolutea  (Rodway & Cleland) Kotl. & Pouzar
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Also listed in Crusts category. Features include a whitish to cream pore surface growing flat on hardwood, angular to maze-like small pores that split to form teeth, and microscopic characters including small spores, abundant small clamp connections, and terminal inflations on some hyphae.^ RANGE Schizopora flavipora has been found in OR, CA, FL, LA, and TX, (Gilbertson), Langer(1) examined material from BC, Argentina, Brazil, Venezuela, Austria, Czech Republic, Germany, Romania, Spain, Cameroon, Gabon, Ethiopia, Kenya, Malawi, South Africa, Tanzania, Uganda, Iran, China, Nepal, Sri Lanka, Thailand, and Australia, (Langer).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, usually developing from fusion of smaller fruitbodies and becoming widely spread out, "leathery when fresh, becoming corky or tough-fibrous when dried, not readily separable"; margin usually sterile, up to 0.2cm wide, whitish, fimbriate [fringed]^, (Gilbertson), resupinate, irregular, forming spreading patches, "tightly attached to substrate", margin usually distinct, paler and sterile, (Buczacki)
FLESH  less than 0.1cm thick, corky when dry, not zoned; cream to buff^, (Gilbertson), "white-cream, soft, fibrous", (Buczacki)
PORES  3-5 per mm, angular to daedaleoid [maze-like], with thin walls that split to form irpiciform teeth; whitish to cream, discoloring to buff on drying; tube layer up to 0.3cm, colored as flesh and continuous with it^, (Gilbertson), 3-5 per millimeter, angular, tubes 0.1-0.4cm deep, whitish, (Buczacki)
STEM  none
ODOR  
TASTE  mild ^ (Gilbertson)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  annual, on dead hardwoods, associated with white rot of dead hardwoods^, (Gilbertson), on dead wood or hardwood trees, also sometimes on shrubs, conifers and structural timbers in buildings; late summer to fall, (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 3.5-5 x 2.5-3.5 microns, elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 12-15 x 5-6 microns, clavate with slight median constriction, with basal clamp; cystidia absent, cystidioles scarcely projecting, 3-4 microns wide, fusoid; hyphae monomitic, generative hyphae of subiculum 2-6 microns wide, colorless in KOH, thin-walled to thick-walled, often branched, with abundant small clamp connections, some ending in spherical thin-walled swollen apex up to 12 microns wide, others with thick-walled nonseptate terminal segments that resemble skeletal hyphae, these with wall often thinning toward the apex; hyphae of trama similar, (Gilbertson), spores 4.5-5.5 x 3-3.5 microns, elliptic, smooth, inamyloid; cystidia obscure; hyphal system monomitic, (Buczacki)
NAME ORIGIN  means "with a yellow pore surface"^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Schizopora radula has an orange-tinted pore surface with pores 1-3 per mm and spores 4.0-5.5 x 3.0-4.0 microns (Ginns(28)). Schizopora paradoxa has pores 1-3 per mm, spores 5.5-6.5 x 3.5-4.5 microns, and skeletal hyphae, (Ginns(28)).
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1), Langer(1), Ginns(28)*, Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Schizoporaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Schizopora paradoxa  (Fr.) Donk^  Persoonia 5(1): 104. 1967;  == Hydnum paradoxum  Fr.;  = Polyporus versiporus  Pers.;  = Polyporus laciniatus  Velen.
ENGLISH NAMES  split porecrust
NOTES  Also listed in Crusts category. Features include 1) resupinate growth on wood, especially hardwood, 2) a fruitbody that is white to yellowish, with a maze-like toothed surface or sometimes poroid, 3) spores that are elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, and colorless, 4) cystidia when present originating in the hymenium, capitate, with a rounded exudate (but hyphal ends from the outer edges of the pores are obtuse not capitate) and 5) a hyphal system that is either dimitic (Hallenberg) or pseudodimitic (Langer), the hyphae with clamp connections.^ Schizopora radula was separated from Schizopora paradoxa in 1983, and previous descriptions of Schizopora paradoxa refer to both species; Langer(1) and Hallenberg(5) separate them slightly differently, see SIMILAR. With respect to Schizopora radula and Schizopora paradoxa, "Usually there are no problems in distinguishing the two species just by a look at the hymenophore (25 X) - at least for C. and N. European specimens. However, there are also specimens which are difficult to interpret, especially among perennial ones or when the fruitbody has grown on a vertical substrate. In these cases the microstructure is distinctive.", (Hallenberg) RANGE Schizopora paradoxa has been found in WA, AK, TN, Brazil, Columbia, Germany, Italy, Norway, Romania, Spain, Sweden, United Kingdom, Iran, Taiwan, and Australia, (Langer), BC, Denmark, Norway, and Sweden, (Hallenberg), LA (Gilbertson(4)), and CA (Desjardin(6)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed; margin usually distinct and coarsely granular^, (Buczacki)
FLESH  soft, when dry hard; white^, (Buczacki)
PORES  whitish - cream-colored; "irregularly hydnoid - denticulate - labyrinthiform, denticles thin - rather thick, often provided with small projections"^, (Hallenberg), white when young, cream to yellowish when old; irpicoid to poroid; pore walls up to 0.5cm deep, (Langer, who refers to the photograph in Breitenbach(2)), usually +/- poroid 2-5 per mm, angular, but also tending to become irregularly labyrinthine or almost toothed on vertical substrates; tubes 0.1-0.4cm deep, whitish, (Buczacki)
STEM  
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  collections examined from Corylus (Hallenberg), Alnus (alder), Araucaria, Betula (birch), Carpinus (hornbeam), Eucalyptus, Fagus (beech), Laurus, Pinus (pine), Quercus (oak), Syringa, Sorbus (mountain-ash), Taxus (yew), (Langer), on dead wood of hardwoods causing white soft rot, occasionally on conifers, shrubs and herbaceous plants and old polypore fruitbodies; all year, (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5-5.5 x 3.3-4 microns, when measured from spore prints, elliptic^; cystidia capitate, rare; hyphae dimitic: "skeletals very thick-walled with a narrow lumen, not branched", acyanophilic, generally abundant (the presence of skeletal hyphae most easily observed in the byssoid margins of the fruitbody or in the trama); generative hyphae with clamp connections, more or less thick-walled; incrustation of hyphae in the pore mouth variable, often absent, (Hallenberg), spores 5.5-6.5 x 3.5-4.5 microns, elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, thin-walled; basidia 4-spored, 17-21 x 4-5 microns, suburniform; cystidia capitate, about 30 microns long, 4-6 microns wide, (with a rounded, apical, colorless exudate), originating in the hymenium; hyphal ends on the edges of the pores are obtuse, not capitate, hyphae pseudodimitic, (Langer), spores 5-6.5 x 3-4 microns, broadly elliptic, smooth, inamyloid; cystidia-like structures clavate, spindle-shaped or broadly swollen, not projecting, (Buczacki)
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  Schizopora radula has 1) a poroid spore-bearing surface, with angular pores, sometimes lacerate, 2) spores measuring 4-5 x 2.8-3.8 microns, 3) capitate hyphal endings that are frequent in the hymenium and in the pore mouths, and 4) a monomitic hyphal system, whereas Schizopora paradoxa has 1) a hydnoid-denticulate-labyrinthiform spore-bearing surface, 2) spores measuring 5-5.5 x 3.3-4 microns, 3) capitate hyphal endings that are few in number, and 4) a dimitic hyphal system, the skeletals with a narrow lumen (generative hyphae in S. radula are thick-walled and could be mistaken for skeletal hyphae but the cell lumen is wide and the septa have clamp connections), (Hallenberg). S. radula has 1) an orange-tinged fruitbody, 2) spores measuring 4-5.5 x 3-4 microns, and 3) hyphal ends from the outer edges of the pores that are capitate, whereas Schizopora paradoxa has 1) a white to yellowish fruitbody, 2) spores measuring 5.5-6.5 x 3.5-4.5 microns, and 3) hyphal ends from the outer edges of the pores that are obtuse, (Langer). See also SIMILAR section of Antrodiella americana.
SOURCES  Hallenberg(5), Langer(1), Ginns(28)*, Eriksson(7) (S. paradoxa sensu lato), Breitenbach(2)* (S. paradoxa sensu lato), Gilbertson(4), Buczacki(1)*, Desjardin(6)*

FAMILY  Schizoporaceae, Order Hymenochaetales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Schizopora radula  (Pers.: Fr.) Hallenb.^  Mycotaxon 18(2): 308. 1983;  Poria radula  Pers.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Also listed in Crusts category. Features include 1) resupinate growth on wood, especially hardwood, 2) a fruitbody that is colored "with an orange tint, cream to ochreous or pale reddish brown" (Ginns), with a poroid or sometimes maze-like toothed surface, 3) spores that are elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, and colorless, 4) capitate cystidia in the hymenium, and capitate hyphal ends in the subiculum and outer edges of the pores, and 5) a hyphal system that is either monomitic (with generative hyphae often thick-walled) (Hallenberg) or pseudodimitic (Langer), the hyphae with clamp connections.^ Schizopora radula was separated from Schizopora paradoxa in 1983, and previous descriptions of Schizopora paradoxa refer to both species. Langer(1) and Hallenberg(5) separate them slightly differently, see SIMILAR. With respect to Schizopora radula and Schizopora paradoxa, "Usually there are no problems in distinguishing the two species just by a look at the hymenophore (25 X) - at least for C. and N. European specimens. However, there are also specimens which are difficult to interpret, especially among perennial ones or when the fruitbody has grown on a vertical substrate. In these cases the microstructure is distinctive.", (Hallenberg). RANGE Schizopora radula has been found in BC, SC, Argentina, Denmark, France, Norway, Spain, Turkey, and Taiwan, (Langer). It has been found in BC, Austria, Denmark, and Sweden, (Hallenberg).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  growing flat on wood with pore surface exposed, tightly attached^, (Buczacki)
FLESH  soft, fibrous; cream^, (Buczacki)
PORES  cream-colored - ochraceous - pale reddish brown; poroid, pores of variable size, 1-3 per millimeter, "angular, in some specimens strongly lacerate", "pore walls thin, in old specimens sometimes rather thick"^, (Hallenberg), irpicoid to poroid, beige with light orange tinge, 1-4 pores per millimeter, pore walls up to 0.01cm thick and pores about 0.2cm deep; subiculum apart from pores up to about 0.01cm thick, (Langer), "pores varying in shape: typically angular and 1-3 per millimetre to strongly lacerated (appearing tooth-like), edges thin, becoming thick", pore surface "with an orange tint, cream to ochreous or pale reddish brown", (Ginns), 2-4 per mm, angular but becoming elongated or +/- labyrinthine, (Buczacki)
STEM  
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  collections examined from Abies (fir), Alnus (alder), Castanea (chestnut), Fraxinus (ash), Quercus (oak), (Hallenberg), on Eucalyptus, Populus, Sorbus (mountain-ash), Quercus, (Langer), all year (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4-5 x 2.8-3.8 microns, elliptic^; cystidia capitate, frequent; hyphae monomitic, hyphae more or less thick-walled, regularly with clamp connections; "some hyphal endings in the trama are - in limited parts - very thick-walled, with a narrow, but unevenly wide lumen"; hyphal endings in pore mouth generally thin-walled, much incrusted, (Hallenberg), spores 4-5.5 x 3-4 microns; basidia 4-spored, 13-18 x 3.5-5 microns; cystidia capitate in the hymenium and also capitate hyphal ends in the subiculum and in the outer edges of the pores, the hyphal ends in the outer edges of the pores incrusted with small crystal masses; hyphae pseudodimitic: skeletoid hyphae 3.5-4.5 microns wide, walls up to 1.5 microns thick, generative hyphae 2-4 microns wide, walls up to 1 micron thick; primary septa of all hyphae with clamp connections, (Langer), spores 3-4.5 x 2-3 microns, elliptic to nearly round, smooth, inamyloid; cystidia sparse, clavate, projecting, heavily encrusted; hyphal system dimitic, (Buczacki)
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  Schizopora paradoxa has 1) a hydnoid-denticulate-labyrinthiform spore-bearing surface, 2) spores measuring 5-5.5 x 3.3-4 microns, 3) capitate hyphal endings that are few in number, and 4) a dimitic hyphal system, the skeletals with a narrow lumen, whereas Schizopora radula has 1) a poroid spore-bearing surface, with angular pores, sometimes lacerate, 2) spore measuring 4-5 x 2.8-3.8 microns, 3) capitate hyphal endings that are frequent in the hymenium and in the pore mouths, and 4) a monomitic hyphal system (generative hyphae in S. radula are thick-walled and could be mistaken for skeletal hyphae but the cell lumen is wide and the septa have clamp connections), (Hallenberg), Schizopora paradoxa has 1) a white to yellowish fruitbody, 2) spores measuring 5.5-6.5 x 3.5-4.5 microns, and 3) hyphal ends from the outer edges of the pores that are obtuse, whereas Schizopora radula has 1) an orange-tinged fruitbody, 2) spores measuring 4-5.5 x 3-4 microns, and 3) hyphal ends from the outer edges of the pores that are capitate, (Langer).
SOURCES  Hallenberg(5), Langer(1), Ginns(28)*, Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Albatrellaceae, Order Russulales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Scutiger ellisii  (Berk.) Murrill^  Bull. Torrey bot. Club 30(8): 427. 1903;  Albatrellus ellisii  (Berk.) Pouzar  Folia Geobot. Phytotaxon 1: 357. 1966;  Polyporus ellisii  Berk.
ENGLISH NAMES  scaly yellow polypore
NOTES  Features include large yellow-green to yellow brown hairy to scaly cap, large white pores that tend to stain greenish, off-center to lateral stem, and growth on ground under conifers^. Audet(1) supported the use of the the 1903 name Scutiger ellisii, although the online Species Fungorum and MycoBank, accessed February 3, 2019, gave the current name as Albatrellus ellisii. On the other hand, the online Species Fungorum had changed current names to reflect the new genus names recommended by Audet(1), and reflected the rcommendation of Audet(1) that the name Scutiger pes-caprae be used for Albatrellus pes-caprae as recommended by Audet(1). RANGE Scutiger ellisii is found in ID, AL, AZ, CA, KY, NC, NJ, (Gilbertson), WA, OR, CO, WY, (Ginns(1)); collections from BC are deposited at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  8-25cm or more, convex becoming flat, wavy or depressed, margin inrolled at first, often wavy; greenish to sulphur yellow or yellow-brown (often a mixture), sometimes with darker brown shades or stains when old; dry, "at first hairy or plushlike but the hairs often matted in age or grouping to form coarse scales"^, (Arora), a mixture of green, yellow, and lavender; dry, scaly, (Ginns(1)), "a mixture of green, yellow, lavender to dark brown" (Ginns(28)), up to 17cm, circular to lobed or fan-shaped; yellowish green; coarsely scaly, scales finely tomentose, (Gilbertson)
FLESH  thick, firm; "white, sometimes bruising greenish slowly"^ (Arora), "white when young, staining bluish lavender when cut, these stains in patches or some specimens staining green", (Ginns(1)), up to 1cm thick, fleshy when fresh, drying hard and rigid; cream to pale tan, not zoned, (Gilbertson)
PORES  0.5-2 per mm, often decurrent; "white, usually staining greenish or yellow-green when bruised or becoming yellowish or dingy greenish in old age"; tube layer 0.2-0.6cm thick^, (Arora), 1-2 per mm, circular to angular, walls thick becoming torn; cream, turning greenish when bruised, drying pallid or greenish; tube layer up to 0.3cm thick, cream to slightly greenish, (Gilbertson)
STEM  3-12cm or more long, 2-6cm wide, usually off-center or lateral, equal or wider at either end, solid, tough; usually colored like cap^, (Arora), up to 8cm long and 5cm wide, central to lateral; greenish yellow; tomentose, (Gilbertson)
ODOR  
TASTE  mild^ (Ginns(1))
EDIBILITY  yes (Arora)
HABITAT  annual; single, scattered, gregarious, or in fused clusters, on ground in woods, especially conifer forest^, (Arora), on soil in coniferous forest, (Gilbertson)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-11(14) x 5-8 microns, elliptic to tear-drop-shaped (with prominent apiculus), smooth, inamyloid, colorless^; basidia 4-spored, 40-48 x 8-10 microns, clavate, with basal clamp connection; cystidia or other hymenial elements absent; hyphae monomitic, generative hyphae with clamp connections, hyphae of context mostly 4-10 microns wide but some inflated to 20 microns, thin-walled to moderately thick-walled, with frequent branching and interwoven, weakly amyloid in scattered regions, gloeoplerous hyphae also present in context, these being sinuous and highly refractive in Melzer's reagent, hyphae of surface squamules more strongly amyloid, much distorted and fragmenting readily, irregularly inflated up to 30 microns, hyphae of trama mostly 2-4 microns wide, more uniform, more compactly interwoven, (Gilbertson), spores (8.0)8.6-9.6(10.0) x (5.6)6.0-6.8(7.2) microns, tear-drop shape to broadly elliptic, with prominent apiculus, smooth, inamyloid, colorless; paraphyses seen in one specimen, rare, 3.0-3.4 microns wide, filiform; cystidioles seen in two collections, infrequent, 10 microns wide, projecting to 30 microns, cylindric to fusoid, with thin colorless walls, (Ginns(1)), spores 8-9 x 5-7 microns, elliptic, smooth, (Arora)
NAME ORIGIN  named for J.B. Ellis^ (Gilbertson)
SIMILAR  Scutiger pes-caprae is very similar in all characters except color is brown, (Gilbertson). S. pes-caprae has a brown cap and does not stain green (Ginns(28)). See also SIMILAR section of Xeroceps skamania.
SOURCES  Gilbertson(1) (as Albatrellus), Arora(1)* (as Albatrellus), Ginns(1) (as Albatrellus), Castellano(2)* (as Albatrellus), Phillips(1)* (as Albatrellus), Lincoff(2)* (as Albatrellus), Audet(1), Desjardin(6)* (as Albatrellus), Ginns(28)* (as Albatrellus)

FAMILY  Albatrellaceae, Order Russulales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Scutiger pes-caprae  (Pers.) Bondartsev & Singer^  Annls mycol. 39(1): 47. 1941;  Albatrellus pes-caprae  (Pers.) Pouzar  Folia Geobot. Phytotaxon 1: 357. 1966;  Polyporus pes-caprae  Pers.;  Scutiger oregonensis  Murrill
ENGLISH NAMES  goat's foot
NOTES  Features include brown cap with small dense scales, large yellowish cream pores, shallow tube layer, off-center to lateral stem, mild taste, and tear-drop-shaped spores.^ Audet(1) supported the use of the name Scutiger pes-caprae, and the online Species Fungorum, accessed September 25, 2018, gave that as the current name. MycoBank, accessed the same day gave the current name as Albatrellus pes-caprae. RANGE Gilbertson(1) list for OR, AL, CA, NC, NJ, and TN. Ginns(28) adds WA to the distribution. Breitenbach(2) list also for Europe and Asia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  (2.5)5-20cm, convex to flat or centrally depressed, margin often deeply indented; dark brown to brown, reddish brown, or pinkish brown; dry, "covered with fine fibrils that form small, dense scales or sometimes a plush"^ (Arora), up to 20cm, circular to kidney-shaped or irregular; brown; with small scales 0.1-0.2cm wide, (Gilbertson), 5-12cm across, convex to umbonate, irregularly rounded, fleshy; red-brown to black-brown; finely squamose [scaly]; margin lobed, wavy, incised, sharp, (Breitenbach), gray, pinkish brown, brown, or blackish brown; dry, somewhat scaly, (Lincoff(2)), scales at first appressed then raised, often delimited by cracks, (Lincoff(1))
FLESH  thick, fairly tough; white, bruising pinkish slowly^, (Arora), up to 2cm thick, firm-fibrous when dry; cream, not zoned, (Gilbertson), fragile, soft; whitish, (Breitenbach), white tending to lemon-colored, reddish at base, (Lincoff(1))
PORES  1-2mm or more, angular, often decurrent; "white to yellowish or with greenish stains, sometimes becoming pinkish in age"; tube layer 0.2-0.5cm thick^, (Arora), mostly 1-2 per mm, but some up to 2mm in diameter, angular, hexagonal, with thin torn walls; yellowish cream to pinkish when fresh, pale brownish when dry; tube layer up to 0.3cm thick, pale brownish, (Gilbertson), 6-10 per mm, somewhat decurrent, irregularly angular-rounded; cream to yellowish, often spotting yellowish when handled; tube layer up to 0.5cm thick, (Breitenbach)
STEM  2.5-8cm x 1-3(4)cm, "occasionally central but usually off-center to lateral", solid, usually wider in lower part, sometimes several from a common base; white to yellowish or when old brownish,^ (Arora), up to 8cm long and 6cm wide, simple or branched, off-center to lateral, cylindric or enlarged at base; pale buff or with spots staining darker brown to reddish brown; bald to faintly squamulose [fine-scaly], appearing reticulate [netted] on underside from decurrent tubes, (Gilbertson), 3-6cm x 1.5-3cm, usually off-center, more rarely lateral, solid, cylindric to clavate-bulbous; yellowish to orange-brownish; finely squamose, (Breitenbach)
ODOR  faint, pleasant (Lincoff(1)), pleasant (Breitenbach)
TASTE  mild (Arora), mild, nut-like (Breitenbach), of hazelnuts (Lincoff(1))
EDIBILITY  yes, according to European sources, (Arora)
HABITAT  annual; single or in groups or clumps on ground in woods^ (Arora), single or several on a branching stem, on ground in coniferous forest, (Gilbertson)
SPORE DEPOSIT  whitish (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-11 x 5-6 microns, elliptic, smooth^ (Arora), spores 8-11 x 6-8 microns, broadly elliptic with conspicuous apiculus giving a tear-drop appearance, smooth, inamyloid, colorless; basidia 4-spored, 30-55 x 8-12 microns, clavate, with basal clamp connection; cystidia absent; hyphae monomitic, hyphae of context 4-14 microns wide, colorless in KOH, thin-walled and usually collapsed, apparently often branched, with clamp connections and simple septa, hyphae of trama similar, hyphae of cap surface scales 5-16 microns wide, "often with thickened walls, with cylindric to clavate apices", with conspicuous clamp connections, inamyloid, gloeoplerous hyphae present, (Gilbertson), spores 8.5-12 x 7-8 microns, broadly elliptic, smooth, inamyloid, colorless, with droplets, (Breitenbach), spores 8-11 x 5-6 microns, oval to broadly elliptic, (Lincoff(2)), spores 8-11 x 5.5-7.5 microns (Lincoff(1))
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin, "like a goat's foot"^, (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  Scutiger ellisii very similar in all characters except color is yellowish green, (Gilbertson). The cap color of S. ellisii is a mixture of green, yellow, lavender, and dark brown, whereas S. pes-caprae has a brown cap that does not stain green, (Ginns(28)). Jahnoporus hirtus is somewhat similar but has a bitter taste (Arora). Bondarzewia species have a less scaly cap, are not normally yellowish or greenish stained, and have spiny spores, (Arora). See also SIMILAR section of Xeroceps skamania.
